Review of the Defence of Insanity in s 16 of the Criminal Code and Fitness to Plead [2019] TASLRI 28
Assessment reports
Review of the Defence of Insanity in s 16 of the Criminal
Code and Fitness to Plead
Final Report no. 28
December 2019
Information
The Tasmania Law Reform Institute was established on 23 July 2001 by
agreement between the Government of the State of Tasmania, the
University of
Tasmania and the Law Society of Tasmania. The creation of the Institute was part
of a Partnership Agreement between
the University and the State government
signed in 2000. The Institute is based at the Sandy Bay campus of the University
of Tasmania
within the Faculty of Law. The Institute undertakes law reform work
and research on topics proposed by the government, the community,
the University
and the Institute itself.
The Institute’s Director is Associate Professor Terese Henning. The
members of the Board of the Institute are Associate Professor
Terese Henning
(Chair), Professor Tim McCormack (Dean of the Faculty of Law at the University
of Tasmania), the Honourable Justice
Helen Wood (appointed by the Honourable
Chief Justice of Tasmania), Ms Kristy Bourne (appointed by the
Attorney-General), Associate
Professor Jeremy Prichard (appointed by the Council
of the University), Mr Craig Mackie (appointed by the Tasmanian Bar
Association),
Ms Ann Hughes (appointed at the invitation of the Institute
Board), Mr Rohan Foon (appointed by the Law Society of Tasmania), Ms
Baumeler (appointed at the invitation of the Institute Board) and Ms Rosie Smith
(appointed at the invitation of the Institute
Board as a member of the Tasmanian
Aboriginal community).
The Board oversees the Institute’s research, considering each reference
before it is accepted, and approving publications before
their release.

Terms of Reference
To consider the operation of the law of insanity in Tasmania with particular
reference to:
• the operation of s 16(3) of the
Criminal Code
• whether evidence of insane delusions arising from mental illness
should form the basis of self-defence;
• if insane delusions arising from mental illness form the basis of
self-defence, whether defendants relying on insane delusions
should be liable to
supervision under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
• if insane delusions arising from mental illness form the basis of
self-defence, whether the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) requires amendment in relation to treatment options for such
defendants.
To consider the operation of the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas) and whether changes are needed to ensure that the Act operates
justly, effectively and consistently with the principles that
underlie it. In
particular, the Institute should consider whether:
• the process of determining fitness to stand trial or establishing the
defence of insanity can be improved; and
• the operation of
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) including in relation to discharge and review of
forensic and treatment orders and whether there is a need for
‘step-down’
Executive Summary
This Report considers two specialised responses for individuals with mental
illness and/or cognitive impairment where their mental
impairment affects their
ability to participate in the ordinary processes of the criminal justice system.
(1) fitness to stand trial; and
(2) the defence of insanity.
Although there may be an overlap in the conditions that give rise to a
finding that a person is unfit to stand trial and a finding
that a person is not
guilty by reason of insanity, the scope of their operation and their legal
requirements are different.
Fitness to stand trial
provision that relates
to the issue of whether the accused has the ability to understand or participate
in his or her trial. It is
concerned with a person’s capacity at the
time of the trial
. It applies where a person’s mental processes are
disordered or impaired, or if for any other reason the person is unable to
understand court processes and make a defence to the charge.
is a matter of
substantive
involving the
determination of a whether a person should not be held criminally responsible on
the ground that he or she lacked the
mental capacity to commit the offence.
Insanity is concerned with an individual’s capacity at the
time of the
. In order to rely on the insanity defence, the person must have a
mental disease (which includes what has in past legislation been
‘natural imbecility’) such that he or she lacked the capacity to
understand the physical character of the act
or to know that the act was one
which he or she ought not do, or, by reason of the mental disease, the person
was deprived of the
power to resist an impulse.
A significant proportion of people within the criminal justice system have
mental and/or cognitive impairments, yet only a small number
of such individuals
rely on unfitness to stand trial or the defence of insanity. More commonly,
people with mental or cognitive impairments
do not rely on the unfitness
procedure or the insanity defence and instead enter a plea of guilty or are
found guilty following a
trial. A focus of this reference has been to consider
barriers that may discourage people from relying on these specialised provisions
when they might otherwise be appropriate. The Issues Paper asked a range of
questions about the operation of the rules relating to
fitness to stand trial
and the defence of insanity in Tasmania. After conducting its review of the
current law, considering the feedback
received to questions asked in the Issues
Paper and examining the research and the developments in other jurisdictions,
the Institute’s
view is that reform is required to the law of fitness to
stand trial, the law of insanity and the structure of the orders that are
available to the court following a finding of not guilty by reason of insanity
(either at trial or a special hearing) or if a finding
cannot be made that the
defendant is not guilty of an offence (at a special hearing).
of this Report details the background to this reference and presents
an overview of the structure of the Report and the scope of
the reference.
of this Report provides an overview of the legislative framework that
sets out the law in relation to the operation of the criminal
justice system for
people with mental health impairments and/or cognitive impairments. Several Acts
set out the law in relation to
the operation of the criminal justice system for
people with mental health impairments and/or cognitive impairments, including
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
Mental Health Act 2013
(Tas) and the
This Part also sets out the distinction between fitness to stand trial and
insanity and considers the principles that underlie the
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas). These principles are fairness to the
accused and the right to a fair trial, the protection of the community and the
recognition
of the rights of mentally impaired individuals consistent with the
principles of least restriction. These principles are considered
in light of the
obligations that arise under international legal instruments to which Australia
is a signatory including the
International Covenant on Civil and Political
’), and the
Convention on the Rights of
Persons with Disabilities
provides an overview of mental health impairments and cognitive
impairments and the criminal justice system. As noted, insanity and
stand trial apply to only a small number of people compared to the number of
individuals with mental health impairments
and cognitive impairments overall who
are involved in the criminal justice system. Individuals with mental health or
cognitive impairments
may not rely on the process of fitness to stand trial or
on the insanity defence but may proceed through the usual criminal justice
process. This is a clear alternative and the possible sentence received (if
found guilty) compared to the consequences of a finding
of unfitness or insanity
are likely to weigh into the decision-making process.
A question considered in the Issues Paper is the relationship between the
‘dangerous criminal’ provisions contained in
Sentencing Act
, Division 3 and the sentencing orders available to the
court under the
Sentencing Act 1997
where an offender found
guilty of an offence may be sentenced to a restriction order, a supervision
order or a treatment order in
cases where the offender appears to be suffering
from a mental illness. The Institute’s view is that a more appropriate
for such an offender – one that will protect the community and
allow the person to obtain appropriate treatment – is
found in the
forensic orders contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
rather than in indefinite detention as a dangerous
criminal under the
Sentencing Act 1997
(Tas). Based on feedback about
whether a statutory trigger is necessary to facilitate consideration of the
forensic orders, the Institute’s
recommendation is that an amendment to
this effect is unnecessary given the factors relevant to the court’s
sentencing decision
and the matters that courts take into account in any event
in deciding whether to make dangerous criminal declarations.
sets out the current test for determining unfitness to stand trial in
Tasmania and considers the law in other jurisdictions. Unfitness
to stand trial
relates to the issue whether an accused is able to participate in the trial
process, and, as the Institute has previously
written, this ability
‘fundamentally determine[s] whether that person can gain access to justice
and whether justice can in
The current focus of the fitness test is on a person’s cognitive capacity
or intellectual ability to understand features of
the criminal trial. Based on
submissions received and its research, the Institute is of the view that this
test is deficient in that
it does not give adequate recognition to whether the
accused can participate meaningfully in his or her trial. The answer to this
question depends on two inter-linked matters: first, whether the accused has the
capacity to make decisions in relation to key elements
of the trial and, second,
whether the necessary conditions exist or can be created to enable him or her to
exercise that decision-making
The Institute recommends a shift from the current focus on cognitive capacity
to a supported decision-making approach based on facilitating
individual’s ability to exercise his/her decision-making capacity and
effectively participate in the trial. The determination
of an accused’s
decision-making capacity needs to be clearly grounded in relevant determinative
criteria. Consequently, what
these criteria are should also be determined and
specified in reforming the fitness test. The purpose of such criteria is to
courts when applying what might otherwise appear to be a relatively vague
and subjective test. The Institute makes recommendations
in this regard. In
summary, the approach recommended by the Institute has three interrelated
components:
• the reformulation of the fitness test to replace the current
‘Presser’ test with a decision-making test;
• the specification of guidance/criteria in the application of that
test to give it content and avoid subjectivity in its application;
• a requirement for assessment of the need for and, where necessary,
creation of the conditions that optimise and/or enable
people to exercise their
decision-making capacity and therefore participate in trials. These might
include making necessary accommodations
in court processes or providing
decision-making and communication supports through the use of communication
assistants or intermediaries.
These recommendations are made in light of the submissions received, the
Institute’s broader research and the requirement set
out in the terms of
reference that the Institute should consider whether changes are needed to
ensure that the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
operates justly, effectively and consistently with the principles that underlie
sets out the current Tasmanian procedure to determine unfitness to
stand trial, the position in other jurisdictions and makes recommendations
reform the current procedure. Concerns that arise in this area include: the role
of experts and experts’ reports in the
process of determining unfitness to
stand trial, the appropriate decision-maker (judge or jury) in making fitness
determinations
in the Supreme Court, and the appropriateness of the current
procedure for determining fitness in the Magistrates Court. After considering
these issues, this Part makes recommendations about developing best practice
guidelines for the preparation of expert reports to
be used by the court to make
a determination of fitness to stand trial or to enter a guilty plea. The
recommendations highlight the
need for experts to provide information to the
court in relation to supports and accommodations that may help the
decision-making capacity and assist him/her to participate in
the criminal justice process. Other significant recommendations are
determinations of fitness to stand trial in the Supreme Court should be
determined by a judge (rather than a jury) and for the
introduction of a
simplified alternative pathway for appropriate cases in the Magistrates
sets out the procedure following a determination of unfitness to stand
trial in Tasmania and in other comparable jurisdictions. This
concerns the
conduct of the ‘special hearing’. If the court determines that the
defendant is not likely to become fit
to stand trial within 12 months or the
defendant does not become fit to stand trial within 12 months, then the court
holds a special
hearing. The special hearing provides a way to test the
prosecution case and is conducted so that the onus of proof and standard
proof are the same as in a trial of criminal proceedings and in other respects
as nearly as possible as if it were a trial of
criminal proceedings. Key issues
that arise in relation to the special hearing are whether the special hearing
process complies with
the human rights obligations under the
whether there should be provision for a judge-alone process for special
hearings. Based on its consideration of the provisions
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
, the Institute’s view
is that the provisions do currently allow for the special hearing to be
conducted as far as possible
like an ordinary trial and, crucially, allow for an
individual to be acquitted if the prosecution cannot establish its case beyond
reasonable doubt in a criminal trial.
However, an area of concern was identified in relation to the role of the
legal practitioner in circumstances where the accused is
unable to give
instructions. This creates practical and ethical difficulties for legal
practitioners. Accordingly, aligned with the
Institute’s recommended
approach of facilitating supported decision-making (as set out in
Institute makes recommendations to use intermediaries and communication
assistants in special hearings to assist with the provision
of instructions. In
these cases, the defendant’s rights, wishes and preferences should be
given effect where he or she is capable
of giving instructions. There will be
cases where this is not possible, and in these cases legal practitioners must
act to promote
and uphold defendants’ personal and social wellbeing and in
a way that is least restrictive of their human rights. This reflects
requirements of the
and the National Decision-Making Principles.
Recommendations are also made in
to allow for a special hearing to be
heard by a judge alone where the prosecution and the defence agree in relation
to the defence
of insanity (or, as recommended in this Report, the defence of
mental or cognitive impairment). This should operate with judicial
oversight to
provide appropriate safeguards, so that the court has access to the expert
reports and the power to hear from expert
witnesses. This is consistent with the
Institute’s recommendation in relation to a ‘consent’ defence
or cognitive impairment in
provides an overview of the law of insanity in Tasmania and identifies
concerns in relation to the existing law before considering
options for reform
and outlining recommendations for reform. Extensive scholarly literature has
identified deficiencies with the
law of insanity. An overview of these concerns
is set out in
, including that it contains inappropriate and outdated
terminology (including ‘insanity’ and ‘natural
imbecility’)
and that the nature of the qualifying mental state (mental
disease) is outdated, limited and offensive. Another area of concern in
to the law of insanity in Tasmania is the role of s 16(3) of the
(Tas) and the interrelationship of the defences of insanity and
self-defence. This matter has previously been addressed by the TLRI
again specifically raised in the terms of reference for this project. Based on
its consideration of the literature, the submissions
received and the approaches
taken in other jurisdictions, the key reforms recommended are to retain and
reform the defence of insanity
Criminal Code
(Tas) s16 by:
• Changing the name of the defence of insanity to the defence of mental
or cognitive impairment.
• Changing the scope of the qualifying condition by replacing the term
‘mental disease’ with detailed definitions
of mental impairment and
cognitive impairment.
• Changing the terminology in s 16(1)(a)(i) from ‘understanding
the physical character’ to ‘understanding
the nature and
quality’ of such act or omission.
• Repealing s16(3).
• Making legislative provision for the prosecution to raise the defence
of mental or cognitive impairment against the wishes
of the defendant with the
leave of the court.
• Providing the court with the power to dispense with a trial if the
prosecution and defence agree that the evidence in a case
raises the defence of
mental or cognitive impairment. This should operate with judicial oversight to
provide appropriate safeguards,
so that the court has access to the expert
reports and the power to hear from expert witnesses.
• Ensuring that the same law applies in the Supreme Court and the
Magistrates Court.
Part 8 examines the orders that can be made under the
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) following a finding that an individual is
not guilty by reason of insanity (either at a trial or a special hearing) or if
finding cannot be made that the defendant is not guilty of an offence (at a
special hearing). An individual is
found guilty of an offence where
he or she is found not guilty by reason of insanity or unfit to stand trial if a
finding cannot
be made at a special hearing that the accused is not guilty.
Accordingly, the person is not ‘sentenced’ for the offence
to the ordinary principles of sentencing. The Terms of Reference require the
Institute to consider the just and effective
operation of the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas), in particular the operation of
of the Act, including in relation to the discharge and review of forensic
and treatment orders and whether there is a need for ‘step-down’
options. In light of these considerations, this Part sets out recommendations
for reform that would abolish the current indefinite
detention scheme for
forensic orders and replace it with a new model with the following features:
(1) A limiting term set by the court following a finding of not guilty by
reason of mental or cognitive impairment or where a finding
cannot be made that
the defendant is not guilty.
(2) The limiting term to be set on the basis of the hypothetical sentence
model. This means that the court must nominate a limiting
term, which would be
the court’s best estimate of the length of the sentence that would have
been imposed had the person been
found guilty at a normal trial.
(3) A limiting term to not operate as an absolute limit of the duration of an
order. The DPP should be able to make an application
to the Supreme Court prior
to the expiration of the limiting term for an extension of a restriction order
or a supervision order.
The DPP should also be able to apply for a restriction
order to be replaced with a supervision order instead of an extension of the
restriction order.
(4) A forensic patient to be able to apply to the Supreme Court for a
discharge of a restriction order or the variation or revocation
of a supervision
order prior to the expiry of the limiting term. This reflects the current
process for the review of orders by the
Supreme Court under the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(5) The test for making, varying or removing orders under the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
to be changed from
‘likely to endanger’ to require determination of whether the
defendant does, or would if released,
pose an unacceptable risk of causing
physical or psychological harm to a person or persons generally.
(6) The test for making, varying or removing orders under the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
to be amended to require
the Mental Health Tribunal and the court to have regard to the person’s
recovery or progress in terms
of treatment progression and personal improvement
in making a decision to discharge a restriction order or vary or revoke a
supervision
Recommendations are also made that consideration be given to empowering
courts to make suppression orders in relation to applications
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas). This would be for
the purposes of assisting the long-term recovery of people subject to the Act
and facilitating their reintegration
into the community.
The Institute has also identified barriers to patient reintegration and
release from forensic orders within the Forensic Mental Health
recommends a review be conducted to ensure that appropriate pathways are
available for the gradual reintegration of forensic
patients into the community,
consistent with the principles of least restriction and community safety. In
addition, appropriate rehabilitation
pathways should be developed for people
with cognitive impairments.
Recommendations
1. It is unnecessary to amend to the dangerous criminal provisions contained
Sentencing Act 1997
(Tas) to provide a statutory trigger for
judicial consideration of the appropriateness of making an order under the
Sentencing Act 1997
instead of a dangerous criminal
declaration. (p 25)
2. There is a need to reform the current test for unfitness to stand trial
contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
3. The doctrine of fitness to stand trial should not be abolished in
Tasmania. (p 39)
4. The right to a fair trial should be given legislative recognition as the
fundamental principle governing the determination of whether
a defendant is fit
to stand trial within the meaning of the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
5. The TLRI does not support the minimalist reform approach of simply
up-dating the criteria for the current cognitive capacity fitness
test) without making additional reforms to that test. More
profound reform is needed. (p 41)
6. The test for assessing fitness to stand trial in
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
should be reformed so that its focus is
on the accused’s decision-making capacity as means of determining whether
can effectively participate in the trial rather than on his or her
cognitive capacity. This recommendation is to be understood in
conjunction with
two other factors:
(1) an identification of the key matters about which a person must be able to
make decisions in the trial in order for them to be
able to meaningfully
participate (see Recommendation 8); and
(2) the need for the court to take into account any accommodations and
supports that would assist a person to be able to exercise
their decision-making
capacity in the trial process (see Recommendations 9 and 10). (p 44)
7. Rationality or rational decision-making should not be included in the test
for determining whether a person should be required
to stand trial contained in
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
8. Criteria should be legislatively prescribed to guide assessments of
defendants’ decision-making capacity. These criteria
should consist of
critical understandings and abilities that defendants must have in order to make
key decisions during trials including:
• an understanding of the actual significance and effect of entering a
• the ability to communicate
instructions to his or
her legal practitioner
• the ability to decide whether to give evidence to support his/her
case and his/her ability to give evidence (that is, the
ability to give a
coherent account in his/her defence and to answer questions put to him/her)
• an understanding of the meaning and consequences of a finding of
guilt including that he/she may be punished and in jeopardy
of imprisonment, if
so advised that they are at risk of such a penalty
• an understanding of the nature of the offence(s) with which he/she is
• the ability to understand the nature of the proceedings as a process
to determine whether it has been proved that he/she has
committed the offence(s)
• the ability to understand and follow the substance of the evidence
that may be given at trial
• the ability to understand the course of the proceedings and what is
going on in a general sense at each stage of the trial
process. (p 52)
9. There should be legislative recognition in the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
of the need for courts to take into account
support measures and accommodations that are available to an individual with a
optimising an accused’s fitness to stand trial in circumstances
where they may otherwise be unfit. Further, the legislation
should specify that
a finding of lack of capacity is only to be made as a last resort, that is, in
the event that support measures
have been exhausted. (p 58)
10. The court’s assessment of the supports and accommodations that may
assist an accused to be fit for trial must be based on
supports/accommodations that are actually available and would be effective to
assist the accused. This should be the subject
of expert reports in relation to
the court’s determination of the fitness to stand trial test. (p 58)
11. There should be a separate test in the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) to determine whether a person is fit to enter a
plea of guilty. (p 62)
12. The test for fitness to enter a plea of guilty should reflect the
decision-making and communication approach contained in the
proposed test for
determining fitness to stand trial (see Recommendation 6) with necessary
alterations to reflect the difference
between entering a plea of guilty and
participating in a defended trial. (p 62)
13. The following trial processes are relevant to a person’s
decision-making capacity to be fit to enter a guilty plea (see
Recommendation
• the nature of the offence with which he or she is charged.
• the right to contest that charge.
• the nature of the hearing if he or she entered a plea of not guilty
as a process to determine whether it has been proved that
the person committed
the offence charged.
• the consequences of entering a plea of guilty, including what is
being admitted when a plea of guilty is entered, the effect
of a plea of guilty,
that the court may impose punishment and a general understanding of the
punishment that the person is in jeopardy
of receiving.
• the accused’s understanding of the course of the sentencing
hearing and a general understanding of what is occurring
at each stage and the
right to challenge the facts stated by the prosecution. (p 62)
14. Best practice guidelines should be developed for the preparation of
expert reports to be used by the court to make a determination
of fitness to
stand trial or enter a guilty plea. These should be developed by an expert
working group, which should consider how
to address challenges arising in the
assessment of fitness to stand trial by experts posed by cultural and ethnic
factors, as well
as other communication difficulties. This may include taking
into account the efficacy of psychometric instruments, where relevant,
developed through studies of Western, English-speaking populations. (p 73)
15. The scope of the best practice guidelines should accommodate the proposed
changes to the fitness test and should provide information
to the court in
relation to the supports and accommodations that may support the person’s
decision-making capacity and assist
an individual to participate in the criminal
justice process. (p 73)
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
(Tas) should be amended to provide that the investigation into
fitness to stand trial is to be determined by a judge (rather than
the jury). (p
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) should be
amended to allow a magistrate to discharge people without making determinations
of their fitness to stand trial or
their criminal responsibility. (p 80)
18. The power to discharge should operate as follows:
(a) It should apply to summary offences and indictable offences dealt with
summarily. The discharge power should not apply to matters
that are referred to
the Magistrates Court for trial from the Supreme Court by virtue of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 308.
(b) The threshold test for the operation of the discretion to discharge
should be that there is a real and substantial question about
defendant’s fitness to stand trial.
(c) Advice in relation to the issue of fitness for the purpose of exercising
the discretion should be provided to the magistrate by
the Court Liaison
Officers. Appropriate resources should be made available to the Court Liaison
Service for this purpose.
(d) The power should operate as a genuine discretion vested in the magistrate
and should not require the consent of the prosecution
and defence.
(e) Legislative guidance as to the factors that are relevant to the exercise
of the discretion to discharge the charges should be
provided and include: (1)
the nature of the offence, (2) the nature of the impairment, (3) the risk to the
community, (4) the treatment,
support or services available to the accused.
(f) The power to discharge should only provide for an unconditional discharge
and not a conditional discharge or the power to order
other therapeutic
intervention by the magistrate. (p 80)
19. The approach taken to the use of intermediaries and communication
assistants in special hearings should reflect the approach recommended
Institute’s Final Report on the establishment of an
Intermediary/Communication Assistant scheme in Tasmania. This will
legislative amendment of the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas). (p 90)
20. There should be legislative recognition that, in the conduct of an
investigation into fitness to enter a guilty plea/to stand
trial and in special
hearings, a person’s rights, wishes and preferences should be given effect
where a person is capable of
giving instructions. However, in circumstances
where defendants are not capable of giving instructions even with support, legal
practitioners
must act to promote and uphold the person’s personal and
social wellbeing in a way least restrictive of their human rights.
21. There should be provision in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
(Tas) for a special hearing by a judge alone where the prosecution
and defence agree that the defence of mental or cognitive impairment
established on the evidence. However, safeguards are required and the court
should have the power to review the evidence
(including hearing from expert
witnesses) in order to be satisfied that the evidence establishes the defence of
mental or cognitive
impairment in a case where the prosecution and defence agree
that the proposed evidence establishes the defence. (p 93)
22. The Institute recommends making changes to the current defence of
insanity contained in the
Criminal Code
s 16. (p 114)
23. The Institute does not recommend abolition of the defence of insanity
simpliciter. (p 119)
24. The Institute recommends retaining and reforming the current defence of
insanity. (p 119)
25. The insanity defence in s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) should not
be wholly replaced by a new defence. (p 125)
26. The name of the insanity defence in s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) should be changed. (p 128)
27. The name of the defence in s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) should
be changed to the defence of mental or cognitive impairment. (p 128)
28. The qualifying condition contained in the
Criminal Code
should be changed from a ‘mental disease’ to mental or cognitive
impairment with detailed definition of these
terms included in the section. (p
29. The definition of mental and cognitive impairment should be based on the
definitions set out by the NSWLRC as follows:
Mental impairment means a temporary or continuing disturbance of thought,
mood, volition, perception, or memory that impairs emotional
wellbeing, judgment
or behaviour, so as to affect functioning in daily life to a material extent.
Such mental impairment may arise from but is not limited to the following:
(i) anxiety disorders
(ii) affective disorders
(iii) psychoses
(iv) substance induced mental disorders.
‘Substance induced mental disorders’ include ongoing mental
impairments, but do not include substance abuse disorders
(addiction to
substances) or the temporary effects of ingesting substances.
Cognitive impairment means an ongoing impairment in comprehension, reason,
adaptive functioning, judgement, learning or memory that
is the result of any
damage to, dysfunction, developmental delay, or deterioration of the brain or
Such cognitive impairment may arise from, but is not limited to, the
(i) intellectual disability
(ii) dementias
(iii) acquired brain injury
(iv) drug or alcohol related brain damage
(vi) autism spectrum disorders. (p 145)
30. Section 16(4) of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) should be repealed. (p
31. The legislative definition of mental impairment and cognitive impairment
should not expressly include or exclude personality disorders
from the defence.
Criminal Code
s 16(1)(a)(i) should be amended to
refer to ‘understanding the nature and quality of such act or
omission’. (p 148)
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16(1)(b) should be retained without
amendment. (p 150)
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16(3) should be repealed.(p 159)
35. A delusional belief should not form the basis for self-defence and a
provision should be inserted in the
to provide that if a person does
an act or makes an omission as a result of a delusion caused by a mental or
cognitive impairment,
the delusion can only be used as a defence under s 16 of
Criminal Code
(Tas) and cannot be relied on to support a defence of
self-defence under s 46 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas). (p 160)
36. The burden of proof in relation to the defence of mental or cognitive
impairment should remain with the defence in cases where
it is raised by the
defence. (p 162)
The prosecution should have the power to raise the defence of
or cognitive impairment against the wishes of the defendant with
the leave of the court. In this case, the onus of proof should be
prosecution and the standard of proof should be the balance of probabilities. (p
38. The court should have the power to dispense with a trial if the
prosecution and defence agree that evidence establishes the defence
of mental or
cognitive impairment. However, safeguards are required and the court should have
the power to review the evidence (including
hearing from expert witnesses) in
order to be satisfied that the evidence establishes the defence of mental or
cognitive impairment
in a case where the prosecution and defence agree that the
proposed evidence establishes the defence. (p 171)
39. The new defence of mental or cognitive impairment introduced to amend the
current defence of insanity contained in the
Criminal Code
should apply to indictable and summary offences. (p 172)
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) should be
amended by abolishing the indefinite nature of forensic orders and introducing
limiting terms. (p 203)
41. In imposing a forensic order under the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas), the court must nominate a limiting term, which
would be the court’s best estimate of the length of the sentence that
would have been imposed had the person been found guilty at a normal trial (a
hypothetical sentence). (p 207)
42. A forensic patient should be able to apply to the Supreme Court for a
discharge of a restriction order or the variation or revocation
of a supervision
order prior to the expiry of the limiting term. (p 207)
43. A limiting term should not operate as an absolute limit on the duration
of an order. There should be power for the prosecution
to make an application to
extend the order. (p 212)
44. The NSW model should be adopted in Tasmania to allow for the extension of
orders beyond the limiting term set by the court, with
appropriate changes to
reflect the Tasmanian approach to decision-making and the role of the Mental
Health Tribunal. The scheme for
the extension of orders should have the
following key features:
(a) The DPP should be able to make an application to the Supreme Court prior
to the expiration of the limiting term for an extension
of a restriction order
or a supervision order. The DPP should also be able to apply for a restriction
order to be replaced with a
supervision order instead of an extension of the
restriction order.
(b) The Supreme Court should make the extension order on the basis of a
twofold assessment of the unacceptable risk of serious harm
to others posed by
the forensic patient if released from detention or supervision and the
inadequacy of other less restrictive means
of managing the risk. This regime
reflects the principles contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
, which provides that the ‘court is to apply, where
appropriate, the principle that restrictions on the defendant’s freedom
and personal autonomy should be kept to the minimum consistent with the safety
of the community.’
(c) Considerations that the court should have regard to include:
• the safety of the community
• expert reports
• any certificates issued by the MHT
• the person’s level of compliance with any obligations imposed
while being a forensic patient, especially while they
were on leave
• the view of the court at the time the limiting term was imposed
• a report from the forensic patient’s treatment team which would
include information about the need for ongoing management
of the person and the
reasons why arrangements that do not involve continued supervision or detention
are not appropriate
• a report on the attitudes of victims, if any, and next of kin.
(d) The term of the extension order should be five years subject to further
(e) The MHT should be mandated to conduct annual reviews of the extended
forensic order.
(f) The Supreme Court should have the power to make an interim extension
order if it appears that the limiting term or existing extension
expire before the proceedings are determined and that the matters alleged in the
supporting documentation would, if proved,
justify the making of an extension
(g) The Supreme Court should at any time be able to vary or revoke an
extension order on the application of the DPP or the person
subject to the
forensic order if satisfied that circumstances have changed sufficiently to
render the order unnecessary. (p 215)
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
should be changed to refer to whether the defendant does, or would if released,
pose an unacceptable risk of causing physical or
psychological harm to a person
or persons generally. (p 221)
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
should be amended to require the Mental Health Tribunal and the court to have
regard to the person’s recovery or progress in
terms of treatment
progression and personal improvement in making a decision to discharge a

a supervision order prior to the
expiration of the limiting term. (p 222)
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) should not
be amended to introduce a presumption that prior to the expiry of the limiting
term a court cannot discharge a restriction
order or revoke a supervision order
unless the court is satisfied in relation to the risk of harm test. (p 223)
48. There should be a statutory requirement in the
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) 1999
(Tas) for the court to take account of the

in favour of varying, revoking or discharging a forensic order if

(Tas) that expressly empowers
courts to make suppression orders and that sets out that the purpose of making
suppression orders is
to enable the long-term recovery of people subject to the

of the community. (p 226)
50. The Tasmanian Government should initiate a review to identify the

into the community with a view to ensuring that appropriate pathways are

Introduction
Background to
the reference
1.1.1 In its Report,
Review of the Law Relating
Self-defence
(Final Report No 20), the Tasmania Law Reform
Institute (TLRI) recommended that a review of the defence of insanity contained
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16 should be undertaken. Concerns were
expressed about the extent to which the criminal law reflects contemporary
knowledge about mental illness. Of particular concern was the
relationship between delusions and self-defence and whether mistakes
from insane delusions can ever ground the defence of self-defence. The Report
noted that to permit complete acquittals in
cases of insane delusions does not
enable appropriate treatment to be provided to deluded defendants or take into
account the need
for community protection. Ultimately, the TLRI recommended that
if a person does an act or makes an omission as a result of a delusion
a mental disease, the delusion should only be used as a defence under s 16 of
Criminal Code
and should not be relied on to support a defence of
self-defence under s
1.1.2 Following the TLRI’s recommendations, the then Attorney-General
of Tasmania, the Hon Vanessa Goodwin, requested the TLRI
to provide advice in
the following terms:
To consider the operation of the law of insanity in Tasmania with particular
reference to:
• the operation of s 16(3) of the
Criminal Code
• whether evidence of insane delusions arising from mental illness
should form the basis of self-defence;
• if insane delusions arising from mental illness form the basis of
self-defence, whether defendants relying on insane delusions
should be liable to
supervision under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
• if insane delusions arising from mental illness form the basis of
self-defence, whether the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) requires amendment in relation to treatment options for such
defendants.
To consider the operation of the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas) and whether changes are needed to ensure that the Act operates
justly, effectively and consistently with the principles that
underlie it. In
particular, the Institute should consider whether:
• the process of determining fitness to stand trial or establishing the
defence of insanity can be improved; and
• the operation of
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) including in relation to discharge and review of
forensic and treatment orders and whether there is a need for
‘step-down’

Scope of the
1.4.1 This Report considers two specialised responses for people with mental
illness and/or cognitive impairment where their mental
impairment affects their
ability to participate in the ordinary processes of the criminal justice system:
(1) fitness to stand trial; and
(2) the defence of insanity.
In examining the law of insanity, this Report considers the interaction of
evidence of an individual’s mental health and/or
cognitive impairment with
other defences, in particular the law of self-defence and the law of
intoxication. It also examines the
orders that may be made following a special
hearing in circumstances where a person has been found to be not fit to stand
after a person has been found not guilty by reason of insanity. A key
point to observe is that a person is
guilty of an offence where he or she is found not guilty by reason of insanity
or unfit to stand trial if a finding is not
able to be made at a special hearing
that the person is not guilty. Accordingly, the person is not
‘sentenced’ for the
offence according to the ordinary principles of
sentencing. Instead, the dispositions available to the judge or magistrate rely
an assessment of the future risk posed by the person to inform the exercise
of judicial discretion.
1.4.2 This Report examines the operation of fitness to stand trial and
insanity provisions for individuals with mental health and/or
impairments. The use of terminology and the scope of definitions are problematic
given that medical and legal definitions
may differ depending on the purpose and
context in which they are
1.4.3 This Report does not involve a review of the
Mental Health Act 2013
(Tas) but does consider the interaction between the criminal justice system
and that Act. In addition, it does not review the
Guardianship and
Administration Act 1995
(Tas) but, as noted earlier, the TLRI has undertaken
a separate review of that Act.
Further, this Report does not review the Diversion List that operates in the
Magistrates Court for individuals who have mental health
and/or cognitive
impairments.
Institute’s recommendations in relation to the recognition of supports and
accommodation in the test for fitness to stand
trial should be read in
conjunction with the recommendations contained in the Institute’s previous
Facilitating Equal Access to Justice: An Intermediary/Communication
Assistant Scheme for
This Report
recommended the introduction of an expert intermediary/communication assistant
scheme in Tasmania for people with communication
needs involved in the criminal
justice system.
and Principles
Introduction
2.1.1 This Part contains an overview of the legislative framework that sets
out the current law in relation to the operation of the
criminal justice system
for people with mental health and/or cognitive impairments. It sets out the
distinction between fitness to
stand trial and the defence of insanity and also
considers the principles that underlie the
Criminal (Justice Mental)
Impairment Act 1999
Brief overview
of legislative framework
2.2.1 In Tasmania, several Acts set out the law in relation to the operation
of the criminal justice system for people with mental
health and/or cognitive
impairments. These include:
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
Establishes:
• the process and criteria for determining if a person is unfit to
stand trial;
• the procedure for a special hearing following a finding of
unfitness to stand trial;
• the findings available at a special hearing;
• the consequences of a finding that an accused is not guilty by
reason of insanity (either following an ordinary trial or a
special hearing) or
where a finding cannot be made that the defendant is not guilty;
• the process and criteria for the discharge of a restriction order,
and the variation or revocation of a supervision order.
The Criminal Code
• Sets down the law and procedure for determining if an accused is
not guilty by reason of insanity.
Mental Health Act 2013
• for the management of involuntary patients (including people placed
on a treatment order);
• for the management of forensic patients (including people placed on
restriction and supervision orders);
• the procedure for the review of orders by the MHT.
Sentencing Act 1997
• contains the process and criteria for making an assessment order,
treatment, supervision and restriction order in respect
of offenders found
guilty of an offence.
2.2.2 Some individuals who have mental health
and/or cognitive impairments who may be unfit to stand trial and/or who may be
to rely on the insanity defence, might opt instead to participate in the
Diversion List (if
An outcome of the Diversion List is to modify and improve court processes to
reflect the court’s aspiration to improve access
to justice for
individuals with mental health problems or cognitive
disabilities.
While a person is
engaged with the diversion program, a treatment plan is developed for them and
their compliance with that plan
is relevant to the final disposition made by the
court. This may include the prosecution withdrawing the charges or the person
a lesser sentence than they would have received if their case had been
heard according to the ordinary court
Another aim of the Diversion List is to achieve long-term savings for the court
and community by reducing reoffending. A concomitant
result is the reduced
number of special hearings under
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
For example, in an evaluation of the Diversion List conducted in 2009, it was
reported that a benefit had been a decrease in the
need for special hearings in
the Magistrates Court.
2.2.3 A Diversion List has operated in the
Magistrates Court in Tasmania since the implementation of a pilot program in
2007. This has now been expanded to Launceston, Burnie and Devonport.
The Diversion List is a pre-sentence ‘problem-solving’
approach that
uses the provisions of the
Bail Act 1994
(Tas) and the
Sentencing Act
(Tas) to divert eligible individuals to mental health, disability and
other welfare services in order to address the underlying issues
criminal offending where they have been charged with summary offences or minor
indictable offences that can be tried
The Diversion List
targets repeat offenders where the nature of their offending is usually not
serious enough to warrant a prison
sentence or a community service
In 2011, it was reported
that an average of 500 Hobart defendants are referred by the court to Forensic
Mental Health Court Liaison
Officers each year for assessment and as of April
2011, 231 individuals had been referred to the
2.2.4 In addition, as discussed further in
, a significant proportion
of defendants have mental and/or cognitive impairments, and, as noted, only a
small number of such people
rely on unfitness to stand trial and/or the defence
of insanity. Many more people with mental and/or cognitive impairments do not
rely on the unfitness procedure or the insanity defence and instead enter a plea
of guilty and/or are found guilty following a trial.
Accordingly, in
understanding the operation of the criminal justice system in relation to people
with mental and/or cognitive impairments,
it is important to understand the role
of impairment as a sentencing factor. This is discussed at [3.3].
Understanding
fitness to stand trial and the defence of insanity
2.3.1 Although there may be an overlap in the conditions that give rise to a
finding that an individual is unfit to stand trial and
a finding that an
individual is not guilty by reason of insanity, the scope of their operation and
their legal requirements are different.
The difference
between insanity and fitness to stand trial
2.3.2 As shown in Table 2.1 below, fitness to stand trial is a
provision that relates to the issue of whether the accused has
the ability to understand or participate in his or her
It is concerned with the
accused’s capacity at the
time of the trial
. It applies where a
person’s mental processes are disordered or impaired, or if for any other
reason the person is unable to
understand court processes and make a defence to
Case example (unfit but no issue of insanity)
D was found unfit to stand trial for offences of arson and attempted arson by
agreement of the prosecution and defence. D has Alzheimer’s
Following a special hearing conducted before a jury, the jury returned a verdict
that they were unable to find D not guilty.
D was released unconditionally.
2.3.3 In contrast, insanity is a matter of
substantive
involving the determination of a whether an accused should not be held
criminally responsible on the ground that he or she lacked
the mental capacity
to commit the
Insanity is concerned with an accused’s capacity at the
time of the
. In order to rely on the insanity defence, the accused must have a
mental disease (which includes natural imbecility) such that the
person lacked
the capacity to understand the physical character of the act or know that the
act was one which he or she ought not
do, or, by reason of the mental disease,
the person was deprived of the power to resist an
Case example (no issue of fitness but insanity)
D was charged with murder. He suffered from schizophrenia or a schizophrenic
condition which resulted in an acute psychosis. Expert
evidence was given that
this disorder deprived him of the capacity to know the acts were ones which he
ought not to do. Following
a jury trial, D was found not guilty by reason of
insanity. D was detained in a secure mental health unit under a restriction
2.3.4 Consequently, an accused may be unfit to stand trial but not insane for
the purposes of the insanity defence, or fit to stand
trial but insane, or unfit
and insane.
Case example (unfit and insanity)
D was charged with assaulting a police officer. D suffered from severe
schizophrenia. Following a finding that D was unfit to stand
trial, at the
special hearing, the jury found that D was not guilty on the grounds of insanity
because D was incapable of knowing
that his acts were ones he ought not to do. D
was detained in a secure mental health unit under a restriction order.

It is important to note

from a finding of guilt (unlike the ordinary trial where sentencing

(Tas), the court can make a treatment order, a supervision order or a

principles underlying the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
Background to
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
2.4.1 In 1999, the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) made significant changes to the law that applies
to individuals with mental illness and/or cognitive impairment in Tasmania.
was part of a package of reforms that followed from a review of the
Health Act 1963
(Tas) and sought to address deficiencies that existed in the
previous law. Formerly, the procedural law on the insanity defence, fitness
plead and disposition of individuals were contained in the
Criminal Code
Mental Health Act 1963
(Tas). Under these provisions, if a
person had been found unfit to stand trial or found not guilty by reason of
insanity then:
Criminal Code
required a judge to make an order that the
person was to be dealt with as a mentally disordered person who had become
subject to the
criminal process. There was no discretion in relation to
(2) When the judge made the order, the Attorney-General was required to
specify an institution in which the person was to be detained,
which was a
Special Institution at Risdon Prison.
(3) When the Attorney-General made the order, the
Criminal Code
stated that the person was to be treated for the purposes of
Mental Health Act 1963
as having been admitted in pursuance of a
hospital order made together with a restriction order.
(4) Once the Attorney-General made the order the person could only be
released by the Governor acting on the advice of the Executive
Council and on
the recommendation of the Mental Health Review Tribunal. The decision as to
whether or not to accept the recommendations
of the tribunal was generally made
by Cabinet.
This was ‘akin to a sentence for the term of the defendant’s
natural life’ (at least for some people) given that
the decision to
release a person ‘was for practical purposes in the hands of the State
Cabinet and therefore subject to political
pressure from a community with little
compassion for, or understanding of, mental
However, while there is a lack of statistics, it has been reported that the
‘Governor’s pleasure regime’ did allow
for the release of some
people after relatively short periods of
2.4.2 Changes were made in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas) in relation to two key issues to address these concerns:
Procedures for a special hearing were created where an accused was
found unfit to stand trial
. Previously, if a person was declared unfit, then
they were detained under a restriction order, ‘notwithstanding the fact
these persons have not been convicted of any offence and irrespective of
both the seriousness of the mental illness, and whether
or not they are a danger
to the public’.
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas), a special hearing
was created to ‘determine the external facts of the case — excluding
the question of intention
— to ascertain whether the basic prosecution
case can be proved beyond reasonable
At the special
hearing, the following findings are available: not guilty, not guilty of the
offence charged on the grounds of insanity,
or that a finding cannot be made
that the defendant is not
It is noted that

The process for determining the appropriate disposition order for
persons found unfit to stand trial or not guilty by reasons of insanity
court was given a discretion in relation to the disposition options ranging from
an unconditional release order up to the making
of a restriction order. The
court, rather than the executive, was also given the power to revoke or
discharge orders. There was a
power for a person to make an application to the
Supreme Court for release every two years and for review hearings to be
annually by the MHT. Under these provisions, if the MHT is of the view
that a restriction order or a supervision order is no longer
required, it issues
a Certificate that allows the person to apply to the Supreme Court for the
release of the restriction order or
the revocation of the supervision order. The
purpose of this was to provide for the eventual release of persons found unfit
trial or not guilty by reason of
Accordingly, while the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) did not make any
changes to the defence of insanity in the
Criminal Code
(Tas), it did
make significant changes to the consequences of a finding that an individual was
not guilty by reason of insanity.
2.4.3 In making these changes, the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
(Tas) was intended to modernise the law in relation to mental
impairment and to recognise the rights of defendants while at the same
protecting public safety.
reflects the background to the unfitness to stand trial doctrine that ‘was
largely incorporated into modern law as a humanistic
measure to protect accused
persons with disabilities, offer a mechanism to test the prosecution, and divert
individuals to relevant
treatment’.
Principles that
underlie the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
2.4.4 The Terms of Reference require the TLRI to consider whether changes are
needed to the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
ensure that it operates justly, effectively and consistently with the principles
that underlie it. The principles that can
be identified from the legislative
provisions and background to the implementation of the Act are: fairness to the
accused person
and the right to a fair trial, the protection of the community
and the recognition of the rights of mentally impaired defendants
with the principles of least
restriction.
2.4.5 In addition, the TLRI’s consideration

(Tas) is informed by
the obligations that arise under international legal instruments to which
Australia is a signatory including
International Covenant on Civil and
Political Rights
sets out the right to a fair trial, the right to be treated with

on the basis of disability, the right to equal

and security of the
In understanding the
