Fairness to an accused person and the right to a fair trial
2.4.6 Fundamental principles underpinning the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) are fairness to an accused person and the right
to a fair trial. This reflects the historical basis for the requirement that
individual must be fit to stand trial. As observed by the VLRC, ‘this
principle is based on the “central precept of
our criminal law ... that no
person shall be convicted of a crime otherwise than after a trial according to
Australian Law Reform Commission (‘ALRC’) has summarised the
justifications for the rule that a person who is unfit
to stand trial cannot be
tried as follows:
• [to] avoid inaccurate verdicts—forcing the defendant to be
answerable for his or her actions when incapable of doing
so could lead to an
inaccurate verdict;
• [to] maintain the ‘moral dignity’ of the trial
process—requiring that a defendant is fit to stand trial
recognises the
importance of maintaining the moral dignity of the trial process, ensuring that
the defendant is able to form a link
between the alleged crime and the trial or
punishment and is accountable for his or her actions; and
• [to] avoid unfairness—it would be unfair or inhumane to subject
someone to the trial process who is
In his submission to this reference, Jamie Walvisch addresses the basis on
which it is appropriate for a person to stand trial for
his or her alleged
actions. He writes ‘that to properly be able to stand trial, a person
needs to be capable of answering for
his or her own
observes that:
This follows from the nature of the criminal trial, which aims to call those
who are accused of perpetrating public wrongs to account.
In doing so, the
polity addresses the accused as a ‘responsible agent’ — as
‘someone who can be called to
answer the charge, and to answer for the
wrongdoing if [he or] she is proved to have committed it; as someone who is
capable of thus
answering...’ If the accused lacks the capacities
necessary to understand the charge or to account for his or her actions (e.g.,
due to a mental illness), then he or she cannot properly engage in the criminal
trial, and must not be held
responsible.
2.4.7 In addition to providing that a person who is unfit to stand trial is
not subject to the normal trial procedure, the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) sets out a process for providing a fair hearing
to test the evidence against a person found unfit to stand
In order to address
concerns about the imposition of orders on a person who has not been established
to have committed an offence
and without the opportunity of an acquittal, the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) provides for a
special hearing. The Act provides that special hearings are ‘to be
conducted so that the onus of proof
and standard of proof are the same as in a
trial and in all other respects as nearly as possible as if it were a trial of
proceedings’.
a special hearing, the defendant can raise any defence that could be raised if
the special hearing were an ordinary trial and
the defendant is entitled to give
In addition, the
defendant’s legal representatives are able to challenge
Following the special
hearing, the jury can make a finding that the person is not guilty, which has
the same effect as if the accused
was acquitted following an ordinary trial
process, or a finding that the accused is not guilty by reason of insanity or
that a finding
cannot be made that the defendant is not guilty of the
Least restrictive alternative
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
expressly states that the court is to apply the principle that
‘restrictions on the defendant’s freedom and personal
should be kept to the minimum consistent with the safety of the
community’.
As noted by
The principle of least restriction is fundamental to considering the
protection of the community, as the rehabilitation of people
subject to the CMIA
Crimes (Mental Impairment and Unfitness to be Tried) Act 1997
through successful community reintegration is the best way to ensure protection
of the community, as well as restoring the
person to a state in which they can
be a functioning member of that
This principle applies to the decision of the court in relation to which
dispositions to impose on an individual, the conditions of
such an order and the
decision as to whether to discharge or vary the order. In reviewing forensic
orders under the
Mental Health Act 2013
(Tas), the MHT must also have
regard to this principle.
Community protection
2.4.9 Connected with the principle of least restrictive alternative is the
need for community protection. A key focus of the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) is the need to protect community safety,
including the need to recognise the interests of victims and family members.
to reflect the interests of a person with mental health and cognitive
impairment within the criminal justice process and the needs
of the community
and victims was articulated in the Butler Report:
It is fundamental for all mental health consumers, including forensic
patients, to be accorded basic rights and treated with humanity
and respect.
They should not be discriminated against on the grounds of mental illness.
Where, however, a person has committed a
serious criminal act, particularly a
violent act, consideration must also be given to the fundamental rights of
others to protection
2.4.10 Community protection is reflected in the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) with the court being directed to have regard to
whether the defendant is, or would if released be, likely to endanger another
person or other persons
The court is also
directed to have regard to the nature of the defendant’s mental impairment
or other condition or
disability,
and a court must not
discharge a restriction order, release a defendant or significantly reduce the
degree of supervision, unless
it has considered expert reports on the condition
of the defendant and the possible effect of the proposed action on the behaviour
of the defendant.
In reviewing
forensic orders under the
Mental Health Act 2013
(Tas), the MHT must also
have regard to these principles.
Community protection is also a factor relevant to the operation of the insanity
defence (as discussed at [
2.4.11 Related to community protection, a significant factor that is
recognised under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
is the rights of victims and family members. Before varying or discharging an
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) provides
that the court must be provided with a report stating the views of the next of
kin of the defendant and the victims
of the defendant’s conduct, so far as
is reasonably ascertainable.
Mental Health Impairments and
Cognitive Impairments and the Criminal Justice System
Introduction
3.1.1 Insanity and fitness to stand trial apply to only a small number of
individuals compared to the number of defendants with mental
cognitive impairments overall who are involved in the criminal justice system.
This Part provides an overview of mental
illness and cognitive impairments and
the criminal justice system.
Overview of
mental illness and cognitive impairments and the criminal justice system
3.2.1 Research has concluded that there ‘is strong evidence that people
with cognitive and mental health impairments are over-represented
throughout the
criminal justice
In 2015, the Australian Institute of Health and Welfare reported that 49 per
cent of prison entrants had disclosed that they had
been told by a doctor,
psychiatrist, psychologist or nurse that they had a mental health disorder
(including drug or alcohol
In Victoria, it has been
estimated that ‘approximately 55% of offenders at court suffer some form
of mental impairment’.
There are also high rates of cognitive impairment identified in individuals in
Victorian research has
found that people with intellectual disabilities are at increased risk of having
a history of criminal charges,
particularly for violent and sexual
The Law Council of
Australia has observed that ‘[b]etween 50 to 78 per cent of prisoners have
experienced a psychiatric disorder
compared with 11 per cent of the general
population, and 20 per cent of prisoners have an intellectual disability
compared with 2
to 3 per cent of the general
population’.
context, Gooding and his colleagues have written that, ‘[a]lthough there
are significant issues with data collection,
[this finding] echo[s] a growing
body of international research suggesting people with cognitive disabilities are
significantly over-represented
... in the criminal justice systems of Western,
high-income countries’.
International research also indicates that the prevalence of mental health
impairments in individuals in contact with the criminal
justice system is much
higher than rates in the general
population.
Bagaric and Edney
indicate that ‘most studies which have been undertaken to measure the
prevalence of mental disorders among
criminal offenders suggest that it is very
high: ranging from approximately one-third to half of criminal
offenders’.
3.2.2 In its submission, Community Legal Centres Tasmania (‘CLC
Tas’) highlighted the growing prison population in Tasmania
overrepresentation of persons with cognitive or mental health impairments in the
criminal justice system. CLC Tas noted that
Australian Institute of Health and
Welfare research found that ‘40% of Tasmanian prison entrants are referred
to prison mental
health services; the second highest rate in
Australia’.
As noted below
], there have been concerns
raised about whether Risdon Prison has appropriate and adequate provision to
meet the mental health care
needs of the Tasmanian prisoner population.
3.2.3 Higher involvement in the criminal justice
system, however, does not reflect a simple relationship between impairment and
Instead, ‘it is frequently the product of impairment together with
other factors, such as disrupted family backgrounds, family
violence, abuse,
misuse of drugs and alcohol, and unstable
Other factors
that have been identified include the ‘deinstitutionalisation of mentally
ill people ... and the limited capacity
of community-based mental health
services to address the needs of mentally ill
offenders’.
Further, as
Cunneen et al observe, ‘for people with mental and cognitive impairment,
the majority of research finds no inherent
link between these illnesses or
disabilities and crime, but a strong causal link between disability and
incarceration’.
the risk of being drawn into the criminal justice system is not spread evenly
for those with mental and cognitive impairments,
as ‘it is only those who
are seriously socially disadvantaged (homeless mentally ill persons in
particular) and from racialised
communities who are likely to be
imprisoned’.
Mental health
impairment and cognitive impairment as sentencing factors
3.3.1 Although a review of the relevance of mental health impairment and
cognitive impairment as a sentencing factor is beyond the
scope of this
reference, it is important to be aware of the operation of the sentencing
process for several reasons. First, an accused
with a mental health or cognitive
impairment may not rely on the process of fitness to stand trial and/or on the
insanity defence
but may proceed through the usual criminal justice process.
This is a clear alternative and the possible sentence received (if found
compared to the consequences of a finding of unfitness or insanity are likely to
weigh into the decision-making process.
As discussed below, there has been a
marked shift in relation to the types of mental health and cognitive impairments
that may mitigate
sentencing and this broadening of approach, together with the
sentencing discount that a guilty plea attracts, may make a guilty
plea a more
attractive option from an accused’s point of view given that the sentence
imposed by the court may be less restrictive
than if the person relies on
unfitness to stand trial and/or insanity and is placed on a restriction or
supervision order.
3.3.2 However, it needs to be borne in mind that
the nature of the deprivation of liberty and the treatment services that a
will receive in prison are not the same as a person detained at the
Secure Mental Health Unit (SMHU) or a supervision order in the
Concerns have been raised about the level of services available for prisoners
with mental health needs at the Risdon Complex
and a taskforce has been
established to investigate the
As noted by CLC Tas, the
2015 Australian Institute of Health and Welfare report into the health of
prisoners in Australia, found
that of those Tasmanian prisoners with a mental
impairment, 54% are discharged with either no improvement or with deteriorating
health compared to when they were
The Custodial
Inspector Tasmania has recently reported that ‘current mental health
services do not meet the needs of the Tasmanian
population.’
issues identified were understaffing, lack of mental health leadership in the
prison, no formal service level agreement
with the Forensic Mental Health
Service, a lack of dedicated spaces that are conducive to the provision of
mental health care in
the prisons, and the need for ongoing training and support
for correctional officers to allow them to ‘understand and manage
with mental health
3.3.3 Another factor that makes it important to be aware of the sentencing
approach to mental health and cognitive impairments is
that if changes are made
to the test for fitness to stand trial (such as the use of supports,
accommodations and intermediaries),
which would assist people to be fit for
trial or if the concept of fit to enter a plea was adopted in
then the approach
taken to the accused’s impairment at the sentencing stage will be
important. Walvisch has argued that ‘great
care would need to be taken to
ensure that the accused person’s impairment be properly taken into account
in the sentencing
process’ and that it would be a concern ‘if such a
system were implemented which assisted an accused to face charges
at trial, only
to abandon him or her at the sentencing
In his submission,
Walvisch reiterated his concern that ‘in implementing such measures, great
care should be taken to ensure
that the accused person’s impairment is
properly taken into account in the sentencing
3.3.4 In Tasmania, as with other jurisdictions, an offender’s mental or
cognitive impairment is relevant to the sentencing
While it is not known
what number of offenders raise mental health impairment or cognitive impairment
as mitigating factors at sentencing
(or, as discussed at [3.2], the number of
offenders with mental health impairment or cognitive impairments who are
involved in the
criminal justice system), it is likely that this may affect a
significant proportion of offenders. Anecdotally, it appears that there
an increase in the number of sentencing submissions in Tasmania that rely on
expert reports outlining impairment. This raises
complex issues. Bagaric and
Edney have written that ‘the sentencing of mentally impaired offenders is
one of the most complex
and acute issues in the criminal justice
3.3.5 The difficulty that arises in sentencing offenders with mental health
or cognitive impairment stems from the number of offenders
who suffer some form
of mental or cognitive impairment and the need to reconcile the tension between
two competing considerations:
the nature of the condition that may reduce the
moral culpability of the offender that may justify a more lenient sentence on
one hand, and, on the other, the nature of the condition that may indicate
that the offender is ‘a more intractable subject
for reform than one who
is not so affected or even as one who is so likely to offend again that he [or
she] should be removed from
society for a lengthy or indeterminate
As recognised in
Veen v The Queen
a mental abnormality which makes an offender a danger to society when he is
at large but which diminishes his moral culpability for
a particular crime is a
factor which has two countervailing effects: one which tends towards a longer
custodial sentence, the other
towards a shorter. These effects may balance out,
but consideration of the danger to society cannot lead to the imposition of a
severe penalty than would have been imposed if the offender had not been
suffering from a mental
abnormality.
Accordingly, in the circumstances of a case, the nature of the
offender’s mental or cognitive impairment may be such that protection
the community carries greater weight in the exercise of the sentencing
discretion. This involves balancing the offender’s
culpability and the
need to protect the community. This complexity has been recognised by the
Tasmanian Court of Criminal
3.3.6 More recently, the principles set out by the Victorian Court of Appeal
R v Verdins
adopted in Tasmania in relation to the mitigation of
These principles
recognise that an offender’s impaired mental functioning, whether
temporary or permanent, is relevant to sentencing
in at least six ways:
1. to reduce the offender’s moral culpability, thereby affecting the
punishment that is just in the circumstances and the importance
of denunciation
as a sentencing consideration;
2. to influence the kind of sentence that should be imposed, or the
conditions under which it should be served;
3. to moderate or eliminate the need for general deterrence as a sentencing
consideration;
4. to moderate or eliminate the need for specific deterrence as a sentencing
consideration;
5. to make a sentence weigh more heavily on the offender than on a person in
normal health, thereby affecting the determination of
a proportionate sentence;
6. to create a serious risk of imprisonment having a significant adverse
effect on the offender’s mental health, suggesting
the need to reduce the
The decision in
was a change from the pre-existing position
‘that only “[s]erious psychiatric illnesses”’ needed to
into account by a sentencing
and so expanded the relevance of mental impairment as a factor relevant to
sentencing. However, following
, the extent to which the
principles applied to personality disorders was unclear and subsequent decisions
were inconsistent in this
3.3.7 The application of the
principles to personality
disorders was considered by the Victorian Court of Appeal in
Director of
Public Prosecutions v
In this case,
the court indicated that the
principles did not apply to the
personality disorder that affected the accused (dependent personality disorder)
but indicated that
this did not mean that the ‘personality disorder should
be completely disregarded by the sentencing
There are also
unresolved issues in relation to the application of the
principles as it is unclear whether the judgment was intended to exclude all
personality disorders (the broad interpretation) or only
those that were similar
in some way to dependent personality disorder (narrow
interpretation).
This ambiguity
has not been resolved by subsequent higher court cases in other
However, in
Tasmania it has been observed that the Court of Criminal Appeal appeared to
apply a narrow interpretation (that is, excluding
only personality disorders
that are similar to dependent personality disorder and not all personality
disorders) and so this is the
interpretation of the law that applies in
It is also clear that
a further limitation of the
principles exists in relation to
self-induced impairment (such as a mental impairment resulting for drug or
alcohol use).
3.3.8 Despite this uncertainty, it is clear that the court’s view was
that a rigorous assessment of the evidence was required.
The court expressed the
view that it was inappropriate to apply a mechanistic approach such that if an
offender suffered from any
impaired mental functioning the
principles were automatically attracted. Instead, the court stated that
careful consideration should be given to the evidence to determine
whether the
offender suffered from an impairment of mental functioning, and if so, whether
there was a connection between the impairment
and the person’s moral
culpability or the need for general and specific deterrence. This meant that
there needed to be a ‘realistic
connection’ between the impairment
and the offence or that it ‘caused or contributed’ to the offence or
was ‘causally
Further, it must be shown
that the mental impairment affected the offender’s ability to appreciate
the wrongfulness of the
conduct, or obscured his or her intent to commit the
offence, or impaired his or her ability to make calm and rational choices or
think clearly at the time of the
The ability of the
court to make this rigorous assessment is clearly dependent on the nature of the
information contained in the
expert reports received by the court. Further, the
information contained in the expert report, in more serious cases of mental or
cognitive impairment, may trigger an exploration of whether the appropriate
pathway for the offender is an order under the
Sentencing Act 1997
(restriction, supervision and treatment orders) rather than a sentencing
order under
–(h) of the
Sentencing Act 1997
3.3.9 Under the
Sentencing Act 1997
, an offender who is
found guilty of an offence may be sentenced to a restriction order, a
supervision order or a treatment order
in cases where the offender appears to be
suffering from a mental illness.
In a case where a person is found guilty, the court makes a restriction order
upon receiving a report from the Chief Forensic Psychiatrist,
psychiatrist, that the person appears to be suffering from a mental illness that
requires treatment, that such treatment
can be obtained by admission to and
detention in a secure mental health unit, that the person should be admitted as
a patient for
the person’s own safety and the Chief Forensic Psychiatrist
recommends the proposed
There is also power
for the court to make a treatment order or a supervision order, subject to the
receipt of an expert report.
its research, the TLRI identified nine cases in the Supreme Court between 2001
and 2016 where an offender was sentenced under
Sentencing Act 1997
(Tas) to either a sentence of imprisonment combined with a supervision order
(five cases) or imprisonment combined with a restriction
order (two cases) or a
supervision order (two cases). In eight cases, the offender entered a plea of
guilty and in one case, the
offender was found guilty by a jury of one offence
(the issue was whether the offender was guilty of attempted murder or attempted
wounding) and entered a plea of guilty to another charge.
3.3.10 The other context in which an offender’s mental condition at the
time of the offence is relevant to the imposition of
sentence, and, again, the
nature of the information contained in any reports provided to the court is
central to the exercise of
judicial discretion, is in making a dangerous
criminal declaration under the
Sentencing Act 1997
dangerous criminal provisions in the
Sentencing Act 1997
Division 3 allow the court to make a dangerous criminal declaration which
provides for indefinite detention and so an offender is
ineligible for release
until the declaration is
discharged.
One of the pre-conditions to making an order is the requirement that the judge
is of the opinion that the declaration is warranted
for the protection of the
Section 19(2)
out a non-exhaustive list of factors that are relevant to the making of the
declaration including medical opinion. It has been
stated that the
‘existence of a mental disorder making a convicted person prone to commit
random and impulsive acts of violence
would be a most material factor in
assessing whether to make a
declaration’.
interaction of an offender’s mental health and the dangerous criminal
provisions can be seen in the case of
McCrossen v
applicant’s complex mental health state was exacerbated by his indefinite
detention and long history of incarceration.
Expert reports to the court
highlighted the rehabilitative benefit to the offender that would result from
his being discharged from
the Dangerous Criminal Declaration and instead treated
in the secure mental health unit under the
Mental Health Act 2013
as an involuntary civil patient. This was the approach to the offender’s
rehabilitation that was taken by Wood J in making
an order discharging the
declaration:
What this case has demonstrated is that the indeterminate nature of the
sentence was in itself crushing and counterproductive in terms
applicant’s rehabilitation. Since there has been talk of the order being
lifted, the applicant has made significant
progress in a short time. To offset
these crushing effects, a prison system would have to be vigilant and have a
committed and co-ordinated
approach to an individual’s rehabilitation.
Questions may be asked about whether more could have been done from the
by the prison system to promote the applicant’s rehabilitation
in a committed way which recognised the applicant’s profound
difficulties
and mental health condition. His potential for reform cannot be doubted, noting
the marked significant improvement away
from the prison during his admissions to
the Wilfred Lopes Centre, particularly in 2009.
3.3.11 It may be that in cases where the basis for the need for community
protection arises from the nature of the offender’s
mental impairment, a
more appropriate pathway for the offender that will protect the community and
allow the offender to obtain appropriate
treatment may be found in the forensic
orders contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
rather than in indefinite detention as a dangerous criminal
Sentencing Act 1997
(Tas). This point was made clearly by the
High Court in
Chester v The
where the court
expressed the view that indefinite detention as a dangerous criminal should not
‘be contemplated when in due
course it may be more appropriate that there
be a justice’s order ... for reception into and detention in an approved
of a person suffering from mental
3.3.12 On this basis, in the Issues Paper the Institute identified that a
possible reform may be to amend the dangerous criminal provisions
contained in
Sentencing Act 1997
(Tas). The proposed reform provides a statutory
trigger for judicial consideration of the appropriateness of making an order for
for assessment with a view to making a restriction, supervision or
treatment order under the
Sentencing Act 1997
, rather than
making a dangerous criminal declaration. The Issues Paper asked:
Should there be an amendment to the dangerous criminal provisions contained
Sentencing Act 1997
(Tas) to provide a statutory trigger for
judicial consideration of the appropriateness of making an order under the
Sentencing Act 1997
instead of a dangerous criminal
declaration?
3.3.13 Five submissions addressed this issue with
two opposed to an amendment to the dangerous criminal provisions in the
Sentencing Act 1997
(Tas) to provide a statutory trigger for judicial
consideration of the appropriateness of making an order under the
instead of a dangerous criminal declaration.
3.3.14 The DPP and O’Connell both opposed such an amendment but
provided different reasons for their opposition. In her submission,
O’Connell opposed any change to the
Sentencing Act 1997
provisions on the basis that ‘the premise that there is a correlation
between dangerous criminals and mental illness is
a discriminatory one’.
Further, O’Connell’s view was that ‘any supervision by the
Chief Forensic Psychiatrist
should be limited to cases where the defendant has a
major mental illness’.
3.3.15 The DPP opposed an amendment to provide a statutory trigger because he
considered it to be unnecessary. He submitted that if
an order under the
Sentencing Act 1997
was appropriate (instead of a dangerous
criminal declaration), this is likely to be readily apparent at the time of
sentencing and
the court would have the benefit of the expert reports provided
at this time to facilitate the making of the appropriate sentencing
3.3.16 In contrast, Walvisch supported an amendment to the dangerous criminal
provisions to:
make it clear that mental health problems per se do not provide a basis for
making a ‘dangerous criminal’ declaration,
even if those mental
health problems are likely to result in violence; but that a consistent failure
to manage one’s mental
health, despite known risks of violence, may be
taken into account (along with the reasons for that failure).
He considered that it was unjust and highly stigmatising to label an
individual a ‘dangerous criminal’ where the
‘dangerousness’
arises from mental health problems over which the
person had no control. Further, in circumstances ‘where the alleged
“dangerousness”
arises from mental health problems over which the
offender has no control, appropriate treatment is more likely to reduce any
to the public than indefinite imprisonment’. In addition, making a
dangerous offender order on the basis of a person’s
mental illness appears
to be contrary to Australia’s international human rights obligations on
the basis that ‘it discriminates
against offenders based on their mental
health’. However, Walvisch considered that it may be appropriate to label
individuals
as ‘dangerous criminals’ if they consistently fail to
manage their own mental health for insufficient reasons. Sufficient
reason would
include ‘a lack of access to appropriate, affordable mental health
treatment; and a lack of insight into one’s
mental health problems or the
risks it poses’.
3.3.17 EOT directly supported amendment to the
Sentencing Act 1997
(Tas) to include a statutory trigger for judicial consideration of the
appropriateness of making an order for referral for assessment
subsequent making of a restriction, supervision or treatment order. In
EOT’s view, this is consistent with reforms
proposed by the TLRI in its
consideration of the dangerous criminal
legislation
and Article 25 of
which has the effect that people with mental illness should have
access to care, treatment and rehabilitation where this is required.
3.3.18 The Institute agrees that it is stigmatising and discriminatory to
perpetuate a stereotype of people who experience mental
illness as violent or to
conflate mental illness with
dangerousness.
Further, as
], there is a complex
relationship between mental illness or impairment and crime with research
showing that there is no necessary
link between mental and cognitive impairment
and crime but rather between disability and incarceration. The Institute also
that individuals with mental impairment who are sentenced by the court
should have access to appropriate treatment and that the court
should give
consideration to whether treatment in a health setting is more appropriate than
imprisonment. In asking for feedback
offender provisions and
Sentencing Act 1997
(Tas) (which
provides for the imposition of forensic orders or treatment orders), the
Institute did not intend to suggest that individuals
with mental illness were
more likely to be dangerous offenders or, conversely, could not be the subject
of a dangerous criminal declaration.
Rather, the Institute was concerned to
ensure that courts give appropriate consideration to legal responses other than

Sentencing Act 1997
However, based on the feedback received

and the matters courts take into account in deciding whether to make

3.3.19 This Report is not a review of the dangerous offender provisions in

The current legislation is limited in scope, and does not easily allow for a

to Stand Trial: Making an Assessment of Unfitness
Introduction
5.1.1 Unfitness to stand trial relates to the issue whether an accused is
able to participate in the trial process, and, as the Institute
has previously
written, this ability ‘fundamentally determine[s] whether that person can
gain access to justice and whether
justice can in fact be
Accordingly, the
basis used to make a finding of fitness or unfitness will have significant
consequences on an individual level.
The aim should be to maximise an
individual’s participation in the trial and provide a separate process for
those who are not
able to make decisions in their own trials even with the
provision of appropriate accommodations and supports.
5.1.2 Unfitness to stand trial is concerned with an assessment of the
accused’s ability at the
time of the trial
rather than at the time
of the offence. There is a presumption that those accused of committing crime
are fit to stand trial unless
it is established that they are unfit to stand
trial. This is determined on the balance of
probabilities.
The issue of
fitness may be raised by the prosecution, the defendant or the court (on its own
initiative) and may be raised at any
time after the accused has been charged,
including after the trial has
In order for a
court to conduct an investigation into a defendant’s fitness to stand
trial, there must be a real and substantial
question in relation to their
In circumstances
where evidence of a person’s fitness to stand trial is raised, a judge can
only accept a plea of guilty if
satisfied that no reasonable jury, properly
instructed, could find the person unfit to stand
5.1.3 The Terms of Reference request that the TLRI consider whether the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) operates justly,
effectively and consistently with the principles that underlie it. In
particular, the TLRI has been asked
to consider whether the process of
determining fitness to stand trial can be improved. There are several aspects to
this question
including the test that is used to make a finding of unfitness,
the procedure that is used to determine unfitness and the nature
of the special
hearing that follows a finding of unfitness. These are all interrelated issues
that need to be addressed in order
to facilitate access to justice for
individuals with mental health and cognitive impairments, as well as to ensure
that the community
is appropriately protected.
of this Report makes
recommendations about the process by which a determination of fitness to stand
trial is made and
makes recommendations for the conduct of special
hearings. This Part makes recommendations in relation to the test used to
fitness to stand trial. The Institute stresses that all these
recommendations need to be viewed as a package of reforms that work
together to
address concerns about the current approach to fitness to stand trial.
5.1.4 This Part addresses a key component of the process for determining
fitness to stand trial — the test applied by the court
when determining
whether defendants are fit or unfit to stand trial. This Part sets out the
current test for determining unfitness
to stand trial in Tasmania and considers
options for reform set out in the Issues Paper as well the submissions received
consultation process.
5.1.5 The current focus of the fitness test is on a person’s cognitive
capacity or intellectual ability to understand features
of the criminal trial.
Based on submissions received and its research, the TLRI is of the view that
this test is deficient in that
it does not give adequate recognition to whether
the accused can participate meaningfully in his or her trial. The answer to this
question necessarily depends on two key interrelated matters, first whether the
accused has the capacity to make decisions in relation
to key elements of the
trial and, second, whether the necessary conditions exist or can be created to
enable him or her to exercise
that decision-making capacity. Accordingly, in
this Part, the Institute recommends a shift from the current focus on cognitive
to a supported decision-making approach based on facilitating an
individual’s ability to exercise his/her decision-making capacity
effectively participate in the trial. The determination of an accused’s
decision-making capacity needs to be clearly grounded
in relevant determinative
criteria. Consequently, what these criteria are should also be determined and
specified in reforming the
fitness test. The purpose of such criteria is to
guide courts when applying what might otherwise appear to be a relatively vague
and subjective test. The Institute makes recommendations in this regard. In
summary, the approach recommended by the Institute has
three interrelated
components:
• the reformulation of the fitness test to replace the current
‘Presser’ test with a decision-making test;
• the specification of guidance/criteria in the application of that
test to give it content and avoid subjectivity in its application;
• a requirement for assessment of the need for and, where necessary,
creation of the conditions that optimise and/or enable
people to exercise their
decision-making capacity and therefore participate in trials. These might
include making necessary accommodations
in court processes and/or providing
decision-making supports through the use of communication
assistants/intermediaries.
5.1.6 These three components work together. Accordingly, in the discussion
that follows, this Report examines these three interlocked
components of the
recommended revised test: the test itself, the criteria underpinning its
application and the need to assess available
accommodations and supports that
may create conditions that optimise and render an accused’s
decision-making capacity a reality.
the test for determining unfitness to stand trial: the current test
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
out the test to determine if a person is unfit to stand trial:
(1) A person is unfit to stand trial for an offence if, because the
person’s mental processes are disordered or impaired or
for any other
reason, the person is –
(a) unable to understand the nature of the charge; or
(b) unable to plead to the charge or to exercise the right of challenge; or
(c) unable to understand the nature of the proceedings; or
(d) unable to follow the course of the proceedings; or
(e) unable to make a defence or answer the charge.
(2) Notwithstanding subsection (1)(e), a person is not unfit to be tried if
the only reason that the person is unable to make a defence
or answer a charge
is that he or she is suffering from memory loss.
5.2.2 These criteria reflect the
criteria, set out in the Victorian case of
where seven
factors were identified as relevant to the determination of fitness:
• ability to understand the charge;
• ability to plead to the charge and exercise the right to challenge
• ability to understand the nature of the proceedings;
• ability to follow the course of the proceedings in broad terms;
• ability to understand the substantial effect of any evidence;
• ability to make a defence or answer to the charge;
• ability to instruct counsel.
In order to be unfit to stand trial, an accused only needs to satisfy one of
the criteria, set out above, as each criterion stands
5.2.3 In Tasmania, there is little case law on the operation of the test.
However, guidance can be sought from other jurisdictions
as indicated in the
following extract from the DPP’s prosecution guidelines:
Where fitness to stand trial is an issue requiring investigation, the
principles discussed by Smith J in
R v Presser
[1958] VicRp 9
[1958] VR 45
[1958] VicRp 9
cited with approval in
Ngatayi v R

at 8, are of
consequence when considering the criteria laid out in
‘A mere lack of formal education, a mere lack of familiarity with court
forms and procedures, would not, of course, render a
man unfit to be tried, but
he may, upon the test of fitness for the purposes of the section that has been
laid down in the cases,
be held unfit to be tried when he is far from being
insane in the colloquial sense. Dixon, J, as he then was, mentioned in
Sinclair v R
[1946] HCA 55
(1946) 73 CLR 316

noticed by the text writers how high a degree of intelligence the test might
demand if it were
literally applied. But he is not there, in my view, suggesting
that it should be applied in any extreme sense, or in any over-literal
needs, I think, to be applied in a reasonable and commonsense fashion. And the
question, I consider, is whether the accused,
because of mental defect, fails to
come up to certain minimum standards which he needs to equal before he can be
tried without unfairness
or injustice to him.
He needs, I think, to be able to understand what it is that he is charged
with. He needs to be able to plead to the charge and to
exercise his right of
challenge. He needs to understand generally the nature of the proceeding,
namely, that it is an inquiry as
to whether he did what he is charged with. He
needs to be able to follow the course of the proceedings so as to understand
going on in court in a general sense, though he need not, of course,
understand the purpose of all the various court formalities.
He needs to be able
to understand, I think, the substantial effect of any evidence that may be given
against him; and he needs to
be able to make his defence or answer to the
charge. Where he has counsel he needs to be able to do this through his counsel
any necessary instructions and by letting his counsel know what his
version of the facts is and, if necessary, telling the court
what it is. He need
not, of course, be conversant with court procedure and he need not have the
mental capacity to make an able defence;
but he must, I think, have sufficient
capacity to be able to decide what defence he will rely upon and to make his
defence and his
version of the facts known to the court and to his counsel, if
5.2.4 Although there is no express reference in
the Tasmanian provision to the requirement that the accused be able to give
provide an account of his or her version of the facts or provide
instructions to a legal practitioner, these requirements may be
implicit in the
requirement that the accused be able to make a
Similarly, there is
no requirement in the Tasmanian provision for a person to understand the
substantial effect of the evidence.
However, the requirement that a person
follow the course of the proceedings has been interpreted to mean that the
defendant is able
to follow the evidence: ‘The accused must have at least
a rudimentary understanding of the reception of evidence adverse to
him, whether
orally or by
5.2.5 The DPP’s guidelines also recognise the need for the court, in
determining a person’s fitness to stand trial, to
take account of any
accommodations that can be made to enable the person to participate in the trial
process. These include such
accommodations as ‘regular breaks, slowing the
pace of proceedings, monitoring the type and length of questioning of witnesses,
including the defendant, and allowing for adjournments where
necessary’.
approach reflects Australian decisional law that accepts the availability of
support as a relevant factor in the determination
of whether someone is unfit to
stand trial. In
Refshauge J
stated that:
Where steps can reasonably be taken to accommodate the difficulties of the
accused, including adjournments, ‘one-on-one’
assistance to follow
the proceedings, insistence on brief, clear questions to the accused if he or
she is examined on oath, an opportunity
for the accused to narrate his or her
version of events without interruption and the like, the implementation of these
the accused is not unfit to plead:
Kesavarajah v The Queen
(1994) 181 CLR 230
at 246; 111,
[2008] NSWDC 23
R v Tuigamala
[2007] NSWSC 493
However, the requirement to take into account accommodations or supports is
not yet recognised in Tasmanian legislation as a matter
relevant to an
assessment of fitness to stand trial.

other jurisdictions

legislation dealing with fitness to stand trial, and although there are some
variations, the
criteria are broadly similar to the

These factors focus

The test is not

However, it is noted that the position in South Australia provides an exception

Consolidation Act
(SA) s 269H provides that the accused is mentally unfit to
stand trial if his or her mental processes are so disordered or impaired

• understand, or respond

• exercise or give
instructions about the exercise of
procedural rights (such as, for example, the right to challenge jurors); or

or the course of the
proceedings.
The need for reform
5.3.1 Law reform bodies in several jurisdictions have considered the need to
reform fitness to stand trial tests and procedures, identifying
a number of
problems in their formulation and application. The ALRC has summarised the key
criticisms of the test as follows:
(1) The test, by focusing on intellectual ability, generally sets too high a
threshold for unfitness and is inconsistent with the
modern trial process. There
is undue emphasis on a person’s intellectual ability and too little focus
on a person’s decision-making
(2) The test is difficult to apply to individuals with mental illness because
the criteria were not designed for
(3) A defendant may not be unfit to stand trial even where the court takes
the view that he or she is incapable of making decisions
in his or her own
In relation to the last two matters, the following illustration is provided
by the Law Commission of England and Wales:
A defendant, A (who has paranoid schizophrenia), has a good understanding of
the trial process and understands the purpose of the
proceedings and roles
played by the different parties. A is also able to instruct his representative
and could give evidence. However,
as a result of his highly delusional state he
is convinced that if he pleads not guilty he will be destroyed by the devil. He
no insight into his condition and insists on pleading guilty to an assault
charge even though the evidence suggests that he may have
acted in lawful
self-defence. Under the current test the defendant would be likely to be found
fit to plead.
This example echoes the concerns of the VLRC that a person with a mental
illness ‘may have a factual understanding of the nature
of the trial.
However, their delusional beliefs may hinder their capacity to make decisions
concerning their trial, or to make such
decisions in an appropriate
5.3.2 Some criticisms of fitness to stand trial relate to the practical
operation of the current test with concerns being expressed
and subjective
application.
Criticisms in this regard have focused on the difficulties facing experts in
assessing an accused’s fitness to stand trial
resulting from the vagueness
of most legal formulations of the test and the opaque nature of the assessment
Freckelton contends
that ‘decision-making in this area is effectively delegated to clinical
evaluators making low visibility
and essentially unreviewed decisions pursuant
to a vague open-textured
conducted in other jurisdictions has identified similar concerns about the
assessment process undertaken by experts. In
England and Wales, empirical
research has found that there is considerable variability in clinicians’
assessments and a failure
by some clinicians to address all the criteria for
unfitness to stand trial.
Comparable findings have been made in Australia. For example, White et al have
found, in a study of assessment reports in New South
Wales, that ‘there
was significant variability in the practices and assessment methods of
‘large number of experts (67.6%) failing to address all six elements of
criteria, with some experts failing to address any of
5.3.3 Other commentators have criticised current
approaches to fitness on human rights grounds. They argue that fitness to stand
should be abolished in order to accord with the requirements of the
. This is because the adoption of a separate legal process for people
with disabilities is inherently
discriminatory.
In particular, it is argued that it is contrary to a number of
articles including Art 12 of the
which sets out
the right to ‘equal recognition before the law’ for people with
disability,
Art 13 which sets out ‘the right to access to justice on an equal basis
with others’,
14, which prohibits the deprivation of liberty on the basis of a
disability.
Accordingly, the United Nations Committee on the Rights of Persons with
Disabilities (‘UNCRPD’) ‘has directed countries
to abandon the
emphasis on identifying the point at which a person should be deemed unfit to
plead, and instead places an obligation
on States Parties to provide support to
the exercise of legal
In this context, Minkowitz asserts that the
Views all measures by which a person is treated unequally in legal
proceedings or in the adjudication of responsibility, including
the insanity
defence, unfitness to plead and incompetence to stand trial, as well as the
disposition to forensic psychiatric institutions,
as inherently suspect and
discriminatory based on disability. Therefore it is necessary to abolish these
measures in order to comply
with the equality and non-discrimination obligations
under various provisions of the
5.3.4 Others do not consider that the
requires the abolition of
the doctrine of fitness to stand
These commentators
instead argue that the uncertain yet potentially significant implications of the
for the current doctrine establish the need for its significant
For example,
Arstein-Kerslake et al have argued that:
One challenge is ensuring that there is a process that protects the rights of
persons with cognitive disabilities to legal capacity
on an equal basis with
others. This would, at a minimum, require that unfitness to plead regimes are
disability neutral on their
face and in their application. This will likely
require a dismantling of unfitness to plead regimes and the construction of a
that never denies legal capacities but instead provides support for the
individual to participate in a trial and a court structure
that ensures fairness
of the trial proceedings without depending on the accused’s particular
abilities to enforce those
On this basis, it is argued that the focus of the current inquiry on
identifying ‘“the line” over which someone
is incapable of
expressing a valid will or undertaking some function’ should be abandoned.
Instead the inquiry should ‘turn
to the support and accommodations
necessary to enable every person with a disability to function as a legal
Accordingly, Gooding argues that ‘the measure of a person’s
decision-making capability, or their legal capacity, [should]
no longer [be]
their inherent abilities, but rather the range and quality of supports and
accommodations around
This approach
concentrates on supports and accommodations that may be available ‘[to
legal processes accessible rather than creating a
“special” alternative measure’ for people with cognitive
difficulties.
5.3.5 While the need to identify supports and accommodations that may
facilitate participation in the trial process for as many people
as possible has
been recognised by various law reform bodies (as discussed at [
]), there is less agreement about the
approach that should be taken in ‘hard’ cases — that is, cases
where people
are not able to participate in the proceedings even with
In such cases,
Arstein-Kerslake et al write that:
A system should exist where the individual is not denied his or her legal
capacity to participate in the trial, but is instead provided
with an advocate
who has responsibility of interpreting the will and preference of the individual
to the best of that person’s
ability and to convey that to the court and
the relevant professionals involved in the trial process. Any such system must
be non-discriminatory
in order to encompass all individuals — those with
disabilities and those without disabilities — who are unable to
participate
in the trial process and also to protect the rights of persons with
disabilities to be free from discriminatory
These authors argue that reforms to the law should ensure that ‘[w]here
there is a question of whether the accused can stand
trial, the ensuing
procedure must not take place on the basis of disability alone and must not
serve to create a segregated criminal
justice process that only applies to
persons with cognitive
disabilities’.
contrast, law reform bodies have taken a different approach to the
interpretation of the
and have understood it as only requiring that
the test for unfitness should incorporate the use of supports and
accommodation.
This approach
still retains a threshold functional test to determine whether a person is able
to participate in the trial process
and operates to divert some individuals into
a special procedure.
5.3.6 In light of this commentary the TLRI sought feedback on the need to
reform the doctrine of fitness to plead in Tasmania. In
the Issues Paper, the
Institute asked the following questions:
Does the current test for unfitness to stand trial contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
continue to be a
suitable basis for determining unfitness to stand trial?
Are there any difficulties that arise from the current application of the
criteria contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
For example, are there difficulties with the test that give rise to a
subjective interpretation of the criteria by medical experts?
Is the current test under-inclusive and not able to appropriately reflect the
issues that arise for individuals with mental illness?
5.3.7 Only one submission received by the
Institute suggested that the current approach to fitness to stand trial in
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) does not
require reform. The DPP acknowledged that the test does involve subjective
interpretation, but did ‘not believe
changing the criteria would enhance
experts’ ability to assess an accused’s fitness to stand
trial’. The other
submissions received by the Institute supported reform.
Most supported fundamental change to the approach for determining fitness
stand trial. However, only minimal reform was proposed by ForensiClinic who
agreed that the current criteria are suitable with
one exception – the
ability to exercise the right to challenge jurors.
5.3.8 Based on the submissions received and its review of the expert
commentary, the Institute’s view is that there is a need
to reform the
current test for unfitness to stand trial contained in the
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
so that it provides a more suitable
basis for determining unfitness to stand trial.
Recommendation
2. There is a need to reform the current test for unfitness to stand trial
contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
Approaches to
5.4.1 Several options for reform were outlined in the Issues Paper including
the abolition of the doctrine or, alternatively, reformulating
and refocusing
the current test for determining fitness to stand trial. Broadly speaking, the
approaches to reformulating the current
test considered in the Issues Paper were
(1) give explicit statutory recognition to the fundamental principle that
underpins the operation of the test — the right to
a fair trial;
(2) retain the current test with its focus on the defendant’s cognitive
capacity as in the
approach but update and expand its
(3) change the focus of the test from cognitive capacity to the
defendant’s decision-making capacity and/or include in the decision-making
capacity test a requirement for
decision-making;
(4) specify the criteria to be applied in assessing an accused’s
decision-making capacity;
(5) require the courts, in assessing an accused’s decision-making
capacity, to have regard to the available supports and accommodations
enable them to exercise that capacity.
Abolition of the doctrine
5.4.2 As discussed at [
commentators have argued that fitness to stand trial should be abolished in
light of the obligations under the
Accordingly, the Issues Paper
sought feedback on this question.
Should the doctrine of fitness to stand trial be abolished in Tasmania?
If so, how should the law be changed to ensure that individuals who are not
able to participate in the trial process (even with the
provision of supports)
receive a fair trial?
5.4.3 Seven submissions to the Institute
addressed these questions either by directly answering them or implicitly by
proposing reforms
to the current
None of these
submissions supported the complete abolition of the doctrine of fitness to stand
trial in Tasmania. However, six of
the submissions identified problems with the
current test and set out proposals for its
5.4.4 Submissions that contained the strongest support for the fitness to
stand trial doctrine tended to focus on fairness principles
and the need to
ensure that people are genuinely able to participate in the criminal justice
process. This critique was particularly
concerned with the injustice of
subjecting people to a criminal process that they cannot properly engage in. For
example, ForensiClinic
wrote that:
fitness to stand trial is an important matter to establish for defendants
with major mental illness, and neurological/intellectual
impairments. It is
important for all defendants to have the opportunity to participate fairly in
proceedings that may have a significant
bearing on their lives.
Similarly, Walvisch wrote that:
the purpose of the criminal trial should be to call to account those who are
accused of perpetrating public wrongs. If the accused
lacks the capacities
necessary to understand the charge that has been brought against him or her, or
to account for his or her actions
(despite the provision of appropriate supports
...), then he or she cannot properly engage in the criminal trial and must not
held responsible.
5.4.5 Other submissions centred on the fair trial implications of fitness to
stand trial processes. For example, EOT observed that
fair trials may simply not
be possible in some cases, regardless of the efforts made to achieve
defendants’ participation.
After reviewing the literature, EOT noted
whilst improvements to the way in which people with disability are assisted
to exercise legal capacity may greatly minimise the circumstances
person is deemed unfit to plead, there [are] likely to remain occasions [when]
supports will not be enough to guarantee
a fair trial.
5.4.6 Walvisch also focussed on the fair trial implications of particular
procedures. Whilst he strongly supported the provision of
accommodations (see discussion at [4.4.50] – [4.4.65]), he argued that
suitable supports would not be sufficient to
allow participation in some cases.
He dismissed suggestions that human rights principles might be met in such cases
by giving legal

not be acceptable for the trial to proceed with the use of an advocate who has
the “responsibility of interpreting the
will and preference of the

Arstein-Kerslake et

While such an advocate would be of great assistance in many other legal fora,
they would be incapable of

the ways required for a criminal trial to properly proceed. This is a role that
only an individual
is able to perform for themselves (with appropriate
5.4.7 In contrast, Queensland Advocacy Incorporated (‘QAI’)
suggested that suitable supports would enable all people to
participate.
Nevertheless, QAI submitted that the current test for fitness to stand trial is
inherently discriminatory towards people
who have intellectual impairment but
did not recommend that the doctrine should be abolished. Instead QAI recommended
significantly reformed, stating that:
It is not controversial to suggest that even some lawyers, let alone those
with no legal training, struggle to understand trial processes,
yet the courts
do not even apply the tests for fitness to run-of-the-mill defendants. The
fitness tests are discriminatory because
they treat some people with disability
less favourably, placing an undue emphasis on a defendant’s cognitive
capacity to understand
specific aspects of the legal proceedings and trial
process. The tests place too little emphasis on a person’s decision-making
5.4.8 QAI criticised the justification for the fitness test in its current
form ‘to shield the person from harm, mischief, exploitation
abuse’ as offering protection at the expense of autonomy:
people with intellectual impairments are no less capable than anyone of
making decisions about their own lives, even if their cognitive
impairment means
that (all other things being equal) they may be less capable of understanding
the relevant criminal law and trial
process. Impairment on its own, however
defined, is not, nor should be a basis on which to deny anyone participation in
proceedings.
As noted, it was QAI’s view that appropriate legal supports and, if

5.4.9 After considering the submissions received, and noting the obligations
created by the
, the Institute’s view is that while there is a

of fitness to stand trial should not be abolished in Tasmania.
Recommendation
3. The doctrine of fitness to stand trial should not be abolished in
Reforming fitness to stand trial
5.4.10 Although the Institute does not consider that the doctrine of fitness
to stand trial should be abolished, it does recommend
that the basis of the test
should be fundamentally reformed. The options in this regard were set out at [
] and considered in detail in the
following sections.
Explicitly grounding the fitness test in the right to a fair trial
5.4.11 In its review of the options available to reform the
test, the NSWLRC recommended that the requirement for a fair trial should be
statutorily prescribed as the overarching foundational
principle to be applied
in its application.
requirement is also made explicit in the Crimes (Mental Impairment and Unfitness
to be Tried) Amendment Bill 2016
(Vic). A fair trial requirement could be
incorporated into the assessment of fitness to stand trial in the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas), and the Institute sought
Should the test of unfitness to stand trial contained in the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
include an overarching
requirement of a fair trial in the application of the criteria?
5.4.12 There was only limited support in the submissions received for the
statutory specification of the right to a fair trial as
the fundamental
principle governing determinations of fitness to plead. The DPP stated that such
a requirement should not be included
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
because there is no need to do so, ‘[t]he right to
a fair trial [being] implicit in the criminal law’. In contrast, Walvisch
stated that ‘courts should always be aiming to ensure that an
accused’s trial is fair’ and so it is appropriate
to make statutory
provision to this effect. However, he considered that it should not be the sole
touchstone for the application
of the test due to its vagueness. Instead, he
stated that ‘effective participation’ more clearly addresses the key
of ‘the accused’s ability to account for his or her actions in
the trial process and will provide better guidance to
expert witnesses and
decision-makers’.
5.4.13 The Institute is of the view that while the right to a fair trial
implicitly governs all criminal proceedings in Tasmania,
there is value in
making explicit legislative provision to this effect in the context of the
fitness to stand trial doctrine. Doing
so serves declaratory and educative
purposes for the general community which, unlike the legal profession, may not
be aware of its
implicit application. It will also promote the explicit and
consistent consideration of this principle in this context. Further,
it may help
to ensure that other concerns, like case management considerations or the
assumed best interests of the accused are not
substituted for or allowed to
become a proxy for the right to a fair trial. Accordingly, the Institute
recommends that an overarching
requirement for a fair trial be included in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
Recommendation
4. The right to a fair trial should be given legislative recognition as the
fundamental principle governing the determination of whether
a defendant is fit
to stand trial within the meaning of the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
Retention of the current Presser test but with up-dated criteria
5.4.14 One approach to reform has been simply to review and update the
current Presser criteria. This involves only modest reform
because it does not
require any alteration of the fundamental nature and basis of the test. This is
the approach recommended by the
NSWLRC and the VLRC.
5.4.15 In the Issues Paper, the Institute sought feedback on whether any
changes need to be made to the current criteria in the Presser

the current criteria but made an exception in relation to the requirement that

received supported this more

following sections where the adoption of a decision-making model, whether that

are considered.
Recommendation
5. The TLRI does not support the minimalist reform approach of simply
up-dating the criteria for the current cognitive capacity fitness
test) without making additional reforms to that test. More
profound reform is needed.
Decision making capacity/effective participation
5.4.18 Possibly the most significant reform to the test in
that has been
advocated by commentators and endorsed by respondents to the Issues Paper is to
move the focus of the test from cognitive
capacity to decision-making capacity
as a means of assessing effective participation in the trial. A prominent
proponent of this
reform, the Law Commission of England and Wales recommended
replacing the
test (the English equivalent of the
test) with a test of decision-making capacity that focuses on an assessment
of the defendant’s capacity to participate effectively
Its view was that the
lack of emphasis on decision-making capacity in the test for fitness is
difficult to reconcile with the requirements
of Art 6 of the
Convention on Human Rights
, which stipulate that an accused be able to
participate effectively in his or her
The Law Commission
also considered that a test of decision-making capacity and effective
participation would modernise the current
approach and replace it with one that
better reflects ‘current medical understanding and legal practice in the
field of incapacity.’
The Law Commission’s recommended model comprises a ‘process’
or ‘functional’ approach, focusing on
the decision-making processes
of the accused, rather than an ‘outcome’ or ‘status’
approach, focusing on
the rationality of the
5.4.19 The decision-making test was also said to
be consistent with the test of decision-making capacity in the
Capacity Act 2005
The Law Commission also relied on the approach in this Act when recommending
that an accused should be found to lack capacity only
if he or she is unable:
• to understand the information relevant to the decisions that he or
she will have to make in the course of his or her trial;
• to retain that information; to use or weigh that information as part
of the decision-making process; or
• to communicate his or her
5.4.20 This is also the ALRC’s preferred approach. Accordingly, the
ALRC has recommended that the
Crimes Act 1914
(Cth) be amended to provide
that a person is unfit to stand trial if he or she cannot be supported to:
(a) understand the information relevant to the decisions that they will have
to make in the course of the proceedings;
(b) retain that information to the extent necessary to make decisions in the
course of the proceedings;
(c) use or weigh that information as part of the process of making decisions;
(d) communicate the decisions in some
The ALRC reasoned that making decision-making ability, in the context of the
particular criminal proceedings, central to the question
of fitness is mandated
5.4.21 Concerns about a focus on decision-making capacity and effective
participation centre principally on the possibility that it
may introduce too
much subjectivity into the assessment process, with concomitant problems for
expert assessments.
was endorsed in the review conducted by the Attorney General’s Department
in Western Australia.
NSWLRC further suggested that an ‘effective participation’ approach
may not only create uncertainty but also be over-inclusive
and so result in too
many people being found to be unfit. The NSWLRC, therefore, did not recommend
adoption of this model.
5.4.22 It has been argued that the assessment of an accused’s capacity
based on a ‘functional’ approach is problematic
because it violates the right to equal recognition before the law
for people with disabilities.
Despite these concerns, the ALRC maintains that ‘it is not practicable to
do away completely with some functional tests of
ability that have consequences
for participation in legal
processes’.
the ALRC stated that:
The integrity of a criminal trial (and, arguably, the criminal law itself)
would be prejudiced if the defendant does not have the
ability to understand and
participate in a meaningful way. It may also breach the person’s human
rights by denying them a fair
trial, implicating arts 12 and 13 of the
Instead, the ALRC recommended a test that takes into account an
accused’s fitness to stand trial in the context of the supports
accommodations that may be made available to maximise his or her
decision-making/participation capacity.
5.4.23 Based on this commentary, the Institute sought feedback on whether a
decision-making approach should be adopted in Tasmania
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
Should the test of unfitness to stand trial contained in the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
include consideration of an
accused person’s decision-making capacity and/or ability for effective
participation?
5.4.24 While respondents to the Issues Paper expressed a range of views about
possible changes to the fitness to stand trial test,
predominantly, submissions
supported replacing the current test which focuses on defendants’
intellectual ability with a test
that requires assessment of their
decision-making capacity. QAI justified this proposition on the basis that the
current test unfairly
discriminates against people with intellectual
disabilities because it focuses on ‘their ability to understand rather
their ability to function or to do something’. QAI submitted that the
test for fitness to stand trial ‘should not be
a de facto test of a
person’s cognitive capacity: that is, it should not be about a
person’s intellectual ability to
comprehend specific aspects of legal
proceedings and trial process’, because this contravenes obligations under
Instead, QAI recommended that the test ‘should focus on a
person’s decision-making ability, with support, and in the context
particular criminal proceedings which they face’. Accordingly, QAI
supported the ALRC approach (set out above) with
its focus on decision-making
5.4.25 Walvisch similarly supported the ALRC and the Law Commission of
England and Wales recommendations that the fitness assessment
should focus
(1) The accused’s capacity to
the relevant trial
(2) The accused’s capacity to
make decisions
relevant to his or
her trial; and
(3) The accused’s capacity to
communicate
those decisions to the
court or his or her lawyer.
Walvisch favoured this model because it more closely aligns with the
rationale for the fitness to stand trial test itself:
While the ability to understand trial processes is important, the accused
must also be able to account for his or her actions. This
requires more than
mere intellectual understanding. It also requires the accused to be able to make
decisions relevant to the trial,
and to be able to effectively communicate those
The Australian Lawyers Alliance (‘ALA’) and EOT also expressed
support for the ALRC decision-making model.
5.4.26 The Institute acknowledges that there are
concerns that adoption of a decision-making test of fitness to stand trial may
in a greater degree of subjectivity in the application of the test with
associated problems for expert assessment. Both the DPP and
ForensiClinic
expressed objection to the decision-making model on this basis, arguing that it
constitutes too broad and vague a basis
of assessment and that it may therefore
increase the number of people found to be unfit.
5.4.27 However, on balance, the Institute considers that the current
cognitive capacity
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) should be replaced by a test that focuses on
defendants’ decision-making capacity. This reflects a decisive shift from
the current emphasis on ‘mere factual understanding’ (passive
participation) to more active participation in the trial
through an assessment
of defendants’ decision-making
Further, the
determination of fitness to stand trial based on decision-making capacity
reflects the approach to capacity in the
Mental Health Act 2013
which is also based on a person’s decision-making
Accordingly, the
Institute recommends that that the central focus of the test in
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) should be on the
defendant’s decision-making capacity as a means of determining whether he
or she can effectively participate
in the trial. The Institute considers that
concerns about the breadth, subjectivity and potentially inconsistent
application of a
decision-making test can be addressed by legislating guideline
criteria for its application. The Institute also considers that concerns
this test may increase unfitness findings may be ameliorated by requiring that
it be applied in light of the supports and accommodations
that can be provided
to defendants to enable them to exercise their decision-making ability and
participate effectively in trials.
Both these matters are considered in detail
below. As noted above, all these reforms work together and therefore should be
implemented
Recommendation
6. The test for assessing fitness to stand trial in
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999

on the accused’s decision-making capacity as means of determining whether

5.4.34 Walvisch suggested that

their choice to make unwise decisions, and so it is

Walvisch stressed that ‘it is essential that people not be

decisions that would be made by expert witnesses, the judge or the jury’.
For these reasons, Walvisch submitted that
‘the notion of rationality
should be incorporated into the test by requiring the accused to be able to make
decisions that
are “reasons-responsive”’. This was explained
as follows:
this is not simply about being unable to recognise a sufficient reason for
action on one particular occasion. It is about failing
to exhibit a pattern of
reason-recognition that is understandable to those who are judging the
agent’s responsibility, and
that is grounded in reality. According to this
view, a person will not be fit to stand trial if he or she is unable to
how reasons fit together, to see why one reason is stronger than
another, or to understand how the acceptance of one reason as sufficient
that a stronger reason must also be sufficient. However, he or she will not be
unfit simply because poor or seemingly irrational
decisions have been made.
This would allow a finding of unfitness to be made where a person’s
delusional beliefs may mean that they do not have the capacity
to make decisions
their trial.

It is often a

a thought process is rational or not.
Rational thinking can be affected by clinical and non-clinical factors.
Non-clinical factors
may include personality (e.g., antagonism, oppositional),
motivation, and naivety. Non-clinical factors would not form a reasonable
for unfitness. Clinical factors may include psychosis and dementia.
Additionally, a rational decision from one cultural background

recommendation accords closely with the approach ultimately recommended in this

Criteria to be applied in assessing defendants’ decision-making
5.4.38 Decision-making capacity will necessarily fall to be assessed
according to prescribed criteria in order to ensure that those
assessments are
consistently carried out. Prescription of applicable criteria will also give
content to the decision-making test
and reduce potential subjectivity in its
application by providing guidance in this regard. This begs the question, what
these criteria
should be. At the core of the recent reviews of the test of
fitness to stand trial is a desire to ensure that the criteria governing
assessment of decision-making capacity reflect a need for an accused to be
‘able to make “true choices” concerning
the crucial decisions
in the trial that are not substantially prejudiced by their mental
condition’.
Commission of England and Wales identified the need to ensure that there is
meaningful participation in the trial process
stating that it would be ‘an
abuse of the process of the law to subject someone to a trial when he or she is
unable to play
any real part in that
Similarly, the
VLRC’s view is that fairness requires that accused only be subject to the
trial process where they are able
to make crucial decisions relevant to their
Accordingly, the VLRC
considered that the most appropriate way to ensure that the test takes this into
account is to:
• evaluate the current criteria for unfitness to stand trial against
the crucial decisions an accused should be expected to
• supplement the criteria with a requirement that the accused be able
to make these specific decisions, where the current criteria
do not cover
Other jurisdictions have also examined the criteria used to assess fitness to
stand trial and considered whether they are appropriate
in the modern trial
5.4.39 Appendix 4 contains a comparison of the
criteria that are used in other jurisdictions, as well as new criteria that have
recommended by law reform bodies.
5.4.40 Criteria that have been identified as essential that are not
explicitly contained in the Tasmanian legislation are:
• The accused’s ability to understand the significance of
entering a plea.
• The accused’s ability to communicate
instructions to his or her legal practitioner.
• The accused’s ability to decide whether to give evidence to
support his/her case, and if he or she wishes to give evidence,
his/her ability
5.4.41 In recommending reform of the basis of the fitness test, the Law
Commission of England and Wales also reviewed the guideline
criteria to be
applied in its application. This is also the approach adopted in Scotland. The
Law Commission of England and Wales
specified a number of abilities as relevant
to the assessment of the defendant’s decision-making capacity and
therefore capacity
for effective participation in the trial. These included the
ability to:
• understand the charges (what the charges mean, their nature and the
evidence on which the prosecution rely to establish the
• understand the trial process and the consequences of being convicted;
• give instructions to a legal representative;
• follow proceedings in courts;
• give evidence;
• make a decision about whether to plead guilty or not guilty;
• make a decision about whether to give evidence;
• make a decision about whether to elect Crown Court trial; and
• make any other decision that might need to be made by the defendant
in connection with the trial.
This approach was also endorsed by the Northern Ireland Law
Commission.
5.4.42 The requirements for effective participation are set out in
legislation in Scotland. Following recommendations of the Scottish
Commission,
Criminal Procedure (Scotland) Act 1995
was amended to provide that
‘a person is unfit for trial if it is established ... that the person is
incapable, by reason of
a mental or physical condition, of participating
effectively in a
In making the
determination of fitness, the court is to have regard to the following factors:
(a) the ability of the person to — understand the nature of the charge;
understand the requirement to tender a plea to the charge
and the effect of such
a plea; understand the purpose of, and follow the course of, the trial;
understand the evidence that may be
given against the person; instruct and
otherwise communicate with the person’s legal representative; and
(b) any other factor which the court considers
5.4.43 Based on these different approaches, the Institute sought feedback on
the current criteria contained in the

5.4.44 Respondents who supported the replacement of the current cognitive
