punishment.
5.4.45 The ALA proposed the following criteria relating to the abilities of
the accused, as matters that should be included in the
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
These criteria were the
• to understand the charges (what the charges mean, their nature and
the evidence on which the prosecution relies to establish
• to understand the trial process and the consequences of being
• to give instructions to a legal representative
• to follow proceedings in courts
• to give evidence
• to make a decision about whether to plead guilty or not guilty
• to make a decision about whether to give evidence
• to make a decision about whether to elect a trial in the Criminal
Court and, if so, by jury or by judge
or any other decision
that might need to be made by the defendant in connection with the trial.
5.4.46 Because the Institute acknowledges that there are concerns about the
broad and potentially imprecise nature of a decision-making
capacity test, and
considers that this could be addressed by identifying the matters about which a
person will need to make decisions,
it recommends that these matters be
legislatively specified as criteria to guide the application of the test. This
was the approach
recommended by the Law Commission of England and Wales, the
Northern Ireland Law Commission and that has been legislated in
It is also the
approach suggested by Walvisch and the ALA.
5.4.47 After reviewing the submissions received and approaches in other
legislatively prescribed to guide assessments of defendants’
decision-making capacity. They should consist of critical
understandings and
abilities that defendants must have in order to make key decisions during
trials. These criteria should include:
• an understanding of the actual significance and effect of entering a
• the ability to communicate
instructions to his or
her legal practitioner
• the ability to decide whether to give evidence to support his/her
case and his/her ability to give evidence (that is, the
ability to give a
coherent account in his/her defence and to answer questions put to him/her)
• an understanding of the meaning and consequences of a finding of
guilt including that he/she may be punished and in jeopardy
of imprisonment, if
so advised that they are at risk of such a penalty
• an understanding of the nature of the offence(s) with which he/she is
• the ability to understand the nature of the proceedings as a process
to determine whether it has been proved that he/she has
committed the offence(s)
• the ability to understand and follow the substance of the evidence
that may be given at trial
• the ability to understand the course of the proceedings and what is
going on in a general sense at each stage of the trial
5.4.48 While these matters contain features that are similar to the current
factors courts use to determine fitness under the
approach, they
are used for a different purpose. Their purpose is to throw light on
defendants’ ability to participate in their
trials by making decisions, by
being able to account for their actions and communicate their decisions. The
inclusion of these criteria
should be understood as providing guidance in the
application of the decision-making test, as giving it content and inhibiting
subjectivity
in its application. In other words, these criteria provide a basis
for the assessment of decision-making capacity.
5.4.49 There may be concern about the possible increase in the number of
people found to be unfit under the new test given that it
requires more than a
capacity for passive involvement through understanding of key features of the
trial and also requires an ability
to participate actively in the trial process
by making decisions. However, it is important to recognise that this revised
not sit alone but is part of three interrelated changes and the
proposed revised test is to be understood alongside a recommendation
implement a legislative requirement to take into account how a person can be
supported to be able to exercise their decision-making
capacity. This involves
the need to assess available accommodations and supports that may create
conditions that optimise an accused’s
decision-making capacity. Combined,
this approach reflects the justification for the requirement that an individual
is fit to stand
trial and reflects the need to ensure that a person is able to
have a fair trial and is able to make the link between their conduct
allegedly is a crime) and the court process and punishment.
Recommendation
8. Criteria should be legislatively prescribed to guide assessments of
defendants’ decision-making capacity. These criteria
should consist of
critical understandings and abilities that defendants must have in order to make
key decisions during trials including:
• an understanding of the actual significance and effect of entering
• the ability to communicate
instructions to his or
her legal practitioner
• the ability to decide whether to give evidence to support his/her
case and his/her ability to give evidence (that is, the
ability to give a
coherent account in his/her defence and to answer questions put to
• an understanding of the meaning and consequences of a finding of
guilt including that he/she may be punished and in jeopardy
of imprisonment, if
so advised that they are at risk of such a penalty
• an understanding of the nature of the offence(s) with which he/she
• the ability to understand the nature of the proceedings as a
process to determine whether it has been proved that he/she has
committed the
offence(s) charged
• the ability to understand and follow the substance of the evidence
that may be given at trial
• the ability to understand the course of the proceedings and what is
going on in a general sense at each stage of the trial
Supports and accommodations
5.4.50 As indicated, the availability of supports and accommodations has been
identified by the Institute as a key aspect of its reform
proposals. Although
case law recognises the relevance of accommodations and supports, it has been
contended that ‘the application
of such accommodations is arguably ad
hoc’ and that a ‘more formalised, systematic application of
procedural accommodations
... could be achieved through the training of judicial
officers and greater statutory protections for support persons to assist accused
persons at risk of being deemed unfit to
Accordingly,
Gooding et al criticise the current test for failing to ‘incorporate a
requirement to consider whether support
or assistance could help the accused to
optimise fitness to stand
5.4.51 The need for greater emphasis on the supports and accommodations that
could be made available to an accused to facilitate fitness
for trial has also
been recognised by various law reform bodies. For example, the Victorian Law
Reform Committee has stated that
the test ‘sets a low threshold for
determining the fitness of an accused with an intellectual disability or
cognitive impairment’.
It indicated that it had received evidence to suggest that ‘the provision
of court support services could provide a mechanism
for overcoming barriers that
[such] a person ... may experience when interacting with the courts and may
therefore minimise the potential
for findings of unfitness to be made against
recommended that there be a reformulation of the test ‘to focus on
whether, and to what extent, a person can be supported
to play their role in the
justice system, rather than on whether they have capacity to play such a role at
Similarly, the
VLRC indicated that ‘the importance of support measures in the unfitness
to stand trial process was one of the
strongest themes to come out of the
Commission’s
In England and
Wales, the Law Commission expressed the view that the test should take
‘into account assistance available to
the defendant’ and that
‘a finding of lack of capacity should only be made as a last
It also noted
that this reflected the current law as applied by the courts but considered that
support should be explicitly recognised
The NSWLRC also
recommended that in determining whether a person is unfit for trial, the court
must consider where modifications
to the trial process can be made or assistance
provided to facilitate the person’s understanding and effective
participation.
The Western
Australian review conducted by the Department of the Attorney General
recommended that the concept of ‘fitness
with support’ should be
specifically included as a factor in the determination of an accused’s
fitness to stand trial.
‘Fitness with support’ refers to ‘modifications to court
processes that are made to assist an individual to participate
processes’.
appears to be a wide consensus in favour of requiring courts to consider the
availability of supports and accommodations
to maximise defendants’
opportunity to participate in the criminal justice process. Explicitly
incorporating this requirement
into the test also reduces the likelihood for
inconsistency in its application.
5.4.52 Despite widespread support for this approach, concerns have been
expressed that to use special measures to allow an accused
to become fit for
trial is not necessarily in his/her best interests. In the view of the Northern
Ireland Law Reform Commission,
it may be inherently unfair for special measures
to be used to ‘make’ an accused fit for
This is because:
The individual has not changed and might be better served by the disposals
which are available following a determination of unfitness
rather than following
a criminal trial, where the focus is on sentencing and rehabilitation rather
than medical treatment and
Accordingly, its view was that special measures should only be considered
once the issue of unfitness to plead has been considered
and a finding of
fitness determined.
5.4.53 In Tasmania, as indicated, the issue of supports and accommodations
for people with communication needs involved in the criminal
justice system was
the subject of recent review by the TLRI. The TLRI examined the feasibility of
instituting a communication assistant/intermediary
scheme in Tasmania, and its
principal recommendation was that such a scheme should be established in
Tasmania with a view to enabling
‘people with communication needs to
participate to the best of their ability in the criminal justice
The proposed
scheme would involve expertly trained intermediaries/communication assistants
who have a range of functions including
advisory and interpretive roles as well
as the power to intervene in inappropriate questioning and to suggest how such
questioning
should be altered. The intermediaries/communication assistants would
be able to provide advice to courts, legal practitioners and
the police and
communication assistance to people with communication needs who are involved in
the criminal justice process. This
will facilitate communication with the police
and legal practitioners and will support individuals with communication needs
court hearings and
Accordingly, the
creation of such a scheme is likely to reduce the need to hold fitness hearings,
and/or reduce the number of people
found unfit to stand trial and enhance the
human rights of people with communication needs in their interactions with the
justice system. The TLRI has also made recommendations in relation to
the importance of appropriate decision-making supports as a
means to facilitate
decision-making to maximise a person’s autotomy and promote a will,
preference and rights approach to decision-making
for people requiring
5.4.54 In addition to the benefits offered by an intermediary scheme in
facilitating participation in the criminal process, other
means available to
increase the level of support for people with intellectual disabilities and/or
cognitive impairments have been
identified as
• the provision of a formal education program that may facilitate
fitness such as the provision of education about the nature
of the trial
process, the nature of the criminal charge and the role of court staff;
• court familiarisation processes;
• the modification of court procedures to suit people with intellectual
disabilities or cognitive impairments, such as shorter
sessions, additional
breaks, reduced formalities, allowing the giving of evidence via videolink;
• improved communications methods, such as use of visual aids and clear
These measures may also allow individuals with intellectual disabilities
and/or cognitive impairments to participate more effectively
in an ordinary
trial and support their fitness to stand trial. The provision of procedural
accommodations to facilitate a person’s
access to justice accords with the
requirements of the
according to Gooding and O’Mahoney, ‘circumvent the need for any
assessment of fitness for trial in the first
5.4.55 On this basis, in the Issues Paper, the
Institute suggested that it may be desirable to incorporate the relevance of
and accommodations specifically into the statutory test, including the
use of expert intermediaries/communication assistants. Further,
judicial officers to make an assessment about fitness taking into account

that should be used.
5.4.56 Accordingly, feedback was sought from stakeholders and members of the
Tasmanian community on the need to include the availability

Criminal Justice (Mental

Should the availability of accommodations and support measures, including the

(Tas) as a factor that needs to be taken into account when determining
unfitness to stand trial?
5.4.57 There was clear support in the submissions
for the legislative recognition of supports and accommodations. While not
supporting reform of the current fitness criteria, the DPP was not
opposed to legislative amendment that requires consideration of
any special
measures that might be implemented to support an accused to meet fitness to
stand trial criteria. The DPP explained that
such a requirement would, in any
event, simply reflect current practice. However, the DPP stated that:
Any such amendment would need to be considered in light of what special
measures are reasonably and practically available. This would
require extensive
consultation with the court and other stakeholders. Intermediaries or
communication assistance, as proposed by
the TLRI in Final Report 23, may enable
a person to be supported to such an extent that they are fit to stand trial.
5.4.58 ForensiClinic also agreed that ‘the potential use of an
intermediary/communication assistant is a helpful concept, particularly
those on the borderline threshold of fitness, or with specific communication
impediments’. Support for legislative recognition
of the importance of
intermediaries/communication assistants in facilitating fitness to stand trial
was found in several other submissions
received. O’Connell wrote that:
I am of the view that an intermediary could assist defendants with mental
illness or mental impairment. There often exists a tension
between the law and
psychiatry and a communication expert could assist all parties in maximising the
defendants participation in
the court process. There are a number of defendants
that are charged with matters, deemed fit to stand trial but the lawyer is
dissatisfied
with the quality of instructions provided. An intermediary would
assist in these matters.
5.4.59 Walvisch recognised the value of using intermediary/communication
assistants to facilitate participation given that ‘[i]t
community’s interests that people who are accused of committing crimes be
called to account for those crimes’.
He agreed with Gooding’s
argument that ‘the measure of a person’s decision-making capability,
or their legal capacity,
[should] no longer [be] their inherent abilities, but
rather the range and quality of supports and accommodations around
Walvisch placed some caveats on this proposition:
(1) ‘in implementing such measures, great care should be taken to
ensure that the accused person’s impairment is
properly taken
into account in the sentencing process’;
(2) ‘it is essential that the decision-maker be certain that such
measures will actually be available to the accused throughout
the trial, and
that there is evidence to support the fact that they will assist the accused to
reach the requisite standards’;
(3) that there is ‘provision to revise the fitness assessment if the
support becomes unavailable, or it becomes apparent that
it is not assisting the
accused to meet the requisite standards’.
5.4.60 EOT stressed the role that supports and accommodations, including
communication assistance could play in reducing the number
of people found unfit
to stand trial. This reflects EOT’s view that the rights of people with
disability are not always upheld
in practice. EOT has undertaken significant
work with stakeholders to develop the
Disability Justice Plan for Tasmania
. In this regard EOT noted that:
A key action identified in the DJPT [was] the need to ‘improve the
justice system’s capacity to recognise an individual’s
disability at
the earliest opportunity and make available appropriate supports and adjustments
to enable them to participate in those
services on an equitable basis with
EOT stated that ‘[c]apacity can be removed in circumstances where it is
not clear that a rigorous assessment has been made or
where the capacity for
decision making ... with the assistance of aids and supports has not been
5.4.61 Accordingly, in light of its research and the submissions received,
the Institute recommends that there should be legislative
recognition of the
need to take into account support measures and accommodations that are available
to defendants with a view to
optimising their fitness to stand trial, in
circumstances where they may otherwise be unfit.
Greater use of
special measures to support an accused’s fitness to stand trial is
recommended on the basis that the optimum
approach is for defendants to proceed
through the normal trial process, wherever this can be fairly
This is both
beneficial to defendants and in the public
Accordingly, the
law should aim to reduce the number of people who take part in special hearings
so that a finding of fitness is
only a last resort where a person cannot be
supported to participate in the ordinary trial process. This approach is
consistent with
the obligations that arise under the
Further, as
recognised by the ALRC, explicitly incorporating the concept of supports and
accommodation into the test for fitness
‘reflects the National
Decision-Making Principles in that any assessment of decision-making ability
should be considered in
the context of available
5.4.62 These recommendations are also aligned to the previous recommendations
of the Institute contained in its Final Report,
Facilitating Equal Access to
Justice: An Intermediary/Communication Assistant Scheme for
5.4.63 In the Institute’s view, a focus on supports and accommodations
combined with a decision-making approach aligns strongly
with the purpose of the
test and identifies the factors that will allow defendants to participate in a
meaningful way in their own
trial. A decision-making model for assessing fitness
to stand trial also aligns closely with human rights considerations. As Walvisch
observes, ‘[t]he key issue in this regard is
whether the
accused suffered from a particular type of mental or physical disability (which
would clearly be discriminatory), but
whether the accused is able to account for
his or her actions if provided with the appropriate supports’.
5.4.64 This approach to assessing fitness to stand trial for an accused is
also consistent with the approach contained in the
Evidence Act 2001
, which deals with the competence of witnesses to give evidence.
There is a presumption of competence, set out in
, ‘unless the
contrary is proved’.
further provides:
(1) A person is not competent to give evidence about a fact if, for any
reason (including a mental, intellectual or physical
disability)
(a) the person does not have the capacity to understand a question about the
(b) the person does not have the capacity to give an answer that can be
understood to a question about the fact –
and that incapacity cannot be overcome
(8) For the purpose of determining a question arising under this section, the
court may inform itself as it thinks fit, including
by obtaining information
from a person who has relevant specialised knowledge based on the person’s
training, study or experience.
Clearly, under
, a witness will only be incompetent if a lack of capacity
cannot be overcome. The Institute recommends that this approach be adopted
relation to fitness to plead and an accused’s capacity to participate in
trials. In common with the recommendation of the
Law Commission of England and
Wales, the Institute recommends findings of lack of capacity, and therefore
unfitness to stand trial,
should only be made as a last resort.
5.4.65 The Institute recognises that the court’s assessment of the
supports and accommodations that may assist an accused to
be fit for trial must
be based on the supports/accommodations that are actually available and that
will be effective to assist the
accused. This should be the subject of expert
reports provided to the court in relation to its consideration of the fitness to
trial test. Again, this is consistent with the
Evidence Act 2001
Recommendations
9. There should be legislative recognition in the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
of the need for courts to take into account
support measures and accommodations that are available to an individual with a
optimising an accused’s fitness to stand trial in circumstances
where they may otherwise be unfit. Further, the legislation
should specify that
a finding of lack of capacity is only to be made as a last resort, that is, in
the event that support measures
have been exhausted.
10. The court’s assessment of the supports and accommodations that may
assist an accused to be fit for trial must be based on
supports/accommodations that are actually available and would be effective to
assist the accused. This should be the subject
of expert reports in relation to
the court’s determination of the fitness to stand trial test.
Pleas of guilty
5.4.66 A related issue is whether the test for determining fitness should be
adapted in cases where an accused wishes to plead guilty.
This question has been
considered in several reviews of the law on the basis that a person may be able
to understand the nature of
the charge and may be able to enter a plea to the
charge but may not be able to understand more complex aspects of the trial
Under the current
law, such a person is unfit to stand trial and is not able to enter a plea of
guilty but instead is subject to
the special hearing
procedures.
anecdotal evidence is that such people do plead guilty rather than rely on the
fitness to stand trial provisions, if their
legal representative is satisfied of
their capacity to plead and participate in the sentencing hearing, in order to
avoid the onerous
consequences of a finding of
5.4.67 There have been differing views expressed in relation to the
appropriateness of adapting the test if an accused wishes to plead
Arguments in favour of a separate test
5.4.68 The following arguments have been advanced in favour of a separate
• The minimum standards to enter a plea are less onerous than those
required to stand trial.
• It would enable defendants who are able to enter a plea to
participate in the usual criminal
• Where an accused is able to make a particular decision, this should
be given effect as far as
This accords with
human rights
considerations.
• It allows an accused to ‘get on with treatment’ without
concerns in relation to fitness procedures and the prospect
of proceedings being
resumed on recovery.
• If an accused can plead guilty, this is better for victims and
• The law requires flexibility to take account of differing levels of
Arguments against a separate test
5.4.69 The following arguments have been advanced against a separate test:
• The concerns about capacity are the same for both fitness to plead
and fitness to stand trial, and so the test should be the
• A separate test would be an unnecessary complication and would be
• Expert assessment can already take into account the differing demands
depending on the nature of the
proceedings.
• Concerns that an accused may not truly understand the nature of the
guilty plea and that difficulties with understanding the
trial process may mean
that they see no option other than to plead
• Concerns that such a test may undermine the legitimacy of the
criminal justice system if it allows ‘individuals who
are otherwise unable
to participate effectively in proceedings to plead guilty, especially to serious
5.4.70 The NSWLRC made no recommendation on this issue in view of the
strength of opposition from key
stakeholders.
The VLRC and
the Law Commission of England and Wales supported varying the test for pleas of
The VLRC expressed
the view that adequate safeguards should be built into the model so that the
revised test for guilty pleas would
only apply if a defendant was legally
represented.
The criteria
that were identified as relevant for fitness to enter a plea were that the
• Understands the nature of the charge.
• Understands the nature of the hearing if they pleaded not guilty.
• Understands the significance of entering a plea of guilty and its
consequences.
• Meaningfully communicates to his or her legal practitioner his/her
decision to plead guilty.
• Follows the course of the plea and sentencing hearing that will
follow a plea of guilty.
It is noted that the test for capacity recommended by the ALRC does not make
a distinction between the test to enter a guilty plea
and the test to stand
trial but does allow the differing decisions that a defendant will need to make
to be taken into account in
the assessment of
5.4.71 In the Issues Paper, the Institute sought feedback on whether there
should be a separate test in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas) to determine whether a person is fit to enter a plea and if so,
the requirements of that test.
Should there be a separate test in the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) to determine whether a person is fit to enter a
If so, what should be the requirements of the test?
5.4.72 The Institute received two submissions that addressed these questions.
The DPP clearly supported a separate test based on the
test proposed by the
VLRC. The DPP wrote that:
I would support the inclusion of a separate test in the
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act
to determine whether a person is fit to enter a
plea. There is a difference between being fit to stand trial and being fit to
guilty. The nature and course of the proceedings is simpler in uncontested
matters, and a person may be able to understand and follow
the nature and course
of proceedings on a plea of guilty but not a trial. It is in the interests of
justice for victims, witnesses
and accused that matters are dealt with as
quickly as possible.
In circumstances where a person has impaired mental functioning, this can be
readily and appropriately managed in the sentencing process.
The test proposed by the VLRC, as set out in 4.4.35 of the [issues] paper
seems appropriate.
5.4.73 While Walvisch recognised the difference between being fit to stand
trial and being fit to enter a guilty plea, he did not
consider that it was
essential to have two separate tests if there was one test with sufficient
flexibility to accommodate both situations.
In Walvisch’s view, the
essential matter is the ‘law should be able to differentiate between
fitness to enter a plea
and fitness to stand trial’ and ‘is clearly
able to differentiate between these two situations’. In Walvisch’s
view, it was necessary to take care:
to ensure that the requisite capacities are not too strictly circumscribed in
any test that applies to fitness to plead, and that
the accused properly
understands the nature of the guilty plea. In such cases, the accused must still
be able to:
• Understand the nature of the charge;
• Understand that he or she has a right to contest that charge, and
what that would generally involve (e.g., a trial involving
witnesses giving
evidence, determined by a jury/magistrate);
• Understand the nature and strength of the evidence that may be given
in support of the prosecution, and the nature and strength
of any legal defences
open to him or her;
• Understand the meaning of conviction and punishment, and the nature
of the sanctions which may be imposed;
• Enter a plea to the charge;
• Make reasons-responsive decisions about the relevant issues; and
• Give meaningful instructions to his or her legal practitioner.
Each of these matters should be clearly spelt out in any separate test.
5.4.74 The focus of the Institute’s recommendations are to maximise an
individual’s participation in regular criminal
justice process wherever
possible and a separate test that would allow for a person’s fitness to
enter a guilty plea to be
assessed would facilitate this. Further, as noted by
the VLRC and NSWLRC, there is anecdotal evidence that individuals who may be
unfit to stand trial may enter guilty pleas to avoid the onerous consequences of
a finding of unfitness in circumstances where their
legal representative was
satisfied of their capacity to
While it is unclear
the extent to which this occurs in Tasmania, it is reasonable to assume that it
does happen on occasion. A legislative
provision that outlines the nature of the
matters to be addressed and also the need to consider supports and
accommodations that
may facilitate fitness to plead would formalise this process
and make the relevant factors more transparent. Accordingly, the Institute
recommends that a specific test be included in the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) to set out the circumstances in which a person is
unfit to enter a plea. This test should largely reflect the test for fitness
stand trial (and so, focus on the accused’s decision-making ability) but
it should also incorporate changes that reflect
the differences between a guilty
plea and a trial.
5.4.75 The Institute’s view is that the following factors are relevant
to a person’s fitness to enter a guilty plea:
• The accused’s understanding of the nature of the offence with
which he or she is charged.
• The accused’s understanding that he or she has a right to
contest that charge.
• The accused’s understanding of the nature of the hearing if he
or she entered a plea of not guilty as a process to determine
whether it has
been proved that the person committed the offence charged.
• The accused’s understanding of the consequences of entering a
plea of guilty, including what is being admitted when
a plea of guilty is
entered, the effect of a plea of guilty, that the court may impose punishment
and a general understanding of
the punishment that the person is in jeopardy of
• The accused’s understanding of the course of the sentencing
hearing and a general understanding of what is occurring
at each stage and the
right to challenge the facts stated by the prosecution.
These factors relate to the key decisions that an accused will need to make
in deciding whether to enter a guilty plea. In addition,
the accused must be
able to communicate to his or her legal practitioner his/her decision to plead
guilty and his/her version of
facts relevant to the sentencing hearing.
Recommendations
11. There should be a separate test in the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) to determine whether a person is fit to enter a
plea of guilty.
12. The test for fitness to enter a plea of guilty should reflect the
decision-making and communication approach contained in the
proposed test for
determining fitness to stand trial (see Recommendation 6) with necessary
alterations to reflect the difference
between entering a plea of guilty and
participating in a defended trial.
13. The following trial processes are relevant to a person’s
decision-making capacity to be fit to enter a guilty plea (see
Recommendation
• the nature of the offence with which he or she is charged.
• the right to contest that charge.
• the nature of the hearing if he or she entered a plea of not guilty
as a process to determine whether it has been proved that
the person committed
the offence charged.
• the consequences of entering a plea of guilty, including what is
being admitted when a plea of guilty is entered, the effect
of a plea of guilty,
that the court may impose punishment and a general understanding of the
punishment that the person is in jeopardy
of receiving.
• the accused’s understanding of the course of the sentencing
hearing and a general understanding of what is occurring
at each stage and the
right to challenge the facts stated by the prosecution.
to Stand Trial: Procedure to Determine Unfitness to Stand Trial
Introduction
6.1.1 This Part sets out the current Tasmanian procedure to determine
unfitness to stand trial, the position in other jurisdictions
and options that
are available to reform the current procedure. Concerns that arise in this area
• the role of experts and experts’ reports in the process of
determining unfitness to stand trial,
• the appropriate decision-maker (judge or jury) in making fitness
determinations in the Supreme Court, and
• the appropriateness of the current procedure for determining fitness
in the Magistrates Court.
6.1.2 In order to address these problems,
makes recommendations for
reform that relate to the need for best practice guidelines to be developed in
relation to the preparation
of expert reports to be used by the court to make a
determination of fitness to stand trial or to enter a guilty plea. Related to
Recommendation 10, the recommendations here highlight the need for experts to
provide information to the court in relation to supports
and accommodations that
may support the accused’s decision-making capacity and assist him/her to
participate in the criminal
justice process. Other significant recommendations
for reforms set out in
are that determinations of fitness to stand trial
in the Supreme Court should be determined by a judge rather than a jury
(Recommendation
16) and the introduction of a simplified alternative pathway for
appropriate cases in the Magistrates Court (Recommendations 17–18).
Tasmanian position
6.2.1 In Tasmania, subject to the potential referral of an accused to the
Diversion List,
procedure applies in relation to fitness to stand trial and insanity regardless
of whether the matter is an indictable offence
heard in the Supreme Court or a
summary offence dealt with in the Magistrates Court. The only difference is that
the magistrate makes
decisions in relation to fitness and the relevant findings
at a special hearing rather than a jury.

or the defendant, it may call evidence on its own

psychiatrist
or other appropriate expert and require the results to be reported
to the court.

The Magistrates
6.3.4 In relation to magistrates’ courts, there are different
approaches taken in relation to fitness to stand trial.
6.3.5 As with Tasmania, in the Australian Capital Territory, South Australia

determine the issue of
unfitness to stand trial.
the ACT, greater flexibility exists in relation to the powers that a magistrate
may exercise for summary offences where fitness

genuine issue. Instead of carrying out an investigation into fitness to plead
and the special hearing

magistrate has a power to dismiss the charge, if:
(a) the court is satisfied that there is a real and substantial question
(b) the court considers that because of the trivial nature of the charge or
the nature of the defendant’s mental impairment,
it would be inappropriate
to inflict any punishment on the defendant in relation to the
6.3.6 In Victoria, the Magistrates’ Court does not have the power to
determine unfitness to stand trial. Similarly, in the Northern
Territory, the
Local Court does not have an express power to determine unfitness to stand
If the issue of
unfitness is raised in relation to a summary offence, the matter is
discontinued.

to conduct special hearings after a finding of
The VLRC also

with or without conditions for a summary

magistrate considers
that the accused does not pose an unacceptable risk of
causing physical or psychological harm to another person, or other people
generally, as a result of the discharge and the accused is receiving treatment,
support or services in the
This reflects the
it was important to retain flexibility by having the option of not proceeding
with charges in particular circumstances. This was seen
as important so that
accused were not picked up by the CMIA pathway and made subject to compulsory
treatment or services where it
was considered unnecessary or inappropriate
having regard to the risk posed to the community and the person’s need for
or services. This may be appropriate where the offending behaviour is
such that the risk posed to the community is low, where the
condition has stabilised and there are appropriate supports in place for the
person in the community, and there is
no need for supervision of the accused
under the CMIA.

consideration
Assessment reports
6.4.1 Reports provided by experts provide the evidentiary foundation for
findings of unfitness and so are fundamental to the determination
of fitness to
stand trial.
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas), in addition to
requiring the court to hear evidence and representations by defendants or
prosecutors as to fitness, there is
also provision for the court to call
evidence on its own initiative and order an assessment of a defendant by a
psychiatrist or other
appropriate
6.4.2 In its consideration of the procedure in relation to determining
unfitness to stand trial, the VLRC observed that ‘[p]otential
arise in relation to the qualifications of experts, the quality and utility of
expert reports and the number of experts
relied on in assessments of unfitness
to stand trial’.
result, the VLRC sought feedback in relation to these issues, as well as other
issues that may exist in relation to the process
for determining unfitness to
stand trial.
6.4.3 In response, it was noted that, despite the general recognition of
expertise and objectivity of experts on issues of unfitness
acknowledgment of the difficult task faced by experts, some problems did arise
in this area. These included:
• discrepancies in experts’ training and expertise;
• reports sometimes lacking a forensic direction or familiarity with
CMIA legal concepts;
• assessments conducted inappropriately (for example, questions not
communicated effectively to people with an intellectual
disability or in a
culturally appropriate manner);
• a small pool of experts, which could compromise
objectivity.
6.4.4 Suggestions made by the VLRC to improve expert assessment in relation
to unfitness to stand trial included:
• Unfitness assessments should be conducted by a multi-disciplinary
specialised team of psychologists, psychiatrists and speech
pathologists.
• There should be careful regulation of experts who assess unfitness in
terms of qualifications and expertise through registration
or accreditation of
their competency to conduct these assessments. ...
• There should be clearer guidance to experts about the test for
unfitness. This could be achieved through the development of
best practice
guidelines on what should be included in a report, or through training.
• A component of every assessment should be standardised. For example,
there should be standard information that should be included
as part of every
report. ...
• Assessments should be conducted using appropriate communication
techniques.
6.4.5 The VLRC concluded that it is necessary to make changes to
‘address unnecessary variability and to ensure the fairness
and accuracy
of expert assessments of unfitness to stand
However, it
considered that ‘experts themselves are the best people to determine the
changes’ that would be necessary
to address these
6.4.6 Anecdotally, the TLRI understands that there is a lack of
standardisation in assessments provided by experts in Tasmania, and
consequently
there is considerable diversity in the assessment reports. This is a concern
given the importance of the expert advice
in the court process. Accordingly, in
the Issues Paper, the TLRI sought feedback on whether there are any issues that
arise in relation
to the role of experts and expert reports in the process of
determining unfitness to stand trial. Further, the TLRI sought feedback
relation to how any difficulties might be resolved, for example whether there
should be a specified minimum number of expert reports
that should be required,
whether there is a need for greater control in relation to the expertise or
accreditation of the person
conducting the assessment and writing the report for
the court, and/or the information that should be contained in the report.
Are there any issues that arise in relation to the role of experts and expert
reports in the process of determining unfitness to stand
If so, how do you think these problems might be resolved?
6.4.7 Five submissions
addressed the questions in relation to the role of experts and expert reports
and some of the potential problems
that may arise. A common theme in several
submissions related to need to bridge the gap between the legal context in which
the information
would be used and the assessment made by medical experts.
Accordingly, solutions proposed tended to focus on the use of guidelines
other strategies to facilitate better communication and consistency in approach.
O’Connell’s submission focused on
the expense of seeking expert
evidence for a defendant who is not funded by Legal
The submission of EOT
expressed the view that there is a need for a multi-disciplinary approach in
making assessments of fitness
involving experts in speech pathology and
communications.
that ‘[r]reports should include an assessment of whether the provision of
supports would assist the defendant to
participate in the trial and the nature
of the supports that should be provided to enable this to be so’.
6.4.8 The DPP indicated that ‘[o]n occasion, issues arise in relation
to the role of experts and expert reports in the process
of determining
unfitness to stand trial. This is often due to an expert not being given
sufficient material to enable an assessment
of the Crown and defence
case’. In response, the DPP indicated that there is ‘merit in the
development of best practice
guidelines that include a list of materials that
must be provided to an expert who is preparing a report assessing a
fitness to stand trial’.
6.4.9 Walvisch saw problems arising ‘due to the lack of a neat fit
between legal and clinical concepts of “mental disorder”’.
response was to have ‘clear guidance ... provided to experts working in
the field about both the
of the test and its precise
. This could be done through the development of a handbook for use
by practitioners, as well as through the implementation of training
courses for
people working in the field’. Walvisch also observed that the development
of a Practice Note by the court with
the assistance of relevant experts may be
useful for this purpose. In this regard, he noted that the Practice Note dealing
with expert
reports on mental functioning of offenders for sentencing hearings
was a possible model that could be adapted for use in fitness
This was developed
by the Forensic Evidence Working Group for use in the Supreme and County Courts
in Victoria. Its purpose is to
enhance the quality and reliability of expert
evidence, as well as the utility of the evidence.
6.4.10 ForensiClinic also saw issues arising from a lack of knowledge and
standardisation in the approach taken by experts to make
an assessment in
relation to fitness:
When issues arise regarding the interpretation of the legal test by assessing
practitioners, these tend to come from a lack of education
stand trial, a lack of education about the legal process, and a lack of
standardisation of methods used to evaluate
fitness. Methodological diversity is
influenced by qualifications (e.g., psychiatry, forensic psychology,
neuropsychology) and the
defendant’s diagnosis. Methodological diversity
and a lack of understanding about fitness causes heterogeneous interpretation
the fitness criteria. A united understanding of precedent and expectation would
resolve this issue.
Accordingly, ForensiClinic’s approach to the improvement of the role of
experts and expert reports included:
• Providing ‘[e]ducational opportunities for collaboration
between legal and forensic mental health practitioners’
• Standardising assessment methodology
• Having feedback provided from the courts and legal practitioners to
medical experts about the strengths and weaknesses of
the report.
6.4.11 In light of the submissions received, the Institute’s view is
that there is scope to improve the use of experts to inform
the court about a
person’s fitness to stand trial. There is a need for clearer guidance to
experts about the test of fitness
through the development of best practice
guidelines. This could be developed through an expert working group (as occurred
in Victoria)
involving judges, forensic psychiatrist and psychologists and legal
practitioners. This expert group should also include appropriate
expertise in
language and communications. In developing the guidelines, the expert group
should consider how to address challenges
arising in the assessment of fitness
to stand trial by experts posed by cultural and ethnic factors, as well as other
communication
difficulties. This may include taking into account the efficacy of
psychometric instruments, where relevant, that were developed
through studies of
Western, English-speaking populations. In addition to developing guidelines,
this collaboration would allow for
educational opportunities for legal and
medical practitioners. Further, the scope of the best practice guidelines would
need to accommodate
proposed changes to the fitness test, particularly in
relation to supports and accommodations reports that may assist defendants
participate in the criminal justice process.
Recommendations
14. Best practice guidelines should be developed for the preparation of
expert reports to be used by the court to make a determination
of fitness to
stand trial or enter a guilty plea. These should be developed by an expert
working group, which should consider how
to address challenges arising in the
assessment of fitness to stand trial by experts posed by cultural and ethnic
factors, as well
as other communication difficulties. This may include taking
into account the efficacy of psychometric instruments, where relevant,
developed through studies of Western, English-speaking populations.
15. The scope of the best practice guidelines should accommodate the proposed
changes to the fitness test and should provide information
to the court in
relation to the supports and accommodations that may support the person’s
decision-making capacity and assist
an individual to participate in the criminal
justice process.
Determination of the issue of fitness by a
judge rather than jury in the Supreme Court
6.4.12 In Victoria, consideration has been given to removing the requirement
for investigations into fitness to be determined by a
jury. The Victorian Law
Reform Committee recommended a judge-alone procedure for investigations of
fitness to stand trial on the
grounds that:
• fitness investigations primarily involve technical matters and
therefore it is more suitable for a hearing to be conducted
• a fitness hearing is not designed to be adversarial and no decisions
are made about the person’s criminal responsibility;
• a judge hearing evidence alone may be quicker, less formal and less
confusing or stressful for the accused with an intellectual
disability or
cognitive impairment, particularly if experts from both sides agree that the
accused is clearly unfit to be
6.4.13 The VLRC, similarly, recommended that unfitness to stand trial should
be determined by a judge or a magistrate on the basis
that this was a pre-trial
issue that need not be determined by a
The VLRC observed the
narrowing of the role of the jury in relation to pre-trial matters more
It also noted that
changing the criteria for a determination of fitness to stand trial meant that a
judge (rather than a jury) was
more appropriately able to undertake the nuanced
nature of pre-trial
decision-making.
6.4.14 In Tasmania, as noted, there is provision for the investigation into
fitness to be determined by a trial judge where the defence
and prosecution
agree. However, consideration could be given to removing the jury’s role
in relation to the determination of
the issue of fitness to stand trial in all
cases. Accordingly, the Institute sought feedback on this issue in the Issues
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
amended to provide that unfitness to stand trial is to be determined by a judge
in the Supreme Court instead of a jury in
6.4.15 Five submissions addressed whether the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) should be amended to
provide that unfitness to stand trial should be determined by a judge in the
Supreme Court instead of
a jury in all cases. This option for reform was clearly
supported in four of the submissions on the basis that the decision to be
aligns more closely with the judicial role in a trial than with the jury, that
it involves a test that is more readily applied
by judicial officers given their
understanding of the trial process and would be likely to lead to a more timely
and less stressful
6.4.16 The DPP supported such an amendment on the basis that it was
consistent with the procedure in other jurisdictions, including
New South Wales,
Western Australia, the Australian Capital Territory and also with other
provisions in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
The DPP also considered that it would streamline the process, create
efficiencies and reduce stress and confusion for the
accused. In addition, the
DPP argued that ‘determining the issue of fitness should not be
unnecessarily adversarial. The issues
that require determination are technical;
they are not determinations about a person’s criminal
responsibility’.
6.4.17 Walvisch also observed that the judge should always have the power to
determine the issue of unfitness to stand trial. In support
of this view, he
noted the views of the VLRC (as set out at [5.4.7] of the
Issues Paper
as well as the following:
• The decision that is being made in this context is a procedural one,
rather than a substantive one. Consequently, it fits
more neatly within the
general role of the judge to practically manage the course of the trial, than
with the role of the jury to
determine the facts surrounding the offence;
• The decision relates to the accused’s ability to meaningfully
participate in the trial process. The judge, with his
or her specialised
knowledge of trial procedure and processes, is far better placed to make a
determination in this regard;
• Contested matters will require the application of highly specialised
expert evidence to complex legal issues. Judges with
experience in these matters
are more likely to be able to adequately perform this task than lay jurors.
6.4.18 CLC Tas reported that ‘[a]necdotally, lawyers who appear as
counsel in these applications observe that the jury is reliant
on expert reports
provided by psychiatrists and other medical expertise’. On this basis, CLC
Tas considered that ‘it
should be sufficient for a judge alone, after
analysing expert medical opinion, to determine the question of fitness to stand
This would be likely to result in fewer delays and finalisation of
the issue in a timelier manner.
6.4.19 ForensiClinic supported reform to provide that fitness to stand trial
should be determined by a judge and not a jury. This
was supported on the basis
that it provides greater transparency of decision-making in the event of an
6.4.20 The Department of Police, Fire and Emergency Management
(‘DPFEM’) did not express clear agreement with the amendment
proposal but gave to support to the idea by expressing the view that
‘consideration could be given to removing the jury’s
determining the issue of fitness to stand trial in all cases’. This was
because of the complicated nature of the assessment
required (and the potential
for conflicting expert evidence) and that it is an extension of the provision in
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) that allows the
judge to determine fitness in cases where the defence and prosecution agree.
6.4.21 Based on the support for reform in the submissions received by the
Institute, the relevant literature and the approach taken
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) should be amended to provide that the
investigation into fitness to stand trial must be determined by a judge (rather
the jury). The Institute’s view is that the determination of fitness
to stand trial is a pre-trial matter that the judge is
best placed to assess.
The recommendation of the Institute in relation to reforms to the test for
determining fitness strengthen
arguments for this position because it is
appropriate that a judge makes the decisions (based on information provided to
by relevant experts) about the supports and accommodations that may
facilitate fitness for trial as part of the decision making process.

Procedure in the Magistrates
6.4.22 In Tasmania, if an issue of fitness to stand trial arises, the
procedure for determining fitness and the conduct of special
hearings applies in
both the Supreme Court and in the Magistrates Court (as set out at [
]). This is a complex process (and a
potentially costly and time consuming one) and unlike the ACT and Queensland,
and as recommended
in Victoria, there is no provision in Tasmania for a
magistrate to dismiss the charge without conducting a special hearing. In New
South Wales, there is also power for a magistrate to dismiss the charge.
Accordingly, it may be argued that it is appropriate to
have a simpler and more
flexible approach available in the Magistrates Court. An option for reform would
be to amend the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
provide for greater flexibility to allow the magistrate to dispose of a matter
in a timelier manner in circumstances where
it appears that a defendant is unfit
for trial and this is appropriate in the context of the
This reform
would mean that the magistrate could make an assessment of whether it appears
that an accused is unfit to stand trial
(and/or has a defence of insanity) and
then discharge the person and divert them from the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act
pathway, if this is appropriate.
6.4.23 Possible models for reform are found in other jurisdictions. The power
to discharge could be made following a determination
that the court is satisfied
that it appears that the accused is cognitively impaired, suffering a mental
illness or suffering from
a mental condition for which treatment is available in
a mental health facility, or that the accused is mentally ill (the New South
Wales position) or that there is a real and substantial question about the
defendant’s fitness to stand trial (the ACT position
and that proposed in
Victoria) or following a finding that on the balance of probabilities the
accused is unfit to stand trial or
unsound (Queensland approach and current
Tasmanian test for unfitness to stand trial). In addition, in the ACT, the power
of discharge
can be exercised where the matter is trivial or, taking into
account the nature of the defendant’s mental impairment, it would
inappropriate to inflict any punishment on the defendant. In Victoria, it was
recommended that the magistrate’s power of
discharge would depend on an
assessment of the risk posed to the community and the treatment, support or
services received in the
community by the accused.
6.4.24 There is also scope to improve the effectiveness of the process, for
example, by adopting the Queensland approach of allowing
Court Liaison Officers
to provide advice to magistrates on a person’s state of mind at the time
of the offence or fitness for
Similarly, in
Victoria, the VLRC’s view was that the operation of the Mental Health
Liaison Service (with appropriate funding)
could be expanded to allow the
service to provide information to magistrates to ‘assist them in
determination whether there
is a real and substantial question as to the
unfitness of the
6.4.25 In the Issues Paper, feedback was sought
in the Magistrates Court.
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
amended to allow a magistrate to discharge an individual without making a
determination of their fitness to stand trial
or criminal responsibility?
What (if any) limitations should be set out in relation to the exercise of
the power of discharge?
6.4.26 Seven responses addressed these questions, and all agreed that
amendment to the current procedure that applies in the Magistrates
appropriate so that the magistrate has power to discharge an individual under
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas). However,
differences of opinion were expressed about the test that should be applied in
relation to the exercise of the power
of discharge.
6.4.27 CLC Tas expressed its strong belief that ‘there should be an
emphasis on diverting persons with cognitive and mental
health impairments away
from the criminal justice system and into treatment.’ For matters in the
Magistrates Court, CLC Tas
highlighted the financial constraints that may pose a
barrier to justice for some individuals. The cost of obtaining a report from
psychiatrist or other appropriate expert (in the absence of legal aid funding)
may result in some people not being able to prove
their incapacity to stand
trial. This was also noted by Marita O’Connell who wrote that
‘[s]eeking expert evidence is
also a very expensive endeavour and places
the onus on the defendant to provide such evidence. If the defendant does not
have a grant
of Legal Aid, this can be a complex and expensive route to
take’. O’Connell also supported the introduction of diversionary
provisions. The cost and complexity of the legal process in raising fitness to
stand trial and/or insanity and the value of diversion
was also a focus in the
submission from TasCOSS.
6.4.28 CLC Tas also pointed to the lack of ‘provision in the
Magistrates Court for a magistrate to dismiss a charge without
conducting a
special hearing, a process that is both complex and time-consuming’. To
address this, CLC Tas supported a simpler
and more flexible approach (as found
in other jurisdictions) but did not have a view of which threshold test was the
most appropriate.
It did, however, strongly believe that magistrates must be
provided with the discretion, available in all three jurisdictions mentioned
above, to dismiss the offence and/or divert the person to treatment, a reform
that would reduce the court backlog, improve access
to services and support
recovery. O’Connell, in her submission, also highlighted the value of
providing diversionary provisions
for defendants with mental illness or mental
impairment appearing in the magistrates court from the point of view of the
and the court system. In O’Connell’s view, a diversionary
process would ‘provide a mechanism for more timely
treatment/support’
as well as cutting ‘down on lengthy and expensive
hearing times and the need for expensive expert evidence for summary
6.4.29 In terms of approach, O’Connell pointed to the recent measures
introduced in Queensland:

6.4.30 The DPP indicated that ‘in some circumstances it may be

the power to discharge

[f]or example, a magistrate should be required to be satisfied, on the

The test should provide magistrates with the power to discharge if

the defendant’s fitness to stand trial. This threshold was suggested

We are concerned to ensure, however, that any presumption made about an

and isn’t used as a mechanism to undermine the rights of the charged

6.4.33 The DPFEM also supported a change to the process that operates in the

is appropriate in the context
of the particular case or exercised where the

The Institute can certainly see the logical connections that exist between

6.4.35 Based on the support for reform in the submissions received by the
Institute and the approach taken in other jurisdictions,
the Institute’s
view is that the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
should be amended to allow a magistrate to discharge an individual without
making a determination of their fitness to stand
trial or criminal
responsibility. It is the Institute’s view that the power to discharge
should operate as follows:
(1) It should apply to summary offences and indictable offences dealt with
summarily. This will provide consistency of approach for
all matters involving
adult offenders dealt with in the jurisdiction of the Magistrates Court.
However, the discharge power should
not apply to matters that are referred to
the Magistrates Court for trial from the Supreme Court by virtue of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 308, as it is the Institute’s view that
matters of fitness are more appropriately resolved in the Supreme Court prior
the judge taking a view that the matter should be tried summarily.
(2) The threshold test for the operation of the discretion to discharge
should be that there is a real and substantial question in
relation to the
defendant’s fitness to stand trial and not the lesser standard of
‘it appears’ (as exists in New
South Wales) or the higher standard
of balance of probabilities (as exists in Queensland). In the view of the
Institute, the ‘real
and substantial’ test should be adopted because
it is the same test as contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
that limits the power of a court to conduct an
investigation into a defendant’s fitness to stand trial. It is not
appropriate
that the test be on the balance of probabilities as this is the same
test that applies for actual determination of fitness and so
this approach is
unlikely to provide for a more efficient or streamlined process in appropriate
(3) Expert advice in relation to the issue of fitness for the purpose of
exercising the discretion should be able to be provided to
the magistrate by the
Court Liaison Officers. This will require that appropriate resources are made
available to the Court Liaison
(4) While it is acknowledged that it is likely that there will generally be
agreement between the defence and prosecution that a discharge
is an appropriate
course for the magistrate to take, the power should operate as a genuine
discretion vested in the magistrate and
should not require the consent of the
prosecution and defence.
(5) There should be legislative guidance on factors that are relevant to the
exercise of the discretion to discharge. Such an approach
may be appropriate,
for example, when the risk posed to the community is low, where the
defendant’s condition has stabilised
or the defendant has accessed
appropriate services or treatment in the community and a more interventionist
response from the court
Accordingly,
legislative guidance should include a non-exhaustive list of matters that are
relevant to the decision to dismiss the
charge, including the following matters:
(1) the nature of the offence, (2) the nature of the impairment, (3) the risk to
the community,
(4) the treatment, support or services available to the
Institute’s view is that such an approach protects the rights of the
individual as well as the safety of the community
consistent with the least
restrictive alternative.
(6) Although the power to discharge a person conditionally exists in some
in Victoria) or to
require a person to undergo examination (for example, in Queensland), the
Institute’s view is that it is
not appropriate to allow the court to
impose conditions on the discharge of the defendant under the flexible pathway
proposed for
the Magistrates Court. The person has not had their fitness
determined by an investigation and the prosecution case has not been
having their responsibility determined by a special hearing. However, the
treatment needs and the willingness and ability
of the person to access
treatment and/or other services appropriate to their needs are factors that are
relevant to the power to
Recommendations
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) should
be amended to allow a magistrate to discharge people without making
determinations of their fitness to stand trial or
their criminal responsibility.
18. The power to discharge should operate as follows:
(a) It should apply to summary offences and indictable offences dealt with
summarily. The discharge power should not apply to matters
that are referred to
the Magistrates Court for trial from the Supreme Court by virtue of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 308.
(b) The threshold test for the operation of the discretion to discharge
should be that there is a real and substantial question about
defendant’s fitness to stand trial.
(c) Advice in relation to the issue of fitness for the purpose of
exercising the discretion should be provided to the magistrate by
Liaison Officers. Appropriate resources should be made available to the Court
Liaison Service for this
(d) The power should operate as a genuine discretion vested in the
magistrate and should not require the consent of the prosecution
and defence.
(e) Legislative guidance as to the factors that are relevant to the
exercise of the discretion to discharge the charges should be
provided and
include: (1) the nature of the offence, (2) the nature of the impairment, (3)
the risk to the community, (4) the treatment,
support or services available to
the accused.
(f) The power to discharge should only provide for an unconditional
discharge and not a conditional discharge or the power to order
therapeutic intervention by the magistrate.
to Stand Trial: Procedure Following a Determination of Unfitness to Stand
Introduction
7.1.1 This Part sets out the procedure following a determination of unfitness
to stand trial in Tasmania and in other comparable jurisdictions.
examines options for reform of this procedure. Aligned with the approach of the
Institute to facilitate supported decision-making
(as set out in Part 4), the
Institute makes recommendations to use intermediaries and communication
assistants in special hearings.
Recommendations are also made to allow for a
special hearing to be heard by a judge alone where the prosecution and the
defence agree
in relation to the defence of insanity (or, as recommended in the
Report, the defence of mental or cognitive impairment).

7.2.4 Although the original intention was for ‘the special hearing ...
to determine the external facts of the case — excluding
the question of
intent — to ascertain whether the basic prosecution case can be proved

it appears that
an accused’s intention is relevant to the jury’s assessment in
special hearings. In
the defendant faced a
special hearing in relation to one count of committing an unlawful act intended
to cause bodily harm and, in
the alternative, causing grievous bodily harm. The
defendant was found not guilty of the first count, but the jury was not able to
find that he was not guilty of the alternative crime of causing grievous bodily
harm. In making orders under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas), Wood J stated that:
Clearly, the jury was not satisfied beyond reasonable doubt that the
defendant had held the necessary intention required for the charge
of committing
an unlawful act intended to cause bodily harm. The jury was not satisfied beyond
reasonable doubt that ... W had intended
to cause Mr S grievous bodily harm. The
jury must have been satisfied that he realised that striking Mr S and causing
him grievous
bodily harm was a likely consequence of driving at him, and that he
went ahead disregarding that
Accordingly, in order for a jury to be unable to find that an accused is not
guilty, the prosecution must prove the external facts
and mental element for the
offence, as well as disprove any relevant defence (such as self-defence) beyond
reasonable doubt. If the
defence of insanity is raised, this must be proved by
the accused on the balance of
probabilities.
7.2.5 At a special hearing, there are three findings available to the
The court may:
(a) find the defendant not guilty of the offence. This has the same effect as
a finding of not guilty following a trial of criminal
proceedings;
(b) find the defendant not guilty on the ground of insanity; or
(c) indicate that a finding cannot be made that the defendant is not guilty
of the offence charged. This finding is made if the jury
‘concluded beyond
reasonable doubt, on the evidence before it, that the accused appeared to be
7.2.6 It is not possible for the prosecution and
defence to dispense with the need for a special hearing by agreeing that the
should be found not guilty on the grounds of insanity. Similarly, it is
not possible for the defendant who is found unfit to stand
trial to enter a plea
However, it is
noted that the Institute has made recommendations that would create a new test
to determine fitness to stand trial
(see Recommendations 4–9) and has also
recommended that the prosecution and defence be able to agree to a finding of
on the basis of mental or cognitive impairment with judicial
oversight (see Recommendation 21).
7.3.1 In Australia, while there is broad similarity in the criteria used to
