consideration
7.4.1 There are issues that arise in relation to the procedure following a
determination of unfitness to stand trial. They are:
• whether the special hearing process complies with the human rights
obligations under the
• whether there should be provision for a judge-alone process for
special hearings.
obligations
7.4.2 Special hearing provisions in Australia (and
comparable jurisdictions) have been criticised by those concerned with the human
rights obligations arising under the
. These criticisms have focused
on the removal of the accused from the mainstream criminal justice system and
the extent to which
the modified process of the special hearing differs from a
criminal trial.
Gooding et al
have suggested that, in order to meet
obligations, the minimum
requirements that should apply to any alternative procedure are:
• The same standard of proof and probative value of prosecution
evidence as in typical trial;
• The same presumption of innocence, with the associated requirement
for proof of all elements;
• The availability to the accused of all defences; and
• That proceedings against the accused must be based on his or her
‘rights, wishes and preferences’ (and not his
or her ‘best
interests’).
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
appear to comply substantially with the minimum requirements under the
as identified by Gooding et al. The provisions require that the same
standard of proof apply and that the special hearing is to be
‘as nearly as possible’ to normal criminal trials. The prosecution
must prove the external and fault elements
of the offence beyond reasonable
doubt and the defendant can rely on any defences that would have been available
in a mainstream
criminal trial.
7.4.4 Nevertheless, specific criticism has been directed at the Tasmanian
provision on the ground that it allows the jury to determine
finding cannot be made that the defendant is not guilty of the offence charged
or any offence available as an
alternative’.
et al, write that:
A literal reading of this provision suggests that in order for the accused to
secure an unconditional acquittal, the special hearing
jury must make a positive
finding that the accused is not guilty. The Tasmanian Supreme Court has
suggested that a qualified finding
is accordingly available “if the jury
concluded beyond reasonable doubt, on the evidence before it, that the accused
to be guilty”. It is not clear what it means for an accused to
‘appear to be guilty’, but this ruling raises concern
integrity of the presumption of innocence.
7.4.5 However, a contrary view is that Gooding et al’s interpretation
of the legislation does not accord with the interpretation
of the provision or
the approach adopted by the court at a special hearing. In the Tasmanian
context, it is clear that the special
hearing does not result in a reversal of
the onus of proof as the jury is directed that an accused is deemed to be
innocent unless
and until the jury is satisfied beyond reasonable doubt that the
accused committed the particular offence and the Crown has the burden
7.4.6 Further, as the Law Commission of England
and Wales has argued, the purpose of the special hearing is to provide an
with an equal opportunity for an
This means that at
a special hearing the jury makes a determination (as with the usual criminal
trial process) as to whether the
prosecution has established the case beyond
reasonable doubt. If the prosecution has not established its case, then the
a special hearing (like an accused in the mainstream criminal
process) is entitled to an acquittal. In a mainstream trial, if the
satisfied that the prosecution has established its case beyond reasonable doubt,
then it will find the accused guilty. However,
at a special hearing, given the
limitations of the accused’s capacity to participate in a trial, it is not
considered appropriate
to find the accused guilty of the offence (and hence the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
that a finding cannot be made that the defendant is not guilty of the offence
charged or any offence available as an alternative).
7.4.7 Further concerns have been raised that the special hearing process
removes the benefits of entering an early guilty plea in
sentence mitigation as
it assumes that the person has pleaded not
However, it should be
remembered that a person dealt with under the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) is not ‘sentenced’ and the usual
sentencing principles (including a discount for a guilty plea) do not apply to
the determination of the appropriate court order. Instead, the court applies the
principles under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
that focus on the nature of the defendant’s mental impairment and the
likely danger that he or she poses to other people,
and the need to ensure that
restrictions on the defendant’s freedom and personal autonomy are kept to
a minimum consistent
with the safety of the
comparison of the potential sentences (taking into account a plea of guilty and
the person’s mental or cognitive
impairment as mitigating factors) and a
finding of unfitness and the dispositions that may follow a special hearing may
inappropriately
influence an accused’s decision to proceed with a guilty
7.4.8 An additional criticism that could be made is in relation to the
requirement for the accused’s legal representative to
exercise his or her
independent decision-making discretion in the best interests of the accused.
This is in conflict with the
requirement that proceedings against an
accused should be based on his or her ‘rights, wishes and
preferences’ rather
than their best interests. It is also inconsistent
with the National Decision-Making
Principles.
7.4.9 Accordingly, in the Issues Paper, the Institute sought feedback on the
nature of the special hearing to explore concerns about
the differences between
a special hearing and an ordinary trial, concerns about the presumption of
innocence and the role of legal
practitioners in special hearings. The Issues
Paper asked the following questions:
Do you consider that the conduct of a special hearing differs from an
ordinary trial in terms of the evidence adduced or the conduct
of the hearing?
If so, in what ways?
Do you consider that the conduct of the special hearing is consistent with
the presumption of innocence?
Do issues arise in relation to the conduct of legal practitioners in acting
in the ‘best interests’ of a person rather
than based that
person’s ‘rights, wishes and preferences’?
7.4.10 Four
submissions to the Institute addressed these questions.
7.4.11 The DPP and O’Connell identified the streamlining of the
evidence as a key difference between a special hearing and an
ordinary trial.
The DPP observed that in special hearings ‘it is common for the parties to
more readily agree matters that
are not in dispute’ but considered that
that the ‘conduct of the special hearing is consistent with the
presumption of
innocence’. The DPP noted that the onus and standard of
proof were the same for a special hearing as in a criminal trial. Accordingly,
the ‘jury still has to be satisfied beyond reasonable doubt of an
accused’s guilt for the offence charged; if they are
not so satisfied an
outright acquittal is returned’. This point was also made by
In contrast,
O’Connell submitted that:
there are a number of cases where the elements of the special hearing are
generally agreed and so the special hearing differs from
an ordinary hearing in
that all facts and evidence are agreed to by both parties. This can make the
process something of a ‘rubber
stamp’ exercise.
7.4.12 EOT highlighted the human rights implications of the special hearing
process noting in particular the inconsistency between
principles and the procedures in several jurisdictions. EOT stressed that
‘of particular concern are limitations placed on the
participate in a meaningful way in proceedings which risks diminishing the
ability of the court to test the case against
the accused’ and ‘that
the holding of special hearings may compromise the common law principle that a
trial not be held
when the defendant’s abilities are so limited that the
trial would be unfair or unjust’. EOT cited the concerns about
the special
process hearing expressed by Arstein et al:
Even though many jurisdictions explicitly strive to emulate the trial process
as closely as possible, a special hearing is fundamentally
not a trial. The
procedures are different. The available verdicts are different. Ultimate
dispositions – potentially including
indefinite detention – are
radically different. Therefore, a special hearing may not adequately uphold the
CRPD obligations
for persons with disabilities to have an equal right to a fair
However, in circumstances where an accused cannot participate in a trial
under the ordinary criminal procedures, EOT recommended adoption
following criteria (as identified by Gooding et al) as the standards to be met
to ensure minimum compliance with the
• The same standard of proof and probative value of prosecution
• The same presumption of innocence and the need for the prosecution to
prove all elements of offences
• The availability of all defences
• Proceedings based on ‘rights, wishes and preference’ and
not ‘best interests’.
7.4.13 As noted, in the Tasmanian context, most of these criteria currently
apply. There is the same standard and onus of proof for
a special hearing as
applies in ordinary criminal procedures, there is the same presumption of
innocence and the same availability
of all defences to an accused. Further, as
noted in the submissions received by the Institute and in the Issues Paper,
concerns about the role of the legal practitioners, particularly where
accused are unable to give instructions. According to O’Connell,
‘the presumption of innocence is compromised for matters that proceed to
special hearing. In the absence of sound instructions,
it is difficult to refute
the charges.’ Similarly, O’Connell noted that:
issues do arise in relation to the conduct of legal practitioners in acting
in the ‘best interests’ of a person rather
than based on that
person’s ‘rights, wishes and preferences’, particularly when a
defendant may be opposed to any
kind of mandated treatment or oversight, which
is the likely outcome of any special hearing procedures.
7.4.14 Walvisch also commented on the inconsistency of the current
legislative approach with the
National Decision-Making Principles. In his view
reform should be enacted:
to provide that legal practitioners should act in accordance with the
accused’s rights, wishes and preferences where these can
be ascertained.
However, it may be necessary to have a fall-back position (such as a ‘best
interests’ test) for use in
cases where no rights, wishes or preferences
can be determined.
7.4.15 The DPP also noted the ethical difficulties created for a legal
practitioner in determining what is in the ‘best interests’
accused person. The DPP stated that this:
requires the legal practitioner to consider the person’s rights, wishes
and preferences. However, as the procedure follows a
finding that the person is
unfit to stand trial, the very purpose of the procedure is that the person may
not be capable of acting
in their own best interests and exercising their
rights. This requires the legal practitioner acting on behalf of such a person
determine what is in the best interests of their client. This in and of
itself may create ethical issues.
Likewise, the NSWLRC has noted the ‘legal and ethical “grey
area”’ faced by legal practitioners when clients
are unable to give
proper instructions
that problems arise when an unfit defendant is ‘unable accurately and
coherently to give evidence’ and in determining
the scope of a legal
practitioner’s discretion in cases where a person is unable to give
instructions’.
7.4.16 Based on its detailed consideration in the Issues Paper and the
submissions received, the Institute’s view is that compliance
requires that the special hearing should reflect the conduct of an
ordinary trial as far as is possible. Further, following a finding
of unfitness,
it is absolutely necessary to have some mechanism to provide an individual with
an opportunity for an acquittal. Given
the finding of unfitness this cannot be
an ordinary trial. It is the Institute’s assessment that the special
hearing provisions
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) allow for this to happen in a way that is compliant with the
with one exception — the role of the legal practitioner in
circumstances where the accused is unable to give instructions.
7.4.17 As noted at [
the criteria for a finding of unfitness relates to the ability to give
instructions to a legal practitioner. This has clear
implications for cases
where a special hearing is held, and an individual is not able to communicate
his or her instructions effectively
to his or her legal representative. The
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
for a legal representative to exercise an independent discretion in such cases
and to act in what he or she genuinely believes
to be the defendant’s best
interests on any question relating to an investigation or special hearing. A key
component of the
Institute’s approach to this reference is to maximise the
participation of defendants with mental health or cognitive impairments.
is scope for the use of intermediaries and other supports to assist defendants
in providing instructions to legal practitioners.
Where an individual’s
rights, wishes and preferences can be ascertained, then a legal practitioner
should act in accordance
with them. In this regard, the Institute notes the
potential role of intermediaries and communication assistants and the vital role
that education and training of legal professionals will play as outlined in the
Institute’s
Facilitating Equal Access to Justice: An
Intermediary/Communication Assistant Scheme for Tasmania?
Report. However, there may be cases where these mechanisms are not available
or are ineffective. This will necessarily raise questions
practitioners are to proceed in the absence of instructions.
7.4.18 The Institute’s view is that this issue requires further
attention given that the intermediary/communication assistant
scheme has not yet
been implemented and the approach taken to the use of intermediaries and
communication assistants in special hearings
should reflect the approach of that
scheme. This will require legislative amendment to the
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) to make provision in this regard.
Further, there should be legislative recognition that in the conduct of an
investigation and
a special hearing the defendant’s rights, wishes and
preferences should be given effect where he or she is capable of giving
instructions. Nevertheless, a decision needs to be made about the obligations of
legal practitioners in circumstances where defendants’
rights, wishes and
preferences cannot be ascertained even with assistance. The Institute’s
view is that, in these circumstances,
legal practitioners must act to promote
and uphold defendants’ personal and social wellbeing and in a way that is
least restrictive
of their human
Recommendations

investigation into fitness to enter a guilty plea/to stand
trial and in special
hearings, a person’s rights, wishes and preferences should be given effect
where a person is capable of
giving instructions. However, in circumstances
where defendants are not capable of giving instructions even with support, legal
practitioners
must act to promote and uphold the person’s personal and
social wellbeing in a way least restrictive of their human rights.
Judge only proceedings
7.4.19 Law reform enquiries in England and Wales and Victoria have considered
whether or not there should be a judge-only alternative
procedure — either
in all cases (England and Wales) or in cases where the prosecution and defence
agree that the evidence establishes
the defence of mental impairment/insanity
(Victoria). It is also noted that in South Australia, the defendant may elect to
matter dealt with by a judge sitting alone rather than having the
investigation conducted by a
Recent reforms in the
Northern Territory allow for the special hearing to be dispensed with if the
parties to the prosecution of
the offence agree that the evidence establishes
the defence of mental
impairment.
7.4.20 In Victoria, there is provision for a ‘consent mental
impairment’ hearing if the prosecution and defence agree
that the evidence
establishes the defence of mental
impairment.
However, this
option is not available for a person who has been found unfit to stand trial and
the determination of mental impairment
needs to be made by a
Accordingly, the VLRC
gave consideration to whether a judge-alone procedure should be available in
place of a special hearing where
the prosecution and defence agree that the
evidence establishes the defence of mental impairment.
7.4.21 In submissions to the VLRC, the following reasons were advanced in
support of such a consent process:
• There is little utility or benefit in court and jury resources being
allocated to a special hearing in these circumstances.
A judge-alone procedure
would avoid the delay, inconvenience and expense of a special hearing.
• The court is able to determine the accused’s criminal
responsibility in a robust manner.
• A judge-alone process would avoid putting the parties through the
stress of a special hearing.
The Law Commission of England and Wales also outlined the following matters
in support of a judge-only process:
• [It would] be less formal, less time-consuming and may lead to fewer
delays in concluding the proceedings.
• A judge may be better positioned than a jury to analyse the expert
evidence adduced.
• Empirical research suggests that in the majority of cases the
[special] hearing is not
• Judges have to give reasons for their decisions which in this context
would be valuable.
• The objection based upon the apparent loss of a trial by jury is
properly met by the fact that this is not a decision which
attributes criminal
7.4.22 Opponents of a judge-alone process observed that a person who is unfit
to stand trial will not be able to instruct their lawyer
to agree to the
This concern was
also shared by the VLRC.
addition, the VLRC expressed the view that the jury (as community
representative) should be involved in determining the issues
at a special
hearing in the interests of the community and the victims and as a means to
protect the rights of the
It observed
the determination of criminal responsibility by a jury provides a greater
level of acknowledgement to victims and their families of
the harm they have
experienced. The importance of acknowledgment of victims and their family
members is not diminished in cases where
an accused has been found under law not
to be criminally responsible due to a finding of not guilty because of mental
impairment.
The Commission recognises the importance of victims and their
families witnessing the process of the hearing, and listening to the
psychiatric
or psychological evidence and the reasons for dealing with accused within the
forensic mental health or disability system
rather than the prison system. In
the Commission’s view, this will promote the acceptance and understanding
of the finding
of not guilty because of mental impairment and its underlying
causes, such as a mental illness, intellectual disability or other
impairment.
Opposition to a judge-alone process reported by the Law Commission of England
and Wales also focused on the erosion of the jury trial
and the role of the jury
as the fact-finder as a vital component of the criminal justice system. It was
thought that to remove the
jury may reduce public willingness to accept the
findings following special hearings and may undermine the legitimacy of the
justice system.
addition, a judge-alone process would be in conflict with the requirements for
equality under the
given that this process would differ from
fact-finding in the usual trial
7.4.23 Responses received by the VLRC and the Law Commission of England and
Wales were divided on this issue. Similarly, the approaches
taken by the law
reform bodies were different. Ultimately, the VLRC recommended against allowing
a judge-alone process and the Law
Commission of England and Wales considered
that a judge-alone process should be available at the election of the
7.4.24 The Issues Paper sought feedback on whether there should be a
judge-alone process for special hearings:
Should any changes be made to the procedure for a special hearing?
Should there be a judge-alone process available instead of a special hearing?
If so, should this be available in circumstances where
the prosecution and
defence agree that the evidence establishes the defence of insanity at a special
hearing? Or should there be
a judge-alone process available instead of a special
hearing in all cases?
7.4.25 Three submissions to the Institute addressed these questions, and, as
with other inquiries into the issue, differing views
were expressed. Walvisch,
for the reasons outlined at [6.4.22], did not support the introduction of a
judge-alone process for a special
hearing. In contrast, the DPP’s view was
that the ‘need for a special hearing should be able to be dispensed with
circumstances where two or more experts agree that the accused should be
found not guilty on the grounds of insanity and the prosecution
and defence
agree’. The DPP noted that ‘[i]t is not uncommon for experts to
agree on their findings. In such circumstances,
it is difficult to see how a
properly instructed jury could reach a verdict that was inconsistent with expert
evidence given at trial’.
The DPP also noted that there may be scope to
limit the use of special hearings in some other circumstances:
There is a distinction between the physical elements and the mental elements
of an offence. It is possible that two or more experts
will agree that an
accused person should be found not guilty on the grounds of insanity and for
prosecution and defence to agree
on that issue. However, prosecution and defence
may disagree as to the physical elements of the crime. In such circumstances, a
hearing to consider simply the physical elements of the offence, to be
determined by a jury, may be beneficial. I would support consideration
given to legislative reform that would divide the trial into objective physical
elements and the mental elements, as is the
approach in South Australia.
O’Connell stated that ‘in the most serious of matters, I do
support a judge-alone model or even a model of a panel of
Justices and a
psychiatrist to consider matters in an inquisitorial manner as is the case for
the mental health court in Queensland.’
7.4.26 After considering these submissions, the approaches to the issue in
other jurisdictions and the relevant literature, the Institute
recommends that
provision should be made for a ‘consent verdict’ of not guilty by
reason of mental or cognitive impairment
to be available at a special hearing
where the prosecution and defence agree that the defence would be available on
the evidence.
This reflects the Institute’s recommendation that there
should be provision for ‘insanity by consent’ in cases
person is fit for trial.
However, as with the process in cases where the person is fit for trial, the
Institute’s view is that this process requires
judicial oversight to
provide appropriate
safeguards.
Accordingly, the
Institute’s view is that the court should have the power to review the
evidence and hear from the experts
in order to be satisfied that the evidence
establishes the defence of mental or cognitive impairment in a case where the
prosecution
and defence agree that the proposed evidence establishes the
7.4.27 Further, it is the Institute’s view that in all other cases the
special hearing should heard by heard by a jury in the
Supreme Court. This
reflects the principle that special hearings should be, as far as possible,
consistent with ordinary trials.
At a special hearing, a jury is not able to
make a finding that a person is guilty but they have the responsibility for
determining
whether a person is acquitted or whether the evidence is such that a
finding cannot be made that the defendant is not guilty of the
Recommendation
21. There should be provision in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
(Tas) for a special hearing by a judge alone where the prosecution
and defence agree that the defence of mental or cognitive impairment
established on the evidence. However, safeguards are required and the court
should have the power to review the evidence
(including hearing from expert
witnesses) in order to be satisfied that the evidence establishes the defence of
mental or cognitive
impairment in a case where the prosecution and defence agree
that the proposed evidence establishes the defence.
Introduction
8.1.1 The Terms of Reference request that the TLRI consider the operation of
the law of insanity in Tasmania with particular reference
(a) the operation of s 16(3) of the
Criminal Code
(b) whether evidence of insane delusions arising from mental illness should
form the basis of self-defence;
(c) if insane delusions arising from mental illness form the basis of
self-defence, whether defendants relying on insane delusions
should be liable to
supervision under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(d) if insane delusions arising from mental illness form the basis of
self-defence, whether the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
requires amendment in relation to treatment options for such defendants.
Aspects of this reference have already been considered by the TLRI in its
review of the law of self-defence, where the TLRI’s
view was that the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16(3) should be repealed and that a provision be
included in s 46 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) to the effect that evidence
of delusions arising from a mental illness cannot be relied on for the purposes
of self-defence.
In this review, the Institute also stated that a review of the
insanity defence was warranted given that the law of insanity ‘pre-dates
the inception of modern psychiatry and psychology as professional
disciplines.’
Particular concerns identified were the scope of the legal concept of mental
disease (compared to the medical understanding of mental
illness) and the focus
on an accused’s incapacity rather than his or her actual awareness or
understanding.
8.1.2 This Part provides an overview of the law of insanity in Tasmania,
identifies concerns in relation to the existing law and outlines
recommendations
for reform. The key reforms recommended are to retain and reform the defence of
insanity in the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s16 by:
• Changing the name of the defence of the mental or cognitive
impairment.
• Changing the scope of the qualifying condition by replacing the term
‘mental disease’ with a detailed definition
of mental and cognitive
impairment.
• Changing the terminology in s 16(1)(a)(i) from ‘understanding
the physical character’ to ‘understanding
the nature and
quality’ of such act or omission.
• Repealing s16(3).
• Making legislative provision for the prosecution to raise the defence
of mental or cognitive impairment against the wishes
of the defendant with the
leave of the court.
• Providing the court with the power to dispense with a trial if the
prosecution and defence agree that the evidence in a case
raises the defence of
mental or cognitive impairment. This should operate with judicial oversight.
Overview of the
law of insanity in Tasmania
8.2.1 The law of insanity in Tasmania has its origins in the rules laid down
in 1843 by the House of Lords’ decision in
this case, medical evidence was presented that the accused held a delusional
belief that he was being persecuted by the police,
on instruction from the Tory
party. At his trial, evidence was presented that the accused, acting under that
delusion, killed a man
believing him to be the Home Secretary, Sir Robert
M’Naghten was
acquitted on the grounds of insanity and, as a result of the considerable public
uproar that followed, the Queen
asked the House of Lords to review the matter.
In response, the House of Lords set out the rules of insanity. These rules first
a presumption of sanity by stating that ‘in all cases that every
man [or woman] is presumed to be sane, and to possess a sufficient
reason to be responsible for his [or her] crimes, unless the contrary be
rules then define the circumstances in which
a person may rely on the defence of insanity. The substance of the defence of
is contained in the statement by the House of Lords that:
To establish a defence on the ground of insanity, it must be clearly proved
that, at the time of the committing of the act, the party
accused was labouring
under such defect of reason, from disease of mind, as not to know the nature and
quality of the act he was
doing; or, if he did know it, that he did not know
what he was doing was wrong.
The House of Lords also considered the situation where a person was acting
under a delusion:
Making the assumption ... that he labours under such partial delusion only,
and is not in other respects insane, we think he must
be considered in the same
situation as to responsibility as if the facts with respect to which the
delusion exists were real. For
example, if under the influence of his delusion
he supposes another man to be in the act of attempting to take away his life,
he kills that man, as he supposes, in self-defence, he would be exempt from
punishment. If his delusion was that the deceased had
inflicted a serious injury
to his character and fortune, and he killed him in revenge for such supposed
injury, he would be liable
to punishment.
These aspects of the
rules find expression in ss 15
and 16 of the
Criminal Code
, which are set out at [
8.2.3 The theoretical basis of the defence of insanity rests on a fundamental
principle of modern criminal law — the idea of
individual responsibility:
‘the idea of the subject as a rational agent with capacities of both
cognition and self-control,
and hence the idea of criminal liability is rooted
in individual
Individual responsibility is seen to be dependent on ‘the principle of
capacity and a fair opportunity to act
otherwise’.
It is for this reason that the criminal law has long recognised ‘the
notion that ... the insane lack the ability to reason’
and this is
reflected in ‘laws excusing them from responsibility for criminal
As noted by
Lacey, Wells and Quick:
A plea of exemption such as insanity goes to the defendant’s capacity
to be addressed as a normal subject of criminal law: if
a defendant’s
mental capacity is such that fundamental questions can be raised about her
cognitive or, perhaps, volitional
capacities, it might be argued that they are
not even the kind of subject whom criminal law aspires to
Fairall and Yeo recognise that ‘the law would be unduly harsh to punish
people who are unable to choose or control their
point to the lack of utility in punishing people who are mentally impaired given
that they ‘would not have been deterred
by a threat of
punishment’.
Accordingly, the function of the law of insanity has been said to ‘define
the point at which mental disorder dissolves [criminal]
responsibility’.
8.2.4 In its consideration of the foundations of criminal responsibility, the
Law Commission of England and Wales stated that:
Criminal law is generally thought to be founded on the principle that a
person must have been responsible for his or her actions in
order to be held
culpable and to be punished.
It would be unfair for those whose serious disorders caused them to lack
criminal responsibility at the time of an alleged offence
to be at risk of the
same outcome (criminal conviction) as people without that serious condition
Accordingly, the Law Commission addressed ‘the concept of
responsibility as part of the essential inquiry: when does the fact
person has a particular condition make it unfair to hold him or her responsible
for his or her otherwise criminal
Commission concluded that, ‘people should not be held criminally
responsible for their conduct if they lack the capacity
to conform their
behaviour to meet the demands imposed by the criminal law regulating that
8.2.5 In his submission, Walvisch also considered the nature of criminal
responsibility:
a person should only be called to account for their wrongdoing if they are
responsible for that wrongdoing. At the very minimum, this
means they must be a
‘responsible agent’ — a person who can be expected to
recognise and discharge the responsibilities
laid down by the criminal law. This
requires them to be reasons-responsive: ‘a responsible agent is one who is
capable of recognising
and responding to the reasons that bear on his
situation’.
In order to be reasons-responsive, it is not just ‘a matter of acting
in conformity with appropriate reasons’, and so
failing to be a
‘responsible agent’ is described as ‘failing to exhibit a
pattern of reason-recognition that is
understandable to those who are judging
the agent’s responsibility, and that is grounded in reality’.
Walvisch wrote
in his submission that a person is not a responsible agent and so
should be exempted from criminal responsibility if at the time
of the offence,
‘the beliefs, attitudes and emotions that informed the accused’s
deliberations and actions were “so
disordered, so detached from reason,
that he was not operating within that realm at
circumstances, it is not appropriate to attribute criminal responsibility to
someone if they were incapable of understanding
the nature and scope of their
duty to avoid those actions and so are unable to be properly guided by them.
8.2.6 In addition to fairness to the individual, the protection of the public
is a (competing) rationale for the defence of insanity
with the law of insanity
being concerned to ‘protect society against recurrence of dangerous
within the insanity defence in the
Criminal Code
s 16 that operates to
separate sane from insane automatism and the forward focused nature of the
assessment made in relation to the
dispositions available under the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
The development of the law of insanity has been said to reflect a need to
identify ‘those defendants who seem more likely to
pose a danger to others
in the future’ and so those who ‘should be subject to supervision
and restraint rather than released
unconditionally’.
The current law
of insanity in Tasmania
8.3.1 In Tasmania, the presumption of sanity is
set down in s 15 of the
Criminal Code,
which provides, ‘[e]very
person is presumed to be of sound mind, and to have been of sound mind at any
time which comes in question,
until the contrary is proved’. This means
that the prosecution does not need to prove in every trial that the defendant is
sane. It can rely on the presumption of sanity until it is displaced by evidence
to the contrary.
8.3.2 The defence of insanity is set out in s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas), which provides:
(1) A person is not criminally responsible for an act done or an omission
made by him —
(a) when afflicted with mental disease to such an extent as to render him
incapable of —
(i) understanding the physical character of such act or omission; or
(ii) knowing that such act or omission was one which he ought not to do or
(b) when such act or omission was done or made under an impulse which, by
reason of mental disease, he was in substance deprived of
any power to
(2) The fact that a person was, at the time at which he is alleged to have
done an act or made an omission, incapable of controlling
his conduct generally,
is relevant to the question whether he did such act or made such omission under
an impulse which by reason
of mental disease he was in substance deprived of any
power to resist.
(3) A person whose mind at the time of his doing an act or making an omission
is affected by a delusion on some specific matter, but
who is not otherwise
exempted from criminal responsibility under the foregoing provisions of this
section, is criminally responsible
for the act or omission to the same extent as
if the fact which he was induced by such delusion to believe to exist really
(4) For the purpose of this section the term mental disease includes natural
imbecility.
Key components of the defence
Mental disease
8.3.3 Central to the operation of the insanity defence is the scope of the
concept of ‘mental disease’. In order to rely
on the defence of
insanity under ss 16(1) and (2), it must be established on the balance of
probabilities that the defendant was
suffering a mental disease. Significantly,
the definition of a mental disease is a legal rather than a medical
It is a legal
question rather than a medical question as to whether any particular condition
is a ‘mental disease’ for
the purposes of the insanity defence.
Further, it is for the jury to determine whether the accused was suffering from
a condition
that answers the description of a ‘mental
In other words, the jury must determine whether a condition is a mental disease
according to the legal definition of the term and
whether the accused actually
has that condition. However, expert medical evidence is clearly likely to be
crucial to the jury’s
determination of these issues.
8.3.4 Mental disease is not defined in the
Criminal Code
(Tas) other than to specify that it includes natural
imbecility,
meaning is found in the common law term ‘disease of the
of the mind’ is ‘a state of disease or disorder or mental
disturbance arising from infirmity, temporary
longstanding’
not include transient conditions caused by external factors unconnected with
An accepted
definition is found in the judgment of King CJ in
R v Radford
, where his
Honour stated that:
The expression ‘disease of the mind’ is synonymous, in my view,
with ‘mental illness’ ... The essential notion
appears to be that in
order to constitute insanity in the eyes of the law, the malfunction of mental
faculties called ‘defence
of reason’ in the M’Naghten rules,
must result from an underlying pathological infirmity of mind, be it of long or
duration and be it permanent or temporary, which can be properly termed
mental illness, as distinct from the reaction of a healthy
mind to extraordinary
external stimuli.
This statement focuses on the distinction to be drawn between the reaction of
a sound mind to external stimulus and the reactions
of an unsound mind
(sound/unsound mind distinction) as a basis for determining whether a condition
was a ‘disease of the mind’.
The key is whether the mental state is
caused by an external influence compared to an internal condition of the
defendant. This approach
reflects the explicit policy of the law, which is
concerned ‘that certain persons likely to be involved in repeated episodes
of dangerous behaviour should be subject to restraint rather than be given an
unqualified acquittal on the basis of automatism or
lack of mens
8.3.5 The legal tests to determine whether or not a condition comes within
the definition of a ‘disease of the mind’ have
largely been
developed to distinguish ‘sane’ and ‘insane’ automatism.
Sane automatism results in a complete
acquittal on the basis that the
prosecution has not proved beyond reasonable doubt that the act was voluntary
and intentional as
required by the
Criminal Code
s 13(1). In contrast,
‘insane’ automatism results in a qualified acquittal (not guilty by
reason of insanity) according
to the insanity defence by virtue of the
Criminal Code
The law provides that an accused cannot rely on automatism if the involuntary
conduct is attributable to a condition falling within
the legal definition of a
mental disease/disease of the mind — in this case, the defendant can only
rely on insanity.
8.3.6 The scope of the concept of a ‘mental disease’/disease of
the mind is also relevant to the distinction made between
the defences of
intoxication contained in s 17 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas), insanity and
automatism. The issue in this context is whether an accused’s involuntary
state at the time of the offence
was attributable to intoxication or a
‘mental disease’. There are limits on the relevance of intoxication
as a denial
of criminal responsibility as evidence of intoxication is not
relevant in determining whether an act is voluntary and intentional.
necessary to determine whether the accused’s state of mind was
attributable to intoxication (a temporary and external
factor) or whether it was
attributable to a ‘mental disease’. Evidence of intoxication is not
relevant to the defence
of insanity unless the person is suffering from disease
of the mind caused by intoxication, such as delirium
8.3.7 At common law, conditions that have been
accepted within the definition of mental disease include:
schizophrenia,
depression,
psychomotor
hyperglycaemia,
arteriosclerosis
post-traumatic stress
As explained, the
common thread in the application of the concept of ‘mental disease’
in these cases is that the conditions
have been held to arise from an internal
rather than an external
This approach
highlights the unusual results that can be created by the current legal test
given that ‘mental disease’
has been held to extend to conditions
that are not normally associated with mental illness, such as
arteriosclerosis (hardening of arteries causing reduced flow of blood to the
There are differing
views about whether somnambulism (sleepwalking) is a ‘disease of the
mind’ depending on the evidence,
with a Canadian court in
finding that sleepwalking was not a mental
and the English Court
of Appeal finding that it was a disease of the
In Australia, it
appears that sleepwalking has generally been regarded as involving non-insane
automatism.
8.3.8 There is uncertainty in relation to the
status of personality disorders for the purpose of the
Criminal Code
16, and their status as a mental disease appears to depend on the evidence
adduced in a particular case.
In some cases, the trial judge has refused to leave insanity on the basis of a
personality disorder,
in other cases, insanity (although not successful) has been left for the jury on
Limbs of the defence
8.3.9 Further, evidence of a mental disease alone will not provide the
defendant with a defence. Under ss 16(1) and (2), it must also
be established
that the effect of the mental disease was either that:
• the defendant did not have the capacity to
understand the physical
character of the act
• the defendant did not have the capacity to know that the
omission was one which he or she ought not do or make
• the defendant acted under an
uncontrollable impulse
Physical character of act
. This reflects the first limb of
M’Naghten’s
case that D be ‘labouring under such defect
of reason, from a disease of the mind as not to know the nature and quality of
the act he was doing.’ In
, Dixon J explained that a
person would not know the physical nature of what they were doing if he or
had so little capacity for understanding the nature of life and the
destruction of life, that to him [or her] it is no more than breaking
destroying an inanimate object. He [or she] would not know the implications and
what it really amounted to.
Illustrations of when a person does not understand the physical character of
the act include placing a baby on a fire thinking it
is a log of wood or
smashing a baby’s head believing it to be a
8.3.11 However, it is unclear whether s
16(1)(a)(i) is limited only to the physical character of the act (eg strangling
another under
the impression of squeezing a lemon) or whether it extends also to
the significance of the
It has been pointed
out in academic critique of the insanity defence that different results may
occur if a narrow (physical act only)
or a broad interpretation (physical act
and its consequence) is applied. For example, in a case where an accused hits
another person
repeatedly with a golf club to try to stop the person from
teasing him or her but does not know that the act of striking will kill,
this person rely on the defence of insanity? Applying a narrow interpretation,
the accused would not be able to rely on insanity
as they understand the
physical character of the act (hitting with the club). However, applying a
broader interpretation they would
be able to rely on insanity, as they did not
appreciate that their conduct could kill
Another example
given in textbooks of the application of a broader interpretation of the
incapacity to understand the physical character
of the act would be where a
person decapitates someone just to see what that person looks like without a
The precise scope of being
‘incapable of understanding the physical character’ of the act is
not settled in Tasmania.
There is support for the narrow view in
accused stabbed and killed the victim. Medical evidence was given at trial which
‘amounted ... to an opinion that
the appellant at the time he did this act
... was probably totally unaware that he was so acting or even unaware that he
basis for the insanity defence was that the accused’s act was not
voluntary and intentional because he had a ‘gross
personality
disorder’. In outlining the scope of the first limb of the insanity
defence, Neasey J relied on the approach taken
it has become the practice to regard a person as ‘incapable of
understanding the nature and quality’ of his act when in
truth he was not
conscious of having acted at all; and so to treat the formula as applicable to
cases of automatism.
Accordingly, Neasey J stated that if the individual ‘was totally
unaware of what he was doing, he was at that time incapable
of understanding the
physical character of his act, because he was at that time incapable of
considering the physical character of
it, or anything else about it, at
This approach
would seem to limit the scope of this limb of the insanity defence
significantly.
8.3.13 In contrast, a broader approach is evident
in the judgment of Crawford J in
Hawkins (No
where his Honour
cited the interpretation of the common law rule ‘as not to know the nature
and quality of the act’ found
interpretation encompassed the act and its consequences: ‘whether the
accused “was in such a condition that he could
not appreciate what death
amounted to or that he was bringing it about or that he was destroying life and
all that is involved in
the destruction of
8.3.14 However, it is difficult to determine the approach that the court is
likely to adopt given that this provision is rarely used
Other than the case
Williams v The Queen
(where insanity was rejected by the jury), the
TLRI is not aware of any case decided in the Supreme Court where this ground for
establishing
insanity has been successfully relied on by the defendant.
Act or omission which ought not do or
. This reflects the second limb of
M’Naghten’s
that the defendant did not know what he was doing was wrong. In
, Dixon J stated that:
The question is whether he was able to appreciate the wrongness of the
particular act he was doing at the particular time. Could this
man be said to
know in this sense whether his act was wrong if through a disease or defect or
disorder of the mind he could not think
rationally of the reasons which to
ordinary people make that act right or wrong? If through the disordered
condition of the mind
he could not reason about the matter with a moderate
degree of sense and composure it may be said that he could not know that what
was doing was wrong. What is meant by ‘wrong’? What is meant by
wrong is wrong having regard to the everyday standards
of reasonable
Accordingly, a key feature of the test is that the defendant could not think
rationally about whether the matter was right or wrong.
Further, the
defendant’s understanding of ‘wrong’ is concerned with the
defendant’s failure to understand
that the act was morally wrong rather
than the defendant’s failure to understand that the act was legally
8.3.16 It has been observed that this is the most
common basis on which insanity is
established.
In Tasmania, this was the ground of insanity used in all of the cases where
insanity was successfully argued in Supreme Court cases
identified by the TLRI
Typically, the
defence was based on the accused’s impaired judgement arising from
delusions resulting from a pre-existing mental
health impairment. For example,
in making orders under the

(Tas) in the case of
Porter J observed that
‘the mental condition and the delusions from which he suffered were of
such intensity in nature that
it impaired and reduced his ability to judge the
situation in an ordinary manner. The disorder deprived him of the capacity to
the acts were ones which he ought not to do’. In another case, the
medical evidence was that the defendant was mentally ill
and experiencing
auditory hallucinations and the crimes were committed in response to the voice
he heard and a belief that his family
would be at risk if he did not commit the
defendant’s condition caused him to experience ‘acute psychotic
symptoms including auditory and visual hallucinations
and persecutory and
referential
delusions’.
8.3.17 A further illustration of the operation of this limb of the insanity
defence (in the Western Australian context) is the case
McHenry v Western
Australia (No 2)
case, the accused killed his brother under the deluded belief that his brother
was a threat to him personally and to the
world. He believed that his brother
was an alien linked to spiders and Satan and that they were planning to blow up
the planet. By
killing his brother, he thought he would destroy the spider and
save many from Satan. He also believed he would protect his brother
from Satan.
Expert evidence was given that the accused suffered from paranoid schizophrenia
evidenced by auditory hallucinations
(hearing voices) and delusional beliefs
(religious and in extra-terrestrial aliens), and that this deprived him of the
capacity to
know that he ought not to do the act of killing. The accused was
acquitted on account of his unsoundness of mind.
Uncontrollable impulse.

Section 16(1)(b) of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) states that a person is not criminally responsible
for an act or omission ‘when such act or omission was done or made
an impulse which, by reason of mental disease, he [or she] was in substance
deprived of any power to resist’. Section
16(2) provides that:
the fact that a person was, at the time at which he is alleged to have done
an act or made an omission, incapable of controlling his
conduct generally, is
relevant to the question whether he [or she] did such act or made such omission
under an impulse which by reason
of mental disease he [or she] was in substance
deprived of any power to resist.
8.3.19 The operation of this limb of the insanity defence was considered in
the case of
, where the Court of Criminal Appeal expressed the
view that the issue for the jury was:
whether the appellant did the acts complained of under an impulse which by
reason of mental disease he was in substance deprived of
any power to resist. It
was for the appellant to satisfy the jury upon the balance of probabilities:
• that the acts were in fact done under an impulse;
• that it was an impulse which by reason of mental disease he was in
substance deprived of any power to
The degree of impairment of violation was addressed by the High Court in
O’Neill v The Queen

be established on the balance of probabilities that ‘the accused had lost
power to resist: that mental disease had left him with no power of
resistance to the impulses to do the fatal

Section 16(3) of the
Criminal Code
substantially reproduces the statement in

that a defendant’s responsibility is judged by reference to the facts as
he or she supposes them to be and not by the
It specifically refers
to the effects of delusions on a defendant’s criminal responsibility. This
provision was considered
by the Court of Criminal Appeal in
a case in which
the accused was charged with murder after he shot and killed an acquaintance at
close range. At trial, the defence
did not seek to rely on insanity. Instead,
the accused sought to rely on self-defence on the basis of a hallucinatory
belief that
at ‘the time of the discharge of the weapon the accused
believed that he was in Korea defending himself from an enemy
The accused
had been a soldier in the Korean War where he had been severely wounded by a
mortar explosion. Subsequently, he had on-going
health problems that required
hospital admission and on some occasions shock treatment. The defence sought to
adduce the accused’s
medical files that ‘showed a history consistent
with a longstanding mental disorder caused by war
experience.’
defence also sought to lead expert evidence from a psychiatrist that the accused
was suffering from post-traumatic stress disorder
and, as a manifestation of
that disorder, might have believed that he was on active service in
8.3.22 The trial judge, Slicer J, ruled that the accused’s delusions
could be relied upon under s 16(3) for the purpose of self-defence
Criminal Code
(Tas). However, his Honour held that self-defence would
only be available in this way once the jury rejected the defence of insanity
under ss 16(1) and 16(2). This could occur where the jury was not satisfied on
the balance of probabilities that the accused’s
disorder amounted to a
‘mental disease’ for the purposes of the insanity defence or that
the mental disease affected
him in one of the ways set out in ss 16(1) and (2).
In these circumstances, Slicer J ruled that the jury could consider the
delusion, pursuant to s 16(3), for the purposes of self-defence.
8.3.23 The jury convicted Walsh of murder. The Court of Criminal Appeal
rejected his appeal stating that the trial judge’s direction
accused’s deluded beliefs could be considered for the purposes of
self-defence after the defence of insanity had been
properly considered was
unduly favourable to the accused. Further, the Court considered that the
accused’s action in shooting
his acquaintance could not be regarded as a
reasonable response, if he was sane. However, the Court accepted the expert
in relation to the accused’s mental disease and quashed the
accused’s conviction for murder and substituted a verdict
of not guilty on
the grounds of insanity.
8.3.24 The Court of Criminal Appeal’s view was that the accused’s
insane delusions were not relevant to self-defence and
would only be relevant to
the defence of insanity. Only the accused’s sane beliefs could provide a
basis for the accused’s
subjective belief in the need for self-defence.
The Court considered that, if accepted by the jury, the evidence of the
deluded belief brought him within s 16(1)(a)(ii) of the
Criminal Code
, and so that limb of the insanity defence governed the
accused’s criminal responsibility. In the words of Crawford J:
On the evidence, if the appellant believed that he was being attacked or
approached by an enemy soldier or by some assailant from
Sydney the cause of
that belief was his mental disease, that is to say the post traumatic stress
disorder. If he acted in defence
of himself under the influence of such a belief
he was insane within the second limb of the M’Naghten rules because he was
affected with mental disease to such an extent as to render him incapable of
knowing that his act was one which he ought not to do
16(1)(a)(ii)).
The effect of the Court of Criminal Appeal’s decision in
is to restrict the relevance of insane delusions to the defence of insanity.
Accordingly, if the jury reject the defence of insanity,
they must judge the
reasonableness of the accused’s conduct on the basis that he is
Section 16(3) cannot be
applied to extend the relevance of an insane delusion to the defence of
self-defence. However, as discussed

in relation to the scope of s 16(3).

Criminal Code
(Tas) has been relied on.
Procedural matters
Onus of proof
8.3.26 As indicated at [
], s 15 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) contains a presumption of sanity. Thus, the onus is on the defendant
to prove that he or she was insane within s 16 on the balance
of probabilities.
This is reinforced by s 381(3) of the
Criminal Code
(Tas), which provides
that ‘the onus of proving the insanity of any such person shall be upon
the defence, but the same may
be established upon the evidence of the
prosecution’.
8.3.27 The language used in ss 381(3), 399 and 401 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) assumes that insanity can be raised by someone other than the
Section 381(3)
provides that ‘the onus of proving the insanity of any such person shall
be upon the defence, but the same may
be established upon the evidence of the
prosecution’. Section 401 of the
provides that a person
convicted may appeal. ‘Person convicted’ includes, according to s
399, ‘an accused person
who, not having set up insanity as a defence, has
been acquitted on the ground of insanity.’ Case authority establishes that
the prosecution can allege and call evidence to prove insanity if the defendant
puts his or her state of mind in issue by alleging
non-insane automatism or the
absence of intent.
the prosecution cannot introduce evidence probative of insanity until the matter
is put in issue by the
Role of judge and jury
8.3.28 As indicated, the definition of mental illness is a matter of law, but
whether the defendant suffered from a mental disease
is a question of fact. This
was set out in
where Green CJ stated
the jury have the sole responsibility for making all necessary findings of
fact and for determining the verdict. The functions of
the judge and jury are no
different in cases in which a defence of insanity has been raised. In such cases
it is for the judge to
inter alia direct the jury as to the meaning of the
expression ‘mental disease’ in s 16 but it is for the jury to decide
whether the evidence satisfies them that the accused was at the relevant time
suffering from a mental
Accordingly, it is a matter for the jury whether the defendant was affected
by a mental disease, and also whether the mental disease
deprived the defendant
of one of capacities in s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas). There is no
provision for the prosecution and the defence to agree on a verdict of not
guilty by reason of insanity.

Issues for consideration
8.7.1 The law of insanity has been controversial,
and as Norval Morris observed in 1970 (quoted in Blackwood and Warner):
of ink, mountains of printer’s lead, and forests of paper
have been expended’ on the issue of the defence of
Since that time, there have been further reviews of the law of insanity in many
criticism, the law of
insanity has remained essentially unchanged since the mid-nineteenth
Key difficulties are
in relation to the terminology used and the disconnect between the legal concept
of insanity and contemporary
psychological understanding.
8.7.2 In the discussion that follows, four options for reform are considered
with a view to ensuring that ‘the law has the right
test to distinguish
between those who should be held criminally responsible for what they have done,
and those who should not because
condition’:
(1) Make no change to the current defence of insanity
(2) Abolish the defence of insanity
(3) Abolish the current defence of insanity and introduce a replacement
(4) Reform the current defence of insanity
(5) Make no change to the current defence of insanity.
Make no change to the current defence of
8.7.3 An option would be to make no change to the current defence of
insanity. As noted, despite the extensive review undertaken of
the defence of
insanity, it has ‘weathered [this] constant
and, despite its flaws, has not changed significantly. The New Zealand Law
Commission (‘NZLC’) suggested that this intractability
may reflect
the core decision that needed to be made by a jury: ‘regardless of what
the rules may say, in the end, the question
jurors will put to themselves when
they retire is simply: “Is this man mad or
Accordingly, despite recognising the ‘not insignificant’ problems
with the defence and the outdated language, the NZLC
did not recommend reform to
the defence.
8.7.4 None of the submissions received by the Institute (which made comment
on the defence of insanity) indicated that the defence
should not be changed.
Further, based on the Institute’s analysis of the defence and its
consideration of other law reform
reports and expert commentary, the Institute
considers that reform is necessary. Accordingly, the Institute does not
recommend making
no changes to the defence of insanity.
Recommendation
22. The Institute recommends making changes to the current defence of
insanity contained in the
Criminal Code
Abolish the defence of insanity
8.7.5 An option for reform at the other extreme would be for Tasmania to
abolish the defence of insanity all together. In some states
in the United
States, the insanity defence has been
In 1989, the Law
Reform Commissioner of Tasmania recommended that the defence of insanity be
abolished and that evidence of mental
or psychological disorder be admissible as
relevant to deny the mental element for the offence in all
The abolition of the insanity defence has also been advocated by some legal
and disability
The Scottish Law Commission, the Law Commission of England and Wales, the New
Zealand Law Reform Commission, the New South Wales
Law Reform Commission and the
Law Reform Commission of Victoria have also considered the abolition of the
defence of insanity.
Commission of England and Wales was the only review to propose a provisional
abolition of the insanity defence. Nevertheless,
the Commission considered that
there is a need for a special defence of non-responsibility and rejected the
adequacy of general principles
of criminal law to address cases where an
accused’s mental disorder resulted in a total loss of capacity. This is
further at [
8.7.6 Abolition of the insanity defence (without a
comparable replacement) would mean that the criminal liability of a person with
a mental illness or intellectual disability would be determined according to the
general principles of criminal law. This would allow
evidence of mental illness
or intellectual disability to be used to deny the elements of the offence (the
voluntary and intentional
act, intention, knowledge, subjective recklessness
etc) or to establish a defence of general application (such as
self-defence).
According to
this approach, concerns about the protection of the public following an
acquittal could be resolved through civil rather
than criminal
procedures.
Reasons advanced for the abolition of the defence
8.7.7 The following reasons have been advanced in
support of the abolition of the defence:
It is inherently discriminatory based on disability and contrary to
right to equality before the law, including the right to
recognition of legal capacity
. The United Nations High Commissioner for
Human Rights has stated that:
In the area of criminal law, recognition of the legal capacity of persons
with disabilities requires abolishing a defence based on
the negation of
criminal responsibility because of the existence of a mental or intellectual
disability. Instead disability-neutral
doctrines on the subjective elements of
crime should be applied, which take into consideration the situation of the
individual defendant.
Further, it is argued that ‘[g]ranting people with mental disability a
special defence stigmatises and marginalises them. The
category of
“criminal insanity” perpetuates the extremely damaging myth that
people with mental disability are especially
dangerous or especially
uncontrollable’.
It is fundamentally flawed to argue that a person who is mentally ill
is less guilty than someone who is not mentally ill but is driven
into criminal
behaviour by circumstances beyond their control (such as social
deprivation)
argument is that there is no reason for distinguishing between different causes
of non-responsibility.
It is wrong in principle
. This argument is based on the concern
that the criminal law is being used inappropriately to detain people who have
been acquitted
on the ground that they ‘were perceived to pose a risk of
Burden of proof and consistency with general principles of criminal
It is argued that it is contrary to ‘notions of justice and fair
play’ to require ‘the accused to prove not only
that he [or she] did
but that he [or she] suffered from a total incapacity
to understand the physical nature of the
This represents a
shifting of the burden of proof to the accused and also the imposition of a more
onerous burden.
It is argued
that the abolition of the insanity defence ‘is more consistent with
general principles of criminal law’
on the basis that ‘if the
prosecution can prove the elements constituting the offence, the charge is made
out, and if it cannot,
then the accused must be
acquitted’.
8.7.8 The following reasons have been advanced to
support the retention of the defence:
The insanity verdict reflects the true ground for the
reflects the foundations of the defence as a ‘denial of criminal
responsibility due to a person’s lack of
observed that:
It is not, in fact, true to say, as the abolitionists do, that the insanity
defence is ill-founded because criminal liability in general
turns on the
presence or absence of criminal intent. The criminal law, and society more
generally, across all like-minded jurisdictions,
does recognise other
circumstances in which factual guilt, and criminal actions with intent, are
justified or excused, resulting
in acquittal, because the offender was placed in
a position in which he or she did not have a “real” choice.
Self-defence,
and duress or necessity, are
Further, there may be some individuals who were proved to have the relevant
intent at the time of the offence but where the intent
was affected by a
delusion. It is argued that it would be unfair to convict such a person where
they were ‘grossly out of touch
with reality at the time of the
It also does
not account for strict liability offences, where the prosecution does not need
to prove any intention beyond a voluntary
and intentional act.
The requirement for legal capacity under the CRPD (which refers to
one’s power to act within the legal
undermined by the insanity defence
. After considering the views expressed by
those who would advocate for the abolition of the insanity defence on human
rights grounds,
Wondemaghen argues that:
Adherence to Article 12 demands that persons with mental and psychosocial
disabilities are recognised as free agents with legal capacity
who may or may
not choose to employ the defence of insanity to negate reasoning, not as a
perpetual and continuous state, but solely
at the time of the commission of a
criminal act.
This can be contrasted with the position of children where lack of criminal
responsibility is an on-going state for children under
the age of 10 and a
rebuttable presumption for children aged between 10 and 14
For individuals who
seek to raise insanity, there is a presumption of sanity and it must be
established on the balance of probabilities
that the person had a mental
disease, which gave rise to one of the relevant incapacities at the time of the
offence. Accordingly,
it is not a permanent or on-going lack of legal capacity
based on disability. Indeed, a person may be found guilty of some offences
not guilty by reason of insanity in relation to other offences for offending
that occurs in a relatively close time
The underlying rationale for the insanity defence is still valid
This was the view expressed by the NSWLRC:
Is there still a need to provide a legal mechanism for excusing from criminal
responsibility those offenders whose mental capacity
is significantly impaired,
for protecting them from themselves or the community where their impaired mental
capacity makes them susceptible
to dangerous behaviour, and for providing them
with the opportunity for treatment, rather than
punishment?
The Commission’s view was that ‘the answer to that question is
uncontroversial, and the underlying rationale for the defence
of mental illness
remains as valid today as it did when it first
Public protection requires the courts to have special powers beyond
civil powers of

A person with mental disorder who has done what would amount to a criminal

a person who has been

on the grounds of public protection,
to detain a person who has been found to

(whether physical or
8.7.9 In the Issues Paper, the Institute sought feedback about whether the
defence of insanity should be abolished:
Should the defence of insanity in s 16 of the
Criminal Code
If you consider that the insanity defence should be abolished, do you think
that a new defence should be created, or should general
principles of criminal
responsibility apply?

(Tas) are sufficient to address

If not, what changes would be necessary?
8.7.10 Five submissions addressed the question of whether the defence of
insanity in s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) should be abolished. No
submissions called for the abolition of the defence of insanity without
replacement. In other words,
there was no support for amending the law so that
individuals who were affected by a mental health or cognitive impairment at the
time of the offence (to such an extent that the defence of insanity would apply)
would be dealt with according to the general rules
of criminal responsibility,
and the powers of civil detention under the
Mental Health Act 2013
However, the issue on which there was a greater divergence of views was whether
the current defence should be abolished and
replaced with a new defence.
8.7.11 The DPP and ForensiClinic supported the retention of s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas). The DPP wrote that ‘the provision works well
in practice. Established and well understood principles would be eroded
defence were to be abolished. I would strongly oppose any proposal to remove
this defence from the Code’. ForensiClinic
also expressed the view that
‘the insanity defence is extremely important’ but proposed reform to
the terminology used
in s 16. The need to change the terminology used in the
defence was raised in many of the submissions received by the Institute.
discussed further at [
8.7.12 On the other hand, there was support in three submissions for the
replacement of the insanity defence with a new defence —
either based on
the England and Wales Law Commission’s provisional
recommendations
introduction of a new defence based in ‘lack of
reasons-responsiveness’.
8.7.13 The introduction of a new defence based on
the England and Wales proposal was supported by EOT and the ALA. Broadly
this defence is grounded in the defence of insanity but has an
expanded operation in that it applies to physical and mental conditions
(recognised medical conditions). In addition, it employs different incapacity
The ALA’s view
was that this new defence should be introduced to replace the insanity defence.
EOT’s submission indicated
that it was ‘attracted’ to the Law
Commission proposal, but it also proposed other reforms to the current defence
the alternative. The ALA supported the reform because it would overcome
‘the disconnection between the legal definition of
“disease of the
mind” and medical understanding of mental illness, and conditions
affecting mental functioning such as
hypoglycaemia’. Similarly, the appeal
of the approach proposed by the Law Commission for EOT was that it would
clearly reflect the broad range of conditions that have previously
been captured by the umbrella of “insanity”, including
impairment and conditions such as sleep apnoea syndrome or narcolepsy’.
EOT considered that the concept of ‘recognised
medical condition’
‘would also help to raise awareness of the diversity of various disability
difference between cognitive impairment (which may
include intellectual disability, learning difficulties, acquired brain injury
and so on) and psychiatric impairments which are linked to mental
illness’. Its view is that this may enable utilisation of
a broader range
of orders than those available under the forensic mental health system.
8.7.14 Walvisch, instead, proposed a new defence
based in a lack of reasons-responsiveness. He argued that a specific legal
is required as it is inappropriate to hold individuals responsible for
their wrongdoing if they:
• Were not capable of recognising and responding to the reasons that
bore on their situation (i.e. they were not reasons-responsive);
• They were not responsible for their lack of reasons-responsiveness.
Walvisch considered that the scope of the defence should reflect its purpose
which is ‘to exculpate people who should not be
held criminally
responsible for blameless conduct’. Accordingly, his view is that the
defence should have two main requirements:
1. That the accused lacked reasons-responsiveness at the time of the offence;
2. That the accused should not be held responsible for that lack of
reasons-responsiveness.
In his view, this approach avoids many of the problems with the current law
because it avoids definitional questions and focuses solely
on the nature of the
accused’s impairment (that is, whether the person was ‘operating
outside the realm of reason at
the relevant time’) rather than its cause.
Further details of this proposal are set out from [
8.7.15 As noted above, there are competing arguments for the retention and
abolition of the defence of insanity. While a case can
be made for the abolition
of the defence, the Institute’s view is that there remains a need for a
special defence so that the
criminal justice system can respond in cases where
it is not appropriate to attribute criminal responsibility given the
individual’s
state of mind at the time of the offence (as outlined at [
]). The absence of criminal
responsibility is clear in the finding that the person is not guilty on the
basis of insanity or mental
impairment. The Institute’s view is that
abolition of the defence would give rise to unfairness because the inevitable
consequence
would be that individuals with mental or cognitive impairments would
be found guilty of offences in circumstances where their mental
state rendered
them unable or unaware of the fact that they should act otherwise. However,
having agreed that there is the need for
the defence, the Institute acknowledges
that the current law is problematic (as outlined below) and that there is a need
for reform.
The Institute’s approach is to retain and reform the

8.7.16 As indicated above, an alternative to the
complete abolition of the defence of insanity would be to replace it with an
alternative

8.7.17 In England and Wales, the Law Commission
provisionally proposed a ‘radical’ change to the
Its view was that
the defence in its current form should be abolished and that a special defence
based on the accused’s lack
of capacity at the time of the offence should
be created that would apply to physical as well as mental conditions that led to
relevant loss of capacity.
It proposed a new defence of ‘not criminally responsible by reason of a
recognised medical condition’. This defence applies
where the defendant,
as a result of a qualifying ‘recognised medical condition’, wholly
lacked the capacity to:
(a) Rationally form a judgment about the relevant conduct or circumstances
(ie ‘could not have reasoned rationally about what
he or she was
(b) Understand the wrongfulness of what he or she is charged with; or
(c) Control his or her physical acts in relation to the relevant
Accordingly, the Law Commission proposed changing the current requirement
that the defendant ‘did not know the nature and quality
of the act’
(the equivalent of s 16(1)(a)(i) of the
Criminal Code
(Tas)) to a test
based on the capacity to rationally form a judgment about conduct or
circumstances. In the Law Commission’s
view, the ability to rationally
form a judgment required at least three types of ‘cognitive
competency’: (1) an ability
to form accurate beliefs; (2) an ability to
draw on their existing wants and beliefs; and (3) ‘an accurate reasoning
that allows them to draw warranted conclusions about the probable
relationships among various wants, acts and
consequences’.
Law Commission’s view, the new test encapsulates these ideas about
rational judgment and better reflects the underlying
rationale for the
8.7.18 Another significant change contained in the
provisional proposal is the shift from ‘mental disease’ to
‘recognised
medical condition’. The Commission’s view was that
the boundary between ‘sane’ and ‘insane’ automatism
should be re-defined, so that a special verdict would be available if a mental
or a physical disorder deprived the defendant of a
relevant loss of capacity. It
outlined the following reasons for this approach:
(a) It removes the artificial division between physical and mental disorders
and focuses on the essential point, which is ‘whether
the accused had the
relevant criminal
There is no
principled reason to treat mental disorders differently from physical disorders
when they gave rise to a loss of
(b) It would reduce the stigma attached to mental illness arising from the
use of terminology of the nineteenth century by introducing
a verdict of
‘not criminally responsible by reason of a recognised medical
condition’.
‘crafted in modern terms with flexibility to accommodate developing
medical knowledge’.
(c) It would result in more appropriate
The Law Commission
provides the example of a person who is charged with causing death by dangerous
driving in circumstances where
they fell into a hypoglycaemic coma. They would
be acquitted outright but the Law Commission argued that this ‘situation
appropriately fits within a defence of not criminally responsible by reason
of a medical
condition’.
also be more appropriate than labelling as ‘insane’ people with
diabetes (hyperglycaemia) and epilepsy.
(d) It would result in more appropriate outcomes by providing the court with
powers over cases where there is a risk of reoccurrence
arising from a physical
disorder (hypoglycaemia),
such as, for example, where someone operates machinery and suffers from sleep
apnoea. If such a person harmed someone, they may have
a defence of automatism
with an acquittal and no power for preventive measures to be imposed by the
court. Under the new defence,
if the person was found ‘not criminally
responsible by reason of recognised medical condition’, ‘the court
require him or her to obtain the necessary medical treatment or
supervision’.
(e) It would address problems that arise from narrow construction of the
incapacities, particularly the fact that insanity is unavailable
where a person
has knowledge of the physical aspect of the act even if they do not understand
the moral dimension.
8.7.19 A further reason that could be advanced for supporting such an
approach is that it more readily reflects the requirement for
equality under the
by creating a defence that applies to recognised medical conditions
(both physical and mental) that give rise to a relevant incapacity.
respect, it is disability neutral in form and substance as it will not only
apply to individuals with mental health or cognitive
impairments.
8.7.20 Although the Law Commission proposed the introduction of a broad test
for the qualifying condition (recognised medical condition),

recommended for policy reasons. The Law Commission’s provisional proposals
were that there be two non-qualifying

conditions that currently fall outside the qualifying condition of a

within the scope of a
‘recognised medical condition’. In relation to acute intoxication,
the Commission’s view
was that the existing approach of the law in
relation to intoxication based on prior fault should continue to
However, this does not
exclude the possibility that alcohol dependency syndrome (as distinct from
drunkenness) may be a qualifying
In addition, the
Commission proposed, on policy grounds, that antisocial personality disorder
should not be a condition that qualifies
as a ‘recognised medical
condition’.

8.7.23 In addition, it can be asked whether it is appropriate that medical
