acquittal. Consider, for example, the situations where a driver suffers a sudden
condition that results in a loss of consciousness causing a crash and
resulting in the serious injury or death of another; or where
a person faints
and falls onto another person causing injury; or where a person commits an

8.7.27 There are two key changes that would be
made to the law in Tasmania if the Law Commission’s proposals were
(1) The requirement that the person was
afflicted with a mental
would be replaced with the requirement that the person had a
recognised medical condition
(2) Instead of being
incapable of understanding the physical character
of their act or omission, the test would be whether the accused was
of rationally forming a judgment
circumstance.
Another change would be the explicit exclusion of certain personality
disorders (anti-social personality disorder) from the definition
‘recognised medical condition’. Currently in Tasmania, personality
disorders are neither expressly included nor excluded
from the scope of a
‘mental disease’ (see [
8.7.28 Other features of the defence would remain essentially the same:
(1) a test based on understanding of the wrongfulness of the conduct (knowing
the act or omission was one which he or she ought not
to do or make)
(2) a test based on incapacity to control conduct
(3) the exclusion from the defence of temporary drug induced psychosis.
8.7.29 Another model for reform (as indicated at
]) was suggested by Walvisch
— the defence of ‘lack of reasons-responsiveness’. In terms of
assessing a person’s
lack of reasons-responsiveness, Walvisch says the
‘key issue is whether,
at the time of the offence
accused’s psychological condition was such that the accused was not
functioning as a responsible agent
in relation to the offending
’. Expressed differently, this means that:
we need to ask whether, at the time of the relevant conduct, the beliefs,
attitudes and emotions that informed the accused’s
deliberations and
actions were ‘within the realm of reason; or were they so disordered, so
detached from reason, that he was
not operating within that realm at
8.7.30 This proposal is different from the current approach in s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) in several ways:
(1) There is no qualifying condition — ‘the defence should be
available to
who non-culpably lacked reasons-responsiveness,
regardless of cause’. This would include personality disorders, substance
or physical illness. However, the defence would not be available in cases of
‘self-induced reasons responsiveness’ —
where the lack of
reasons responsiveness at the time of the offence can be traced back to an
earlier time when the individual made
a decision while they were
reasons-responsive. This may arise in cases where the accused’s lack of
reasons-responsiveness is
attributable to their own behaviour such as a failure
to take prescribed medication or the decision to take illicit drugs. However,
this prior ‘reasons-responsiveness’ would not preclude the defendant
from relying in the defence in all cases but would
require a balancing of the
circumstances in each
(2) The tests of incapacity would be changed to a focus on the person’s
lack of reasons-responsiveness (capable of recognising
and responding to the
reasons that bear on his/her situation). In Walvisch’s words:
a person will generally not be responsible if he or she is unable to
recognise how reasons fit together, to see why one reason is
stronger than
another, or to understand how the acceptance of one reason as sufficient implies
that a stronger reason must also be
sufficient.
While this proposed defence is expressed very differently from the current
tests of incapacity in s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas), Walvisch’s
view is that ‘many (if not all) of the individuals captured by the current
defence would continue to
be captured by this revised defence’. This is
because ‘that understanding the physical and moral nature of one’s
conduct, and being able to act in accordance with one’s deliberations,
constitute essential elements of reasons-responsiveness’.
In addition, it
would appear under the model that individuals whose lack of
reasons-responsiveness can be attributed to prior fault
(such as drug taking or
drinking) would be excluded from the defence.
8.7.31 As outlined above, the Institute
acknowledges that there are benefits in adopting the English or Walvisch’s
However, as the DPP has indicated, the law of insanity reflects
well-established legal principles and to move to a completely new
and untested
approach may create new and unforeseen difficulties in practice, where no
difficulties currently exist. While the Institute
accepts that there are
problematic aspects in the current defence, it is preferable to try to resolve
those difficulties rather than
introduce a new defence. The Institute’s
view is that the legislative statement of the scope of the defence serves an
function of community education, and so the qualifying condition
should not be too broad. Accordingly, after considering the alternative
proposed by the Law Commission of England and Wales and Walvisch, the
Institute’s view is that the preferred approach
is to retain the current
defence of insanity but make reforms to address its defects.

(Tas) should not

overview of these concerns is set out
below as well as the feedback received on

• To change the name of the defence of insanity
• To change the scope of the qualifying conditions for the defence
including the terminology used
• To clarify the interrelationship between insanity and
self-defence.

mental illness but on a
distinct legal criterion of
responsibility’.

inappropriate and outdated terminology such as ‘insanity’,

the concept of a ‘disease of the mind’.
These terms are considered
to be stigmatising, and, as recognised by the Law Commission of England and
Wales, not medical terms but
rather outdated legal

8.7.41 In the Issues Paper, the Institute noted

specifies that mental illness ‘does not include a

8.7.49 Another approach to addressing the deficiencies in using outdated
concepts of ‘disease of the mind’, ‘defect
and ‘mental disease’ is found in the review of the law conducted by
the NSWLRC. It recommended the introduction
of a detailed definition of
‘mental health impairment’ and ‘cognitive impairment’.
It was recommended that
mental health impairment be defined as follows:
(a) Mental health impairment means a temporary or continuing disturbance of
thought, mood, volition, perception, or memory that impairs
emotional wellbeing,
judgment or behaviour, so as to affect functioning in daily life to a material
(b) Such mental health impairment may arise from but is not limited to the
(i) anxiety disorders
(ii) affective disorders
(iii) psychoses
(iv) substance induced mental disorders.
“Substance induced mental disorders” include ongoing mental
health impairments such as drug-induced psychoses, but do
not include substance
abuse disorders (addiction to substances) or the temporary effects of ingesting
substances.
For the purposes of this section “mental health impairment” does
not include a personality disorder.
8.7.50 The NSWLRC further recommended that
cognitive impairment be defined as:
(a) an ongoing impairment in comprehension, reason, adaptive functioning,
judgement, learning or memory that is the result of any
damage to, dysfunction,
developmental delay, or deterioration of the brain or mind.
(b) Such cognitive impairment may arise from, but is not limited to, the
(i) intellectual disability
(ii) borderline intellectual functioning
(iii) dementias
(iv) acquired brain injury
(v) drug or alcohol related brain damage
(vi) autism spectrum disorders.
The NSWLRC argued that the definitions are appropriate for the defence of
mental illness because they: (1) capture the appropriate
people; (2) are
consistent with the definitions recommended for diversion and bail; (3) reflect
contemporary psychological and psychiatric
understandings; (4) are respectful of
people with such impairments; and (5) are tighter and more precise than the
current outdated
terminology.
The NSWLRC considered that the fit between cognitive impairments and
‘disease of the mind’ is not a good one, and that
it should be made
clear that the defence applies to people with cognitive
impairments.
8.7.51 The NSWLRC addressed concerns that such a broad test is inappropriate
as the basis for an exculpatory defence. It argued that
the broad test is
appropriate as it is only the preliminary ‘gate’ that the defendant
must pass to succeed in the defence,
and that ‘defendants must also pass
through a second, and much narrower, “gate” by demonstrating the
required nexus
between their impairment and the

8.7.53 There was a range of viewpoints expressed in the submissions, but a
common view was that the terminology is outdated and inappropriate.
caused by the inappropriate use of language relate to stigma, and also to the
failure of the defence to reflect contemporary
understanding of mental
impairment or cognitive impairment.
8.7.54 The stigma caused by the use of language was identified by the
Outdated and offensive terms such as ‘mental disease’ and
‘natural imbecility’ significantly contribute to
the systemic
stigmatisation of people with mental illness and intellectual disability in our
communities. These outdated terms do
not communicate dignity and self-respect
for individuals experiencing mental ill health or cognitive impairment.
The term ‘mental disease’, as explained by MHCT ‘is not a
term commonly used within the community-managed mental
health sector, or across
the system in a contemporary setting’. Similarly, O’Connell wrote
that, ‘all terms associated
with a s16 defence do need review including
insanity and imbecility due to the stigma they attract.’
8.7.55 The submissions also identified issues arising from the language used
beyond its stigmatising effect. EOT wrote that:
As outlined in the Issues Paper the use of terms such as ‘mental
disease’ and ‘natural imbecility’ has led
to a body of case
law that is both arbitrary and inaccurate. It has the capacity to, for example,
capture actions arising from the
side-effects of diabetes such as hyperglycaemia
or epilepsy as ‘mental diseases’. Not only is the use of the term
disease’ inaccurate, in the context of making out an
insanity defence it has the capacity to be highly stigmatising
ForensiClinic, noted that:
the term ‘mental disease’ does cause a problem in practice, as
many mental health/cognitive impairments are not the result
of disease, and a
medical expert is not necessarily best placed to assess the implications of an
impairment on criminal behaviour
and associated defence. For example, a medical
expert may not be an expert in the assessment of intellectual disability.
O’Connell wrote that:
The term mental disease does cause some difficulty in practice and there
exists a tension between medical concepts and the law. This
is especially true
with the rise in cases of serious psychosis (in the absence of an underlying
mental illness) as a result of methamphetamine

The Charter

Mental Health Act 2013

(1) For the purposes of this Act –
(a) a person is taken to have a mental illness if he or she experiences,
temporarily, repeatedly or continually –
(i) a serious impairment of thought (which may include delusions); or

8.7.60 Walvisch approached the issue from the perspective of the underlying
purpose of the defence, which is primarily ‘to identify
individuals who
lacked reasons-responsiveness at the time of the offence, and so should not be
held responsible (in the absence of
prior fault)’. Walvisch argued that
the focus of lack of reasons-responsiveness meant that it is not necessary to
address the
cause of an accused’s impairment but rather its nature. In
Walvisch’s view, this approach overcomes many of the problems
current law (and many alternative suggestions) and would appear to be compliant
as it is disability neutral. Walvisch argued that
‘[w]hile in most cases an individual’s lack of
reasons-responsiveness
will result from a mental illness or cognitive
impairment, this need not be the case: the defence should be available to anyone
non-culpably lacked reasons-responsiveness, regardless of the cause’.
Accordingly, it isn’t necessary to consider if
the disorder was internally
or externally caused, whether the accused ‘suffered from a mental illness
or a personality disorder,
or whether the accused’s use of drugs caused
his or her disorder or triggered an underlying pathology. Instead, the focus
be squarely on the key question: did the accused lack
reasons-responsiveness’.
8.7.61 There was cautious support for the inclusion of more detailed
definitions in some of the submissions. The DPP considered that
‘mental illness’ and ‘intellectual disability’
‘may ... benefit from non-exhaustive definitions.
I would recommend
consultation with appropriate medical experts in considering appropriate
definitions’.
8.7.62 EOT wrote that:
We are supportive of the inclusion of statutory definitions covering these

flexible enough, however, to capture evolving understanding of medical
conditions. For example, as cognitive impairment
associated with dementia
evolves a more detailed understanding is developing regarding how it can impact
on behaviour.
8.7.63 The MHCT expressed its agreement with the NSWLRC’s
recommendation of separating the definitions of mental health and

The descriptions are aligned with contemporary understandings and provide a
more updated and respectful use of terminology. Utilising
such terminology
within court proceedings would support more respectful language within court
documents, media reporting and subsequently
within the community.

8.7.65 After considering the literature and the submissions received, the
Institute agrees that the language in the
Criminal Code
(Tas) should be
reformed. The more difficult question is how it should be amended. In this
regard there is little uniformity in the

proposals and the submissions to the Institute. However, it is possible to
identify a number
of approaches to reform:
Minimal reform
. This would involve changing the language in
Criminal Code
(Tas) but largely preserving the existing approach to
the scope of qualifying mental states. Precedents for this approach are found
South Australia, the Commonwealth, the Australian Capital Territory, the
Northern Territory and Western Australia, where the defence
has been changed to
mental impairment, which is defined to include mental illness, intellectual
disability or a senility (or words
to a similar effect). In these jurisdictions,
the definition of mental illness generally reflects the pre-existing test of
of mind’ from
This approach
has the advantage of retaining the pre-existing case law on the scope of the
qualifying conditions. However, it does
not resolve the difficulties and
anomalies created by the internal/external cause test. These criticisms also
apply to the approach
of the VLRC, which defined mental impairment to include
mental illness (which was to incorporate the common law notion of ‘disease
of the mind’). However, it could be argued that the current anomalies (for
example, hypo/hyperglycaemia) are well-understood
and that any new definition
will give rise to new ‘anomalies and that its novelty would cause more
uncertainty than presently
However, other
concerns with this approach are that it does not appear to address the problems
of disparity between legal and medical
understandings of mental illness or
claims that such a defence is discriminatory and contrary to the
Remove the requirement for extraordinary stimuli to be

out above) by adopting the Western Australian definition of mental
illness that
does not specify that the extraordinary stimuli must be external. Instead, as

the reaction of a healthy mind

healthy mind’s reaction to

the defence&#

On this basis, it is arguable that the
exclusion of the word ‘external’ from the test would mean
involuntariness arising
from such conditions would be dealt with as automatism
rather than as a mental illness for the purposes of the mental impairment
defence. This seems appropriate and preferable to option (1). However, it is
uncertain whether conditions such as diabetes and epilepsy

8.7.74 After considering these approaches to reform and the submissions
received, the Institute’s view is that the most appropriate

disorders from the insanity defence:
• The inclusion of personality disorders would open the floodgates as
many people who commit crimes have personality disorders.
Its view was that to
include personality disorders is to throw ‘the net too wide. In
particular, ... [it is not] appropriate
for those with anti-social personality
disorder or psychopathy to be exculpated substantially because of their criminal
behaviour.’
• It is against the weight of community opinion, the approach in the
majority of Australian jurisdictions and academic expert
• The evidence base for policy development needs to be developed as
‘the psychiatric understandings of personality disorders,
precision with which they are defined, is not sufficient to allow their
inclusion with any degree of confidence at this present
8.7.78 The NSWLRC also set out reasons in favour of the inclusion of
personality disorders as follows:
• The gate can be narrowed by the requirement that the personality
disorder is severe, and further that the ‘person must
pass through the
second, and narrower, gate and show that the personality disorder had the effect
that he or she did not know what
they were doing, or know that it was wrong, or
that he or she was unable to control their
• There is no evidence of over-reliance on the defence in jurisdictions
that allow personality disorders to be
Other reasons identified in favour of including (or at the least not
excluding) personality disorders include the view that particular
should not be singled out in legislation for exclusion, and that there should be
flexibility to consider whether a particular
condition, including a personality
disorder, qualifies for the
The Law Commission
of England and Wales also noted that it can be difficult to distinguish
personality disorders from other mental
8.7.79 As indicated, another approach is neither to expressly exclude nor

the approach in New Zealand, where the NZLC recommended no change be made to

8.7.80 In the Issues Paper, the Institute sought feedback on the approach
that should be taken to personality disorders:
Should the definition of mental impairment include some or all personality
disorders or expressly exclude some or all personality
disorders, or should the
definition not specifically refer to personality disorders? If the definition of
mental impairment is to
distinguish between personality disorders, which should
be included or excluded from the scope of s 16?
8.7.81 There were different views about the approach that should be taken to
personality disorders in the submissions received.
8.7.82 The DPP did not support the express inclusion of any personality
This is consistent with the majority of other jurisdictions which do not
include personality disorders in the statutory definition
of mental impairment.
There are a range of personality disorders and there is a divergence of opinion
whether or not they amount
to a psychiatric illness. For example, the Victorian
Supreme Court of Appeal found that the Verdins principles were inapplicable
personality disorders in
DPP v O’Neill
[2015] VSCA 325.
inclusion of personality disorders may open the floodgates. The NSWLRC, as
summarised in 7.5.29 of the paper, stated the “evidence
base for policy
development needs to be developed as the psychiatric understandings of
personality disorders, and the precision with
which they are defined, is not
sufficient to allow their inclusion with any degree of confidence at the present
time.” However,
if a person with personality disorder otherwise fits the
criteria for the defence as provided in s 16 of the Code, the defence should
available to them.
O’Connell also did not support the inclusion of specific personality
8.7.83 On the other hand, EOT and the MHCT did not recommend the express
exclusion of personality disorders. EOT wrote that:
Exclusion of personality disorders risks scoping out some major conditions
that might ordinarily be associated with mental health
impairment, including
paranoia, schizophrenia and/or borderline personality disorder. Accordingly EOT
does not support excluding
these conditions.
Similarly, MHCT did not support express exclusion. MCHT highlighted the high
prevalence of co-morbidity of personality disorders with
other mental health
conditions as well as the ‘complexity of personality disorders and that
the condition is a diagnosed mental
illness’. MHCT also noted that meeting
the criteria for a mental health impairment does not mean that the defence would
successfully made out, as the defence has a second component (in terms of the
relevant incapacities) and that narrows the gate.
8.7.84 The ALA addressed the issue of personality disorders in the context of
a new defence applying to ‘recognised medical
conditions’. As noted
], its view was that policy
considerations do not justify excluding anti-social personality disorders.
8.7.85 Similarly, Walvisch’s approach would not exclude personality
disorders, as the focus should be on the nature of the accused’s
impairment rather than its cause. In his proposal, the additional threshold for
the operation of the defence of lack of reasons-responsiveness
would provide a
difficult threshold to meet — namely that the accused ‘must have
been operating outside the realm of
reason at the relevant time’. Further,
under this approach, the defendant would be precluded from relying on the
defence due
to prior fault (for example, in some cases where the person had
failed to take medication or otherwise manage their mental health).
8.7.86 After considering the submissions received and the different
approaches and views on the issue, the Institute’s view
is that the
legislative definition of mental impairment and cognitive impairment should
neither expressly include or exclude personality
disorders from the defence.
This provides flexibility in relation to whether a particular disorder qualifies
for the defence in the
circumstances of the case and reflects the current
approach in Tasmania.
Substance use
. Currently, s 17(1) of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) recognises that intoxication may bring an accused within the insanity
rules contained in the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16 if the accused’s
intoxication has caused a disease of the mind. This is relatively
uncontroversial. It is also uncontroversial
that a person who is temporarily
intoxicated as a result of substance use should rely on the intoxication defence
(and not the defence
of insanity), However, as the TLRI’s report on
self-defence noted, there are complexities that arise from the interaction of
the defences of intoxication, insanity and self-defence in cases where a person
experiences substance related
8.7.88 In Carroll et al’s analysis of drug-associated psychosis and
criminal responsibility, the authors have identified at
least four ways in which
drug-associated psychosis may arise:
(1) a person may experience a drug induced psychotic episode that is part of
an intoxication syndrome that resolves rapidly with
the excretion of the drug
from the body;
(2) a person may experience relatively short-lived psychotic symptomatology
due to the direct psychological effects of an ingested
substance and the
symptoms may persist for a short period (days or weeks) after the excretion of
the substance;
(3) a person’s use of drugs may be associated with the development of a
psychotic illness that then has an independent long-term
(4) a person with an established psychotic illness may engage in substance
abuse, which appears to precipitate psychotic
Currently, in Tasmania, defendants can rely on the insanity defence if they
suffer from a disease of the mind caused by intoxication
and this would
encompass (3) and (4) where a person has an independent psychotic
However, issues
remain as to the appropriateness of excluding an accused from the insanity
defence where his or her psychosis is
attributable to the intoxicating effects
of the substance or short-term symptoms associated with drug use, (the states of
(1) and (2)) which may give rise to ‘temporary
insanity’).
8.7.89 The weight of commentary by academics and law reform bodies has taken
the view that there should be a distinction between self-induced
independent
conditions resulting from substance use (which should be included in the
definition of mental disorder) and the temporary
effects of ingesting drugs
(which should be excluded from the definition of mental
For example, in
its review the NSWLRC recommended that ‘substance induced mental
disorders’ be included within the definition
of mental health impairment
but that this should not include substance abuse disorders (addiction to
substances) or the temporary
effects of ingesting substances. It drew a
distinction between casual intoxication, substance use disorder or addiction,
and ongoing
cognitive and mental impairments induced by the use of such
substances.
intoxication refers to the temporary effects of the use of alcohol, drugs and
other substances and its relationship to criminal
responsibility is contained in
the law of intoxication as set out the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 17.
Substance use disorder refers ‘to the abuse of, and dependence on, drugs,
alcohol and/or other substances “to
the extent that the person’s
functioning is
affected.”’
Accordingly, the NSWLRC indicated that substance use disorder is:
distinguished from casual substance use or temporary intoxication. It is also
to be distinguished from a substance-induced mental
disorder, an impairment that
is caused by a person’s “substance use, abuse, intoxication or
withdrawal”. Such disorders
can be the product of long-term abuse of drugs
and alcohol or, in some instances, can be triggered after a single
It indicated that this approach would include within the definition of mental
health impairment ongoing mental health impairments
such a long-term drug
induced psychiatric disorders like Korsakoff’s Syndrome and drug induced
8.7.90 The NSWLRC stated that the approach it had taken is consistent with
the common law and the exclusion of the ‘reaction
of a healthy mind to
extraordinary external stimuli, including psychoactive substances’ from
the definition of ‘disease
This approach
would appear to encompass (as with Tasmania) the states set out in (3) and (4)
above within the definition of mental
health impairment. However, it appears
that a temporary drug induced psychosis (being the temporary effect of ingesting
substances)
would be excluded. This reflects the common law approach that
‘a drug induced psychosis (unaccompanied by a separate psychiatric
illness) does not constitute a disease of the mind as understood at common
8.7.91 In its consideration of the issue, the VLRC
also expressed the view that ‘self-induced conditions that result from the
temporary effects of ingesting substances’ should be excluded from the
definition of mental impairment. However, it considered
that self-induced
independent conditions that result from ingesting substances that persist after
the drugs have left the person’s
system should be included in the
definition.
acknowledged that this distinction would create considerable practical
difficulties for mental health experts in cases where
it is difficult to
determine whether the person was suffering from the temporary effects of
substance use or a permanent
For example, as
Bourget has written, ‘it is not often possible to distinguish
substance-induced psychosis from a first-episode
psychosis in the context of a
primary mental disorder due to the very high level of
comorbidity.’
Nevertheless, her view was that the distinction is supported on sound public
policy grounds.
8.7.92 The definition of ‘mental impairment’ in the
Law Consolidation Act 1935
(SA) also excludes intoxication, which is defined
as ‘a temporary disorder, abnormality or impairment of the mind that
from the consumption or administration of intoxicants and will pass on

This approach is

8.7.96 Walvisch also considered that there is no need to exclude drug-induced

8.7.97 The Institute agrees with the position of the DPP and considers that
it is appropriate to make a distinction between drug-induced
psychosis that
arises from the temporary effects of drug use and ongoing mental impairments
arising from drug use. This reflects
the current position in Tasmanian law and
the recommendations of the Institute in relation to the qualifying condition and
definitions are not meant to result in a change to this approach.
This is important from a policy point of view and prevents reliance
on a defence
of mental or cognitive impairment where the reason for the person’s
psychosis is substance use (unaccompanied
by a separate psychiatric illness).
Accordingly, as with the VLRC and the NSWLRC, the Institute’s view is that
self-induced
conditions that result from the temporary effects of ingesting
substance should be excluded from the defence.
8.7.98 Based on this discussion, the Institute’s view is the defence of
mental and cognitive impairment should apply to ongoing
mental impairments
arising from substance use. In the words of the NSWLRC, this would include
‘a person who has a psychiatric
disorder at the time of the offence,
albeit one brought on by abusing
substances’.
it should not include any temporary conditions resulting from the effects of
ingesting substances. It should also not include
substance use disorders
(addiction to substances).

28. The qualifying condition contained in the

impairment with detailed definition of these

29. The definition of mental and cognitive impairment should be based on
the definitions set out by the NSWLRC as follows:
Mental impairment means a temporary or continuing disturbance of thought,
mood, volition, perception, or memory that impairs emotional
wellbeing, judgment
or behaviour, so as to affect functioning in daily life to a material extent.
Such mental impairment may arise from but is not limited to the following:
(i) anxiety disorders
(ii) affective disorders
(iii) psychoses
(iv) substance induced mental disorders.
‘Substance induced mental disorders’ include ongoing mental
impairments, but do not include substance abuse disorders
(addiction to
substances) or the temporary effects of ingesting substances.
Cognitive impairment means an ongoing impairment in comprehension, reason,
adaptive functioning, judgement, learning or memory that
is the result of any
damage to, dysfunction, developmental delay, or deterioration of the brain or
Such cognitive impairment may arise from, but is not limited to, the
(i) intellectual disability
(ii) dementias
(iii) acquired brain injury
(iv) drug or alcohol related brain damage
(v) autism spectrum disorders.

Narrow scope of incapacities
. It has been argued that some
‘persons with medical conditions whom we would regard as insane are
outside the scope of the
because of the very narrow interpretation of the relevant incapacities set out
in the insanity defence. As noted at [7.3.14],
the first limb of the insanity
defence (that the person does understand the physical character of their act) is
rarely used because
of its narrow interpretation. The Law Commission of England
and Wales has observed that it is very rare for a person not to know
and quality of his or her actions in a physical sense (as distinct from the
moral aspects of their conduct) and that,
further, its ‘exclusive focus on
cognitive questions excludes other sorts of problems in the functioning of minds
and brains,
such as mood disorders or emotional
The limited
utility of this limb of the insanity defence has also been noted by other legal
commentators.
8.7.100 In addition, in Tasmania the tests are focussed on people’s
‘capacity’ to know or understand rather than
their actual knowledge
or understanding. This is also the position in Queensland and Western
The NSWLRC has
observed that capacity tests may create problems ‘because a person with a
mental illness may have capacity at
one time but not at another, or may have
capacity to understand some things but not
In contrast,
in other jurisdictions, the test is whether the accused knew the nature or
quality of the conduct or that the conduct
Yeo has written
The element of incapacity is narrower than lack of knowledge because it is
possible for a person to generally possess the cognitive
capacity to know the
nature of his or her act or that it was wrong, but not to have known of it at
the time when the crime was
Yeo suggests that courts have taken a pragmatic approach to the issue and
avoided applying an overly restrictive approach to ‘incapacity’,
has expressed the view that:
Ideally, however, the word “incapacity” should be avoided so as
to enable the defence to succeed so long as the accused
was dispossessed of the
relevant mental faculty at the time of the offence, even if he or she might have
possessed such a faculty
on other occasions.
However, the precise difference between ‘capacity’ and
‘knowledge’ is unclear. For example, the VLRC has written
‘there is some contention over whether a “knowledge” or
“capacity” approach is more
restrictive’.
8.7.101 As indicated, there is also uncertainty about whether an
understanding of the physical character of the act relates just to
character of the act or whether it extends to ‘the capacity to know and
understand the significance of the act’
or the ‘consequences of the
Allnutt, Samuels and O’Driscoll have suggested that the broader view (for
example, that would require an understanding that
a stabbing could result in
death) is reflected in the Queensland and WA criminal codes, which refer to the
‘capacity to understand
what the accused is

8.7.104 While the Institute notes that the scope of the limbs in s 16(1)(a)

understanding of the physical character

One interpretation is that s 16(1)(a)(i) is limited only to the physical
character of the act and on this narrow view, the defence
is only available if
the person was totally unaware of what he or she was doing. The broader view
(and one reflecting the common
law) is that the defence is available if the
defendant is incapable of understanding the nature and quality of the act. This
interpretation
extends to understanding the act and its consequences. It is the
Institute’s view that it is not desirable for the law to be
uncertain and
that there should be reform to make the interpretation clear. Further, the
Institute considers that s 16(1)(a)(i) should
be amended to refer to an
incapacity to understand the nature and quality of the act. This is consistent
with the law in most other

Irresistible impulse
. Concerns have also been raised in
relation to the ‘irresistible impulse’ limb of the insanity defence
in s 16(1)(b)
Criminal Code
(Tas). This has been described as the
‘most controversial’ aspect of the insanity defence because:
it is difficult to determine objectively whether or not the action is the
consequence of ‘an irresistible impulse or an impulse
not resisted’,
that is, whether or not the person was genuinely incapable of controlling the
behaviour or whether or not he
or she chose not to inhibit the
In addition to the difficulty of determining whether an accused was unable or
unwilling to resist the impulse, Bronitt and McSherry
criticise the
‘volitional’ limb as being contradictory to modern psychology as it
requires the separation of cognition
and action: it ‘assume[s] that a
person can know what he or she is doing is wrong, yet be unable to control his
or her actions’.
is more forcefully stated by Kenny, who asserts that ‘the notion of
irresistible impulse is an incoherent piece of
8.7.107 The majority of Australian jurisdictions have a volitional element in
the insanity defence.
However, it does not exist in Victoria and New South Wales. This issue has also
been subject to review, and as noted by the SASAC,
‘of the six reviews
which have recently addressed this issue, three recommended incorporating a
volitional element ... (NSW,
WA and England) and three opposed its adoption
(Victoria, New Zealand and
Scotland)’.
8.7.108 Reasons for the inclusion of a volitional element are that:
(a) It exists in most other jurisdictions.
(b) Even if it may be difficult to differentiate, there are people who are
genuinely unable to control behaviour and as a matter of
principle, such people
should be exculpated.
8.7.109 Reasons for rejecting a volitional element are that:
(a) It is difficult to differentiate between irresistible impulses and
non-resisted impulses.
(b) The other limbs of the insanity defence are sufficient to accommodate
cases where a defendant was unable to control his or her
conduct, and so a
volitional element is
unnecessary.
8.7.110 In the Issues Paper, the Institute sought feedback on whether the
volitional test in the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16(1)(b) should be retained
or amended.

Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16(1)(b) should be retained?

Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16(1)(b)?

was in substance deprived of
any power to resist.

Section 16(3): The interrelationship between
insanity and self-defence
8.7.122 Another area of concern in relation to the
law of insanity in Tasmania is the role of s 16(3) of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) and the interrelationship of the insanity and self-defence. The law of
self-defence in s 46 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) provides that a person is
justified in using such force in defence of him or herself or another person as
is reasonable in the
circumstances as the defendant believes them to be. The
test for self-defence requires that: (1) the defendant believes that he or
was acting in self-defence; and (2) the force used by the defendant was
reasonable in the circumstances that the defendant believed
to exist. In cases
where the accused’s perception of the need to use defensive force
corresponds with the actual need to do
so, the application of the law of
self-defence is unproblematic. However, difficulties arise when there is a
difference between the
actual circumstances and the circumstances as the accused
mistakenly believed them to be. And, in the context of this review, key
are: (1) whether an accused can rely on a mistaken belief arising from a
delusion caused by a mental illness for the purposes
of self-defence; and (2)
the role of s 16(3) of the
Criminal Code
8.7.123 These issues are a focus of the Terms of Reference for this Report
and, as discussed at [
], were the
subject of a previous TLRI law reform report. Accordingly, in this section, the
TLRI sets out its previous views and stakeholder
necessary to revisit these recommendations.

Relating to Self-defence

Is s 16(3) limited to insane delusions or does it apply to delusions
more generally?
On the basis that s 16(3) does not refer to insane
delusions, some commentators have suggested that ‘specific delusions may
be exempt from criminal responsibility even though they are not caused by mental
Read in this
way, s 16(3) could apply to a delusion arising from drug-induced psychosis
rather than from an underlying mental disease.
However, the opposing view is
that s 16(3) should be read as applying only to delusions that are produced by a
mental disease. This
interpretation is supported by the location of s 16(3)
within the provision that sets out the insanity defence. This issue is
unresolved.
If an accused is able to rely on a sane delusion under s 16(3) as a
basis for raising self-defence, is the accused entitled to a complete
qualified acquittal?
In Tasmania, reliance on s 16(3) has more commonly been
understood to provide the accused with a basis for raising self-defence under
46, and if the prosecution cannot prove beyond reasonable doubt that the
accused’s use of force was excessive, the accused
would receive a complete
position is that s 46 works within s 16 to provide the accused with an
additional way of proving insanity and obtaining
a qualified acquittal; that is,
not guilty by reason of
8.7.125 Accordingly, the TLRI expressed the view that there is a need to
clarify the interaction of the law of self-defence and
8.7.126 The Terms of Reference for this current review ask the TLRI to
provide advice as to whether evidence of insane delusions arising
from mental
illness should form the basis for self-defence. This has previously been
addressed by the TLRI, and it was recommended
Criminal Code
(Tas) be amended to provide that if a person does an
act or makes an omission as a result of a delusion caused by a mental disease,
the delusion can only be used as a defence under s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) and cannot be relied on to support a defence of self-defence under s
Criminal Code
8.7.127 This recommendation reflects the TLRI’s view that to allow all
evidence of mental illness to be relevant to self-defence
is contrary to
community expectations and does not reflect the respective purposes of the
insanity defence and the defence of self-defence.
While, as a matter of policy,
it may be accepted that an accused should be able to rely on sane though
unreasonable mistakes for
the purposes of self-defence, it does not follow that
an accused should similarly be entitled to rely on insane delusions. As
by Baker, a belief arising from a delusion is not a
Further, in the case of a (sane) mistake the jury can apply their common sense
and may refuse to accept a patently absurd and unreasonable
mistake as one that
is not genuinely held. This ‘reality testing’ is unlikely to be
possible for an insane delusion.
This means that the prosecution will only be
able to establish beyond reasonable doubt that the accused was not acting in
self-defence
by proving either that the delusion was not one of
immediate/imminent harm
that the force used was excessive in the circumstances as the accused believed
them to be. But exactly how would the prosecution
be able to do this? It would
require them to engage with the insane delusion as though it had some basis in
reality. This gives rise
to the possibility of trials in which the
‘facts’ to be established would consist entirely of the content of
Similarly, asking jurors to assess the reasonableness of an accused’s
response to an insane delusion obliges them to engage
in fantastical reasoning.
This begs the question, just how can a jury realistically or rationally assess
the reasonableness of a
response generated by an insane
Additionally, to
permit complete acquittals in cases of insane delusions does not enable
appropriate treatment to be provided to
deluded defendants or take into account
the need for community
protection.
8.7.128 Illustrative cases where a defendant relied on insanity but where the
nature of the delusion meant that the defendant believed
that he was acting in
self-defence include:
(a) A case where the accused suffered from severe schizophrenia and was
described by a forensic psychologist as ‘seriously unwell’.
accused’s condition caused acute psychotic symptoms including auditory and
visual hallucinations and persecutory and referential
delusions. The accused
believed that the police officers were trying to kill them.
(b) A case where the accused suffered from schizophrenia, with the most
prominent symptoms being a belief that family members were
being impersonated,
delusions and other abnormal beliefs. The accused repeatedly hit and injured
their family member, believing that
the person was impersonating their family
member (rather than actually being the family member). The accused believed the
‘impersonator’
was dangerous as the accused believed the
‘impersonator’ had killed the family member.
(c) A case where the accused threatened police officers because, due to a
psychotic illness, the accused believed that the police
were a source of
profound danger.

Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16(3) and recommended that it should be

8.7.144 Walvisch also stressed the need to distinguish between self-defence
and beliefs attributable to mental illness:
In addressing this issue, it is useful to consider the differing purposes of
the defences of traditional (non-mistaken) self-defence
and mistaken
self-defence. Traditional self-defence is a defence of justification: the
accused is acquitted because he or she acted
correctly in the circumstances, all
things considered. By contrast, an accused who acts on the basis of mistaken
self-defence has
not acted in a justified fashion: when all things are
considered, he or she acted wrongfully (due to the mistake). However, as the
accused has acted in response to what reasonably appeared to be good reasons,
his or her actions are considered warranted in the
circumstances, relieving him
or her of criminal liability.
It can be seen from this description that self-defence arising from a deluded
belief does not properly fall within either of these
categories: it is neither
justified (as it was not the correct thing to do in the circumstances) nor
warranted (as it was not committed
in response to what reasonably appeared to be
good reasons). Consequently, if the accused is to be relieved of liability it
be on a different basis: namely due to his or her lack of
reasons-responsiveness (or whatever other form of the ‘insanity’
defence is enacted).
Consequently, I agree with the view of the TLRI that s 16(3) of the Criminal
Code should be repealed and a provision inserted in the
Code to provide that if
a person does an act or makes an omission as a result of a delusion caused by a
mental disease, the delusion
can only be used as a defence under s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) and cannot be relied on to support a defence of
self-defence under s 46 of the
Criminal Code
8.7.145 Accordingly, the Institute reaffirms its earlier recommendation that
a delusional belief should not form the basis for self-defence
provision should be inserted in the
to provide that if a person does
an act or makes an omission as a result of a delusion caused by a mental
disease, the delusion can
only be used as a defence under s 16 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) and cannot be relied on to support a defence of
self-defence under s 46 of the
Criminal Code
(Tas). However, this prior
recommendation needs be changed to refer to mental or cognitive impairment in
light of the Institute’s
recommendations in relation to terminology used
in s 16 in this Report.

Procedural aspects of the
Burden of proof
8.7.146 As indicated at [
the burden of proof is on the defendant to prove that he or she was insane
within the meaning of the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16. Concerns have been
expressed about the burden of proof that is cast on the defendant to prove the
insanity defence on
the balance of probabilities. This is contrary to the
general rule of criminal law that the legal burden of proof is on the
prosecution.
It is also different from other defences where an accused raises
the defence of mistake or self-defence or seeks to rely on evidence
automatism or intoxication to deny criminal responsibility. In relation to these
matters, the accused has an evidentiary onus
8.7.147 Arguments in favour of placing the burden of proof on the defendant
are based on the view that if the burden were placed on
the prosecution,
‘it would be impossible for the prosecution to disprove an assertion of
insanity, resulting in unmeritorious
acquittals’.
Bronitt and McSherry write that the ‘modern rationale ... lies in the fear
that if an accused only has to bear an
evidentiary burden in relation to mental
impairment, more individuals would be found not criminally responsible than
should be the
However, they observe that this argument is difficult to support in relation to
insanity when it does not apply for other defences,
given that ‘[t]here
appears little support for the proposition that it may be easier to fake a claim
of mental impairment’
than these other
8.7.148 Other law reform reviews, including those conducted by the Scottish
Law Commission, the NZLC and the LRCWA, did not recommend
a change to the
current burden of proof that is placed on the
In contrast, in
relation to its new defence of ‘not criminally responsible by reason of a
recognised medical condition’,
the Law Commission of England and Wales
recommended that there should be an evidential burden only on the defendant
(discharged by
the provision of supporting expert evidence) but that the
prosecution should bear the burden of disproving the defence once it has
8.7.149 In the Issues Paper, the Institute sought feedback on the burden of
proof for the insanity defence:
Criminal Code
(Tas) be amended to provide that the burden
of proof for the insanity defence rests on the prosecution and that the
defendant bears
an evidential burden only in relation to this defence?
8.7.150 There were two submissions that addressed this question with opposing
views expressed.
8.7.151 The DPP opposed a change to the burden of proof to place it on the
prosecution with the defendant bearing only an evidential
burden. The DPP
considered that this ‘would be illogical ... [as] the defence have more
information in respect of this issue
than the prosecution. Further, it only
becomes an issue once guilt has been established’.
8.7.152 On the other hand, Walvisch supported this amendment on the ground
that ‘there seems little reason in principle to differentiate
the insanity
defence from other defences in this regard’. Further, Walvisch expressed
the view that ‘it is arguable that
requiring people to prove their
impairment to a greater extent than they are required to prove other defences
(such as self-defence
or duress) constitutes discrimination on the grounds of
disability’.
8.7.153 The Institute recognises that there are arguments that would support
placing the burden of proof on the prosecution in cases
where it is raised by
the defence given that other defences (such as self-defence (s 46) and mistake
(s 14, 14A, 14B)) contain elements
that relate to the defendant’s state of
mind at the time of the offence, and the prosecution still bears the onus of
in these cases. However, the Institute considers that this is a
controversial reform and should only be made if there is very strong
justification for it. The Institute did not receive any submissions indicating
that the current position is causing difficulties
for defendants. Further, if
the burden of proof changed, the standard of proof would necessarily change from
on the balance of probabilities
to beyond reasonable doubt, and this may create
difficulties in respect of expert evidence. Accordingly, the Institute’s
is that the burden of proof should remain with the defence. This is
consistent with the approach in other jurisdictions and the recommendations
by a clear majority of other law reform bodies.
Recommendation
36. The burden of proof in relation to the defence of mental or cognitive
impairment should remain with the defence in cases where
it is raised by the
Raising the defence of insanity
8.7.154 As indicated at [7.3.27], the prosecution cannot raise the defence of

contrast, in other Australian jurisdictions, mental impairment may be raised by

is in the interests of

‘requires an explanation as to the availability of the defence to be put

(absent the accused putting the state of mind in issue) is
supported on the

recognised in
by Deane and Dawson JJ, who observed that
‘nowadays it is often in the interests of the prosecution (or, at all
events, the

considered that there are very few cases where the absence of a prosecution

considered that the prosecution should have such a power to ensure that the

insanity defence is contrary to fundamental rights of the

reasons, the NZLC recommended that the Crown should only be able to adduce
