8.7.157 Accordingly, a fundamental issue in Tasmania is whether the Crown
should be able to raise the defence of insanity contrary
to the wishes of the
defence. Feedback on this issue was sought in the Issues Paper:
Should the prosecution have the power to raise the defence of insanity
against the wishes of the defendant?
Should the leave of the court be required for the prosecution to do this?
8.7.158 Three submissions to the Institute addressed these questions.
8.7.159 Both ForensiClinic and the DPP supported legislative imprimatur for
the prosecution to raise the defence of insanity. ForensiClinic
stated that this
‘may result in a more appropriate therapeutic outcome and associated risk
reduction’. Similarly, the
DPP focused on the protection of the community
and fairness to the accused to allow insanity to be raised by the
prosecution:
The prosecution would need to be able to establish an evidentiary basis on
the facts of the case to raise the issue of insanity. It
would be appropriate
for the leave of the court to be required for the prosecution to do this.
If the prosecution has evidence that an accused may have a defence of
insanity it is appropriate that it be raised. It may be that
defence counsel,
acting in the best interests of their client as permitted by
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
, may choose not to explore or
rely on the defence given the orders that may follow. The fact that an accused
person does not wish
to raise insanity, perhaps perceiving it is not in his best
interests to do so, does not mean it should not be raised. It is in the
interest that the defence be raised; it is a matter of fairness to an accused
and protection of the community.
As noted by the DPP, this is the position in the Australian Capital
Territory, Northern Territory, Victoria and South Australia. It
supported by the NSWLRC.
8.7.160 On the other hand, Walvisch outlined the arguments both in favour and
against allowing the prosecution to raise the defence
against the wishes of the
accused, and strongly argued that there are powerful reasons not to provide the
prosecution with this power.
8.7.161 In Walvisch’s submission, there were two main policy grounds
that would support the view that the prosecution should
be given such a
First, it can help to ensure that people who are not criminally responsible
for their actions are not improperly punished. This is
important both from the
perspective of the individual (who only deserves to be punished if he or she has
committed a blameworthy
act) and society (which has an interest in ensuring that
‘insane’ people are not incorrectly labelled as criminals).
Secondly, it aims to protect the public, by ensuring that people who have
committed ‘criminal’ acts and who meet the requirements
insanity defence are subjected to the supervisory dispositions available in
relation to mentally impaired offenders. In this
regard, it is at least arguable
that such people are potentially ‘dangerous’ to others, and should
be monitored rather
than set free.
Walvisch also set out a number of arguments that can be advanced against
allowing the prosecution to raise the defence of insanity:
Foremost amongst these is the contention that, in a system which claims to
respect the autonomy and dignity of its participants, an
accused person should
have the right to control his or her own
This is an issue that affects all people who are charged with offences, and
who, by virtue of the
principle, are denied the ability to conduct
their own defence. However, it is a particular problem for offenders who suffer
mental illnesses, due to the discrimination and stereotyping often faced by
such people:
In denying the mentally disabled personal autonomy in decision-making, it
reinforces the stereotype that they are incapable of rational
thought and the
ability to look after their own interests [
[1991] 1 SCR
In addition, the whole trial process may be distorted if the insanity defence
is raised against the wishes of the accused. In particular:
• The accused may be placed in a position where inconsistent defences
must be advanced;
• The accused’s credibility may be undermined, potentially
prejudicing the other defences he or she wishes to raise; and
• The jury may be left with the impression that, because of the mental
illness, the accused is the ‘type of person’
who would have
committed the offence. [reference omitted]
These last two matters are a particular problem due to the stigma that is
attached to mental illness in our society. This factor was
highlighted by the
Canadian Supreme Court in
, with Chief Justice Lamer suggesting that
the ‘irrational fear of the mentally ill in our society’ could
result in an
accused’s credibility being irreversibly damaged where the
issue of insanity is raised [39].
In fact, it is arguable that this ‘irrational fear’ underlies the
public protection aim of the current law. This aim is
premised on the view that
offenders who meet the requirements of the mental impairment defence are
potentially dangerous, and need
to be contained in the interests of public
safety. Yet there is no empirical evidence that firmly establishes that
offenders with
mental illnesses are any more likely to reoffend, or to reoffend
in a more serious way, than offenders without mental illnesses.
people with mental illnesses to a different legal outcome on account of an
unproven and stereotyped view that they are
‘dangerous’ therefore
appears discriminatory.
This is a particular problem given that, under the current scheme, an
individual can be deprived of his or her liberty on a standard
of proof which is
less than the ordinary criminal standard. This is because it appears that the
prosecution may only need to prove
that the accused committed the physical act
alleged beyond reasonable doubt, rather than all of the elements of the charged
If they can then prove, on the balance of probabilities, that the
requirements of the insanity defence have been met, the result
may be lengthy
detention in a secure facility.
This concern is compounded by the possibility that the jury will return a
compromise verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity where
they are not
convinced of the accused’s guilt beyond reasonable doubt, but are
satisfied that the accused ‘is mentally
ill and perhaps dangerous’.
Furthermore, it is important to note that the consequences of a qualified
acquittal may be more
harmful to the accused than a conviction. He or she may
ultimately be detained or subjected to supervision for a longer period than
he or she been found guilty of the offence. In addition, he or she must
‘live with the stigma of being held to be both
a criminal and insane, and
may face conditions worse than those obtaining in prison’.
8.7.162 Accordingly, in Walvisch’s view, these arguments seem ‘to
far outweigh the policy goals which the suggested amendment
seeks to achieve.
Rather than being protected, the accused is denied the right to autonomy and
dignity, and placed at risk of greater
state intervention’. Further, it
was argued that ‘[t] he aim of “public protection” seems to be
a discriminatory view of the dangers posed by individuals with mental
illnesses. Moreover, where there is a genuine need to protect
the community from
a truly dangerous person, civil commitment laws can be relied upon’. In
this case, Walvisch argued that:
That simply leaves the problem of potentially convicting some people who are
truly not blameworthy. This could largely be overcome
by the use of
prosecutorial discretion — the prosecution could decide not to proceed
with charges against an individual who
is believed to be have a valid defence.
Alternatively, the judge could direct an acquittal in such circumstances,
perhaps accompanied
by a recommendation that an investigation be undertaken into
the possible need for civil commitment. While a few individuals may
slip between
the cracks of these approaches, and may be convicted despite the fact that they
were not criminally responsible for
their actions, the numbers are likely to be
extremely limited. In light of this fact, there does not seem to be sufficient
justification
for allowing the insanity defence to be raised against the
accused’s wishes.
Instead, a competent accused should be allowed to waive his or her right to
raise the defence, just as he or she can waive other rights.
This is the
approach that has been taken in a number of cases in the United States, such as
Because the defendant must bear the ultimate consequences of any decision, we
conclude that if a defendant has acted intelligently
and voluntarily, a trial
court must defer to his or her decision to waive the insanity defense
, 378 (DC App Ct, 1979)].
8.7.163 Walvisch also gave consideration to the limits that should be placed
on the power of the prosecution to raise the defence
(if such a power were
recognised in Tasmania). His view was that ‘the ability to do so should be
heavily circumscribed’.
Accordingly:
the trial judge should not be required to give leave insanity whenever there
is sufficient evidence to raise the defence. Instead,
the judge should be given
a broad discretion to focus on whether the interests of justice require the
prosecution to raise the matter,
taking into account factors such as:
• The amount of evidence addressing the accused’s impairment;
• The extent to which that evidence raises a question about the
accused’s criminal responsibility for his or her actions;
• The likelihood that the accused committed the criminal act in
• The nature and seriousness of the offence;
• The extent to which the accused poses a threat to society; and
• The prejudice that will be caused to the accused by raising the
Such an approach has the benefit of ensuring that the defence will only be
raised against the accused’s will where it is seen
to be absolutely
necessary, rather than potentially being addressed in every case where there is
some evidence of mental illness.
8.7.164 As noted above, the power of the
prosecution to raise the defence of insanity/mental impairment exists in statute
Tasmania, the prosecution can raise the defence of insanity if
the defendant
puts their mental state in
As explained, this
includes cases where the defendant raises non-insane automatism or absence of
intent. This would seem to reduce
the need to make specific provision for the
Crown to have the power to raise insanity. As the NSWLRC observed, there may
a ‘few cases where the absence of a prosecution power to raise
[the defence] arises as a practical problem’ given a defendant’s
mental state will frequently be in
However, it observed
that ‘they may be some cases where the defence wishes to avoid raising the
issue of the defendant’s
mental state ... but where the prosecution
believes it to be in the public interest to raise the
The issue is
whether it is appropriate that the prosecution be given the power to raise the
defence in these circumstances, and,
as noted by Walvisch, there are competing
arguments that involve balancing the interests of the defendant in maintaining
of their defence, particularly given the indefinite nature of forensic
orders, and the public interest in community protection by
allowing the
defendant to be detained and receive
8.7.165 After considering these complex issues, the Institute’s view is
that the prosecution should have power to raise the
defence of insanity with the
leave of the court. As recognised by the NSWLRC, there may be cases where the
court determines that
it is in the public interest for the prosecution to raise
the defence when the defendant wishes to avoid raising his or her mental
As indicted, this
reflects the approach under the Model Criminal Code, in Victoria, South
Australia, Northern Territory and the Australian
Capital Territory. In such
cases, the onus of proof should rest with the
prosecution.
A model for
reform in this regard can be found in Victoria, where the
Crimes (Mental
Impairment and Unfitness to be tried) Act 1997
(Vic) provides that:
21(1) A person is presumed not to have been suffering from a mental
impairment having the effect referred to in section 20(1) until
the contrary is
(3) If the defence of mental impairment is raised by the prosecution or the
defence, the party raising it bears the onus of rebutting
the presumption.
22(1) The question of mental impairment may be raised at any time during a
trial by the defence or, with the leave of the trial judge,
prosecution.
Recommendation
37. The prosecution should have the power to raise the defence of mental or
cognitive impairment against the wishes of the defendant
with the leave of the
court. In this case, the onus of proof should be on the prosecution and the
standard of proof should be the
balance of probabilities.
A ‘consent defence’
8.7.166 Unlike the criminal law generally, where the prosecution can accept a
plea by a defendant, currently in Tasmania it is not
possible for the defendant
to enter a plea of not guilty by reason of insanity. In the Supreme Court a
verdict of not guilty by reason
of insanity must be delivered by a jury, and in
the Magistrates Court it must be given by a magistrate. In the letter to the
setting out the Terms of Reference, the then Attorney-General indicated
that the DPP had requested that consideration be given to
reforming the law in
relation to the criminal procedure relating to the defence of insanity and to
legislating to remove the necessity
for a jury to determine the question of
insanity in certain circumstances. Similarly, the NSWLRC recommended that if the
prosecution
and defence agree that evidence in a case establishes the defence of
mental health or cognitive impairment, then the court must enter
a verdict of
not criminally responsible by reason of mental health or cognitive impairment if
satisfied that the defence is established
This has been
described as a defence of mental illness by
Similarly, the Scottish Law Commission and the Law Commission of England and
Wales recommended that there should be provision for
the verdict of insanity by
The absence of a
‘consent defence’ in Tasmania contrasts with the position in
Victoria, Western Australia, South Australia,
the Australian Capital Territory
and the Northern Territory, as well as in New
8.7.167 Reasons for allowing a consent defence are that:
(a) It ‘avoid[s] farcical trials where insanity is not in
It avoids the
jury process becoming ‘artificial’ or a
‘formality’.
may result in a loss of faith in the jury system as the jury is essentially
confirming the view of the defence and
prosecution.
(b) It saves court time and consequently
(c) It reduces stress on the defendant (who may still be ill at the time of
the trial) and witnesses including victims and family
members’ in cases
where this is the obvious
(d) In the case of an accused who is unfit to stand trial in Tasmania, it is
possible to determine this issue by consent but it is
not possible to resolve
the question of criminal responsibility by consent.
8.7.168 The VLRC noted that where the prosecution and defence agree that the
evidence establishes the defence of mental impairment,
‘it is unlikely
that the jury will arrive at a different
conclusion.’
8.7.169 The importance of the role of the jury in representing the community
in determining criminal responsibility has been identified
as the predominant
reason provided for not allowing a consent defence of
On this basis, the
VLRC changed its view from prior recommendations it had made and recommended
that the law be changed to require
a jury to determine criminal responsibility
in all criminal trials in higher courts under the
The VLRC observed that
the jury protects the interests of the community by ensuring the sound
administration of justice, and that
this is particularly important in these
that has profound
and lasting consequences for the accused, victims and the
community’.
for public examination of the issues ‘in a way that is comprehensible to
the accused, victims and the community’.
This has an educative function
for the public.
protects the accused by requiring their criminal responsibility to be determined
by independent members of the community.
In addition, ‘the determination
of criminal responsibility by a jury provides a greater level of acknowledgement
and their families of the harm that they have
experienced’.
8.7.170 Other reasons in favour of retaining the jury are that the trial
process operates as a check and a safeguard not just by the
involvement of the
community but also in that the scrutiny inherent in the trial process may
promote caution in the formulation of
opinion by expert witnesses. Further,
expert opinion is only as good as the information that is provided to the expert
as the basis
for the opinions expressed, and the factual matrix that may emerge
at trial may differ from the facts that appear in the Crown papers.
that experts may refine their opinions contained in the pre-trial reports to
reflect the change in facts that emerge at
trial. This caution and refinement
may be lost if the trial process is replaced by a ‘defence consent’
8.7.171 On the other hand, it is noted that the DPP routinely makes decisions
the DPP is to make
decisions about whether or not to institute proceedings and this involves
decisions in relation to the charges
laid (for example, whether an accused is
charged with attempted murder or causing grievous bodily harm), the acceptance
of guilty to particular charges and/or in relation to decisions not to
proceed with a matter (such as where the prosecution accepts
that a defence of
self-defence is made out on the
In making these
decisions, fairness is the overarching principle and it may be questioned as to
why decisions in relation to acceptance
of the insanity defence should be
treated any differently.
8.7.172 In the Issues Paper, the Institute sought feedback on whether a
consent defence should be permitted:
Should there be legislative change to allow the prosecution and defence to
agree that a defendant is not guilty by reason of insanity?
If so, are there any protections in the interests of the defendant that need
to be put in place?
8.7.173 There were three submissions that addressed this issue and all
supported allowing the prosecution and defence to agree that
a defendant is not
guilty by reason of insanity.
8.7.174 The DPP provided detailed consideration of
the issue, highlighting the problems that potentially arise when a trial is
in circumstances where both parties agree that the defendant satisfies
the requirements for a finding of not guilty on the grounds
of insanity:
In recent years this Office has conducted a significant number of trials
where both the prosecution and defence have agreed that the
accused was insane.
Our Office only reaches such a position after having had the accused
independently assessed and receiving independent
psychiatric evidence that the
accused is insane.
These trials have been considerably traumatic for the accused (who is
generally mentally very unwell), the victim(s), their families
and witnesses.
Juries find it difficult to understand why they are there because, as a matter
of practicality, there is only one
possible result with Crown counsel, the
defence counsel and the judge telling the jury although it is a matter for them
only one appropriate verdict. It turns the trial into a farce. Further,
the cost involved in these unnecessary hearings is a relevant,
albeit minor,
consideration.
In circumstances where the independent evidence suggests the accused is not
insane, the Crown strongly disputes any assertion to the
If the Crown, having received independent advice, was able to accept a plea
of not guilty on the grounds of insanity, the above problem
would be removed
where it is not accepted the trial would take place in the usual manner.
The argument that these matters should be determined by a jury, as
representatives of the community, is erroneous:
• In circumstances where experts give unchallenged evidence that the
defendant is not guilty by reason of insanity but the jury
makes an alternative
finding, the matter would inevitably be successfully appealed on the grounds the
verdict was unsafe and unsatisfactory,
(1993) 22 Tas R 368.
Pennicott v State of Tasmania
CCA 1183 of 2013, the Court of Criminal
Appeal quashed the appellant’s convictions and substituted verdicts of not
insanity, the court published reasons to the parties only.
• At a plea hearing the prosecution must state the facts. The facts
will inevitably include information as to the medical evidence
relied upon to
support the plea. The prosecution will be accountable in open court for the
• The Director of Public Prosecutions makes decisions which affect the
criminal liability and responsibility of accused persons
routinely, for example,
when determining whether to charge with manslaughter or murder, or whether to
accept a plea to a lesser charge.
Thus the law routinely entrusts an independent, statutorily appointed person,
to make such decisions. There is no reason to depart
from this in circumstances
where prosecution and defence agree that a defendant is not guilty by reason of
8.7.175 DPFEM also agreed that a consent verdict should be available. Its
view was that this would result in a saving of court time
and money in avoiding
trials if both the prosecution and defence agree. DPFEM also indicated that
removing the need for a trial may
protect a person with mental health
impairments from the trauma of the trial process. DPFEM considered that a
defence of mental impairment
by consent is an extension of the power of the
Prosecution Services and the Office of the DPP to discontinue matters according
the balance of public interest and chance of success determinations.
8.7.176 Although the Institute recognises the importance of the role of the
jury in representing the community, the Institute agrees
with the DPP,
ForensiClinic and DPFEM that the prosecution and the defence should be able to
agree that the evidence establishing
the defence of mental impairment. Contrary
to the current position in Tasmania, this is possible in Victoria, Western
South Australia, the Australian Capital Territory, the Northern
Territory and in New Zealand. It avoids unnecessary trials that cause
victims and families, and to individuals who are mentally unwell, and reflects
the responsibility placed in the Office
of the DPP and Prosecution Services to
represent the public interest. It also consistent the power of the court
contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
to dispense with an investigation into the defendant’s fitness to stand
trial if the prosecution and defendant agree.
8.7.177 However, the Institute’s view is that safeguards are required
in this process and that the ‘consent defence’
should operate with
judicial oversight. Accordingly, as with the view expressed by the NSWLRC, the
Institute’s view is that
the court should have the power to review the
evidence in order to be satisfied that it establishes the defence of mental or
impairment in a case where the prosecution and defence agree that the
proposed evidence establishes the defence. If the court is
satisfied that the
evidence establishes the defence, then the judge will enter a verdict of not
guilty by reason of mental or cognitive
impairment. If the trial judge is not
satisfied, then the case must proceed to a jury
The court should have
access to the expert reports as well as the power to require oral evidence to be
given by the expert.
Recommendation
38. The court should have the power to dispense with a trial if the
prosecution and defence agree that evidence establishes the defence
of mental or
cognitive impairment. However, safeguards are required and the court should have
the power to review the evidence (including
hearing from expert witnesses) in
order to be satisfied that the evidence establishes the defence of mental or
cognitive impairment
in a case where the prosecution and defence agree that the
proposed evidence establishes the defence.
The operation of
in the Magistrates
8.7.178 There are two matters that require attention in relation to the
operation of the proposed defence of mental or cognitive impairment
Magistrates Court:
(1) The introduction of a simplified procedure (such as recommended in this
Report for fitness to stand trial).
(2) The application of the new defence of mental or cognitive impairment to
summary offences.
Simplified procedure
. A further issue that should be
considered is the operation of the recommended power to discharge in the
Magistrates Court for the
new defence of mental or cognitive impairment (or the
defence of insanity if this is retained). In
, the Institute recommended
that magistrates be provided with a power to discharge people without making a
determination of their
fitness to stand trial or their criminal responsibility
in some circumstances (Recommendations 17 and 18). This was not raised in
Issues Paper in relation to the defence of insanity. However, similar issues
arise in relation to the complexity and expense
of raising the defence of
insanity for relatively minor matters in the Magistrates Court. It is noted that
in Queensland the discharge
powers of the court operate in relation to both
fitness and insanity. Accordingly, the Institute suggests that consideration be
to allowing magistrates to discharge for the defence as well as for
fitness matters and that discussions with stakeholders take place
implementation of Recommendations 17 and 18.
Summary offences
. The current position in the Magistrates
Court is that the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16 applies to summary offences
where there is a parallel
This includes
assault, assaulting a police officer, indecent assault and unlawfully injuring
However, common law
principles of criminal responsibility (including the defence of insanity) apply
to other summary offences.
While this may not have created any particular difficulty when the common law
provision in relation to insanity were very similar, it is
the Institute’s view that the new defence of mental or cognitive
impairment should apply to all offences in Tasmania — indictable and
summary offences. Accordingly, the Institute recommends
that the new defence of
mental or cognitive impairment should apply to indictable and summary offences.
8.7.181 The Institute notes that this problem extends further than the
Criminal Code
(Tas) s 16 and exists in relation to all principles of
criminal responsibility (including defences) that operate in Tasmania.
Previously,
the Institute has highlighted the complexity and inconsistency
created by the operation of fundamentally different rules in its consideration
of evidence of intoxication used to deny criminal
responsibility.
Institute’s view that this requires further examination for all principles
of criminal responsibility for summary
offences without
parallels in
Tasmania and suggests that this matter could be pursued as a separate
Recommendation
39. The new defence of mental or cognitive impairment introduced to amend the
current defence of insanity contained in the
Criminal Code
should apply to indictable and summary offences.
Disposition:
Forensic and Treatment Orders
Introduction
10.1.1 This Part examines the orders that can be made under the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) following a finding that an
individual is not guilty by reason of insanity (either at a trial or a special
hearing) or if a
finding cannot be made that the defendant is not guilty of an
offence (at a special hearing).
10.1.2 The Terms of Reference ask the TLRI to address the following matters
relevant to disposition:
(a) if insane delusions arising from mental illness form the basis of
self-defence, whether defendants relying on insane delusions
should be liable to
supervision under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(b) if insane delusions arising from mental illness form the basis of
self-defence, whether the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) requires amendment in relation to treatment options for such
defendants.
Based on the Institute’s recommendation that insane delusions arising
from mental illness should only be used in relation to
a defence under s 16 of
Criminal Code
(Tas) and not for the basis of self-defence, there is
no need to address these matters further. Individuals who consider that they
need to use force against another person based on a delusion will be able to
rely on the defence of mental or cognitive impairment,
and if found not guilty
on that basis, the court will be able to impose any of the orders under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
10.1.3 The Terms of Reference also require the TLRI to consider the just and
effective operation of the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas), in particular the operation of Part 4 of the Act, including in relation
to the discharge and review of forensic and treatment
orders and whether there
is a need for ‘step-down’ options. In light of these consideration,
this Part also sets out
recommendations for reform that will abolish the current
indefinite detention scheme for forensic orders and replace it with a new
with the following features:
(1) a limiting term set by the court following a finding of not guilty by
reason of mental or cognitive impairment or where a finding
of cannot be made
that the defendant is not guilty.
(2) The limiting term should be set on the basis of the hypothetical sentence
model. This means that the court must nominate a limiting
term, which would be
the court’s best estimate of the length of the sentence that would have
been imposed had the person been
found guilty at a normal trial.
(3) A limiting term should not operate as an absolute limit of the duration
of an order. The DPP can make an application to the Supreme
Court prior to the
expiration of the limiting term for an extension of a restriction order or a
supervision order. The DPP can also
apply for a restriction order to be replaced
with a supervision order instead of an extension of the restriction order.
(4) A forensic patient should be able to apply to the Supreme Court for a
discharge of a restriction order or the variation or revocation
of a supervision
order prior to the expiry of the limiting term.
(5) The test for making, varying or removing orders under the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
should be changed from
‘likely to endanger’ to refer to whether the defendant does, or
would if released, pose an unacceptable
risk of causing physical or
psychological harm to a person or persons generally.
(6) The test for making, varying or removing orders under the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
should be amended to
require the Mental Health Tribunal and the court to have regard to the
person’s recovery or progress in
terms of treatment progression and
personal improvement in making a decision to discharge a restriction order, or
vary or revoke
a supervision order.
Consequences of findings under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999

(Mental Impairment) Act 1999

Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
there are five orders that a court can make if a defendant is found not guilty

if a finding cannot be made that the
defendant is not guilty. These are to impose:
(a) a restriction order;
(b) release the defendant and make a supervision order;
(c) a treatment order;
(d) a conditional release order; or
(e) an unconditional release

Process for making orders
10.2.9 In making orders under the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(1), the court
is directed by
to apply, where appropriate, the principle that restrictions
on the defendant’s freedom and personal autonomy should be kept
minimum consistent with the safety of the
In exercising its
powers under
, the court is also directed by
to have regard to the
(a) the nature of the defendant’s mental impairment or other condition
or disability;
(b) whether the defendant is, or would, if released, be likely to endanger
another person or other persons generally;
(c) whether there are adequate resources available for the treatment and
support of the defendant in the community;
(d) whether the defendant is likely to comply with the conditions of a
supervision order; and
(e) other matters that the court thinks
For the purposes of assisting a court to determine proceedings under
provides that the Attorney-General must provide the court with a report
stating, so far as reasonably ascertainable, the views of
the next of kin of the
defendant and the victims, if any, of the defendant’s
The court must not
release a defendant under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(1) unless it has considered an expert report on the
condition of the defendant and the possible effects of the proposed action on
the behaviour of the defendant. The court also must have considered the report
of the attitudes of victims, if any, and next of kin,
and be satisfied that the
defendant’s next of kin and victims have been given reasonable notice of
the proceedings.
10.2.10 A review of cases indicates that factors such as the nature of the
mental illness or intellectual disability, including its
amenability to
treatment, an individual’s compliance with treatment and insight into
their condition, the person’s previous
history of offending and/or
violence are relevant to the exercise of the court’s discretion. In cases
where restriction orders
were imposed, individuals typically had chronic mental
illnesses that were difficult to treat and had a lack of insight into their
condition and a history of non-compliance with treatment. In contrast,
supervision orders, treatment orders and conditional release
orders were
typically imposed where the person had an awareness of their condition and no
history of offending or
Similar conditions
were imposed in most cases where supervision, treatment or conditional release
orders were made and these involved
submission to treatment, taking medication
and residential restrictions. Factors that seemed to influence the making of a
supervision
order as opposed to a treatment order or a conditional release order
were the need to ensure long-term compliance with treatment
and the more limited
powers that existed in the event of non-compliance with the conditions of the
order in the case of treatment
and supervision orders.
10.2.11 In the Supreme Court cases where the person was found not guilty, or
not guilty by reason of insanity, and details of the
mental illness or
intellectual disability were known (n = 23), there were 15 cases involving
mental illness (predominately schizophrenia),
seven cases involving intellectual
disability and one case involving cognitive impairment. In relation to the seven
cases where the
defendant was classified as having an intellectual disability,
in two cases the person received a conditional release order and in
five cases a
supervision order. In relation to defendants with a mental illness, five
defendants received a restriction order, six
defendants received a supervision
order, two defendants received a treatment order and two defendants were
conditionally released.

Factors relevant to the discharge or
revocation of orders
10.2.22 The factors set out at [
] in relation to making of orders
are also relevant to the discharge of a restriction order and the variation or
revocation of a supervision
order by the Supreme
In making a decision
to impose an order, or vary or discharge an order, the relevant legislative
sections involve the exercise of
judicial discretion as they require a value
judgment to be
Court of Criminal Appeal considered the interaction of the principle that
‘where appropriate, restrictions on the defendant’s
freedom and
personal autonomy should be kept to a minimum consistent with the safety of the
community’ contained in
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
with the matters set out in 35(1). The Court stated that,
if a court, by reference to the matters set out in
considers it is
not appropriate to apply that principle, for example it concludes by reference
factors that a less restrictive order is not consistent with the
safety of the community, then it is entitled to make an order disregarding
first part of the principle.
This weighs the balance in favour of community safety at the expense of the
minimal restriction on the defendant’s freedom and
autonomy. Similarly, in
Secretary of the Department of Health and Human Services v
the Court of
Criminal Appeal stated that ‘the issue of the respondent’s freedom
and personal autonomy is not one which
should override all else. It is an issue
to be considered “where appropriate” and in the context of community
It is also to be considered in the context of the factors identified in
10.2.23 A key factor identified in
need to assess the risk that the defendant poses to other people. In making an
assessment of the likelihood of the person being
a danger to others (as required
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
s 35(1)(b))
the view was expressed that the court is required to do more than simply
approach the issue on the basis that ‘the greater
the degree of likelihood
of danger found, the less likely a court would be to order
revocation’.
the Court has to make a finding, on the balance of probabilities, whether or not
a state of affairs is
Court noted that different interpretations of ‘likely’ include that
there is a ‘real or substantial chance
possibility’
something is ‘more likely than
However, the
precise meaning of ‘likely’ was not resolved with the Court of
Criminal Appeal indicating that in that case,
the primary judge ‘was
entitled to be satisfied that there was, at least, a real chance or possibility
of the appellant again
becoming a danger to members of the
community’.
10.2.24 The Court also considered the meaning of ‘freedom and personal
autonomy’ and examined whether this encompasses
restrictions arising from
the way in which the order could be revoked or varied (for example if the order
had to be revoked by the
Supreme Court or lapsed through the passing of
It was held that it
does not extend to the difficulties, ‘in practical terms, for the person
to make an attempt to be relieved
of some or all of the consequences of the
This means that
the indefinite nature of the supervision order compared to the finite nature of
a treatment order is not considered
relevant to an assessment of the
restrictions that either order may place on a defendant’s freedom and
personal autonomy. Instead,
‘freedom and personal autonomy’ mean the
‘ability to move freely and function within the community, and to be
self-regulatory
and self-sufficient as far as possible, in all
10.2.25 In making decisions in relation to the revocation of orders, the
Supreme Court has been particularly concerned about the powers
that exist under
orders in the event that the person is non-compliant with their treatment
regime. In cases where the Supreme Court
refused to revoke a supervision order
and replace it with a treatment order, the applicants had long-standing mental
illnesses requiring
ongoing medication to manage the illnesses. The concern was
expressed that, while they did not present a risk to the community when
appropriately treated, there was a risk of harm to others if the medication
regimes were not complied with. Accordingly, the court
considered that
supervision orders were required (rather than treatment orders) to ensure
compliance with medication treatment and
mental health
management.
This allowed for
oversight by the Supreme Court and the power to detain the person in a SMHU, if
necessary. It also meant that orders
would not lapse (unlike treatment orders
that require administrative action if they are to be renewed).
10.2.26 Subsequent to the decisions in
of the Department of Health and Human Services v
the Victorian Court of Appeal considered in detail the interaction
between the Victorian equivalents to
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas), in particular the meaning of
case, the primary judge refused to revoke a supervision order on the basis that
he had concluded that the risk of danger
to the public was significant if the
individual was non-compliant with treatment (a risk assessed as low). This
approach was rejected
by the Court of Appeal:
His Honour, in our respectful opinion, wrongly focused upon the gravity of
the potential harm to others involved with such a risk
in assessing the likely
danger to others as ‘significant’. In assessing the likely danger
... the low likelihood of such
a risk materialising should have been the
critical consideration, rather than the gravity of the harm in the event that
eventuated.
Endangerment is about the risk of harm. The gravity of the harm may be
relevant to assessing the nature of the risk, but the probability
of any risk,
be it high or low, is the critical concept of endangerment. ... The ordinary
meaning of endangerment entails the concept
of chance or risk. The terms of [the
provision] require a court to assess whether a person is ‘
endanger themselves or others’. This serves to emphasise that the focus is
upon the extent of the chance, risk or peril of
some harm materialising. If the
harm or injury which is likely to result is substantial but the
‘chance’, ‘risk’
or ‘peril’ of it
eventuating is minimal, then a person subject to a supervision order is not
necessarily ‘likely
to endanger’ ...
It is an assessment of the likelihood of the risk materialising and whether
or not that risk is more than merely possible that is
the critical
consideration, not the gravity of the harm that may
This interpretation reflects the approach evident in the decisions of the
Court of Criminal Appeal in Tasmania, where the focus is
on the risk of danger
rather than the magnitude of the danger.
10.2.27 An additional issue considered in
relevance of any change in the practical restrictions on the defendant arising
from the removal of the supervision order in
considering the balance between the
safety of the community and the person’s freedom and autonomy. The primary
judge had expressed
the view that the continuation of the order would not impose
any significant practical restrictions on the defendant as his treatment
remain the same with or without the order. However, the Court of Appeal accepted
that ‘the finding that the nature and
degree of the restrictions on the
appellant’s freedom and autonomy would have no “significant
practical effect”
does not provide a basis for refusing to revoke the
supervision order’.
Court’s view was that supervision ‘is a restriction on liberty and
autonomy and it can be justified only where it
is found to be necessary’
and ‘if it was not necessary to impose any restriction on the appellant to
ensure the safety
of the community, the statutory regime, informed by the
principle of parsimony, did not allow for the consideration of the degree
inconvenience to the appellant to justify non-revocation of the
This differs
from the approach of the Court of Criminal Appeal in

Issues for consideration
10.3.1 There are a number of issues in relation to the imposition and
discharge/revocation of forensic orders under the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas). Broadly speaking, they include:
(1) Whether it is necessary to make a change to the indefinite nature of
forensic orders imposed under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas)? And if so, how?
(2) Whether there is a need to make a change to the statutory test contained
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
making, varying, discharging and revoking an order?
(3) Whether there should be change to the judicial model of decision-making
to allow the MHT to exercise powers of variation, discharge
or revocation of
forensic orders under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(4) Whether there is a need for a change the leave provisions and/or to make
other changes to provide an appropriate pathway for gradual
reintegration of a
person subject to a forensic order into the community?
(5) Whether the orders are appropriate for people with intellectual
disabilities and cognitive impairments rather than mental illness?
10.3.2 Another issue is whether the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
(Tas) should be amended to provide for a limiting term for
conditional release orders to replace the current position where it is
for the court to make an indefinite order. This order is not a forensic order
and does not involve the CFP or the MHT. Instead,
it is an order made and
supervised by the court.
Indefinite nature of forensic
10.3.3 In Tasmania, restriction orders and supervision orders are indefinite,
subject to review by the MHT and revocation or discharge
by the Supreme Court.
This aspect of the insanity and fitness to stand trial regime has been the
subject of significant criticism
(as discussed below). Although the move away
from the traditional ‘Governor’s Pleasure’ regime (in Tasmania
elsewhere) was intended to remove the arbitrary and political nature of
indefinite detention, criticism has been directed at the
Tasmanian system on the
basis that the Governor’s Pleasure model is retained ‘in substance,
if not in form’.
be argued that the current system is unfair to individuals and does not reflect
the principles underlying the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) which seek to provide an appropriate balance between individual
freedom and autonomy and community safety.

10.3.4 As shown in Appendix 3 Table A3.3, several other Australian
subject to mechanisms for review and ending the orders. This is the position in
New South Wales (for those found not guilty

Northern Territory,
a period is set for a major review of the order, referred to
as a nominal term, either by reference to a specified statutory period
(Victoria) or by the court (Northern Territory). At the major review, there is a
presumption that the level of supervision will be
In Queensland,
Western Australia and New South Wales (as with Tasmania), there is provision for
periodic reviews, which may result
in the orders being varied or revoked. In
Queensland, orders may be revoked or varied by the Mental Health Tribunal,
subject to any
non-revocation period that may have been set for prescribed

Accused Review Board. In New South Wales, the court
or the Mental Health Review

10.3.5 Other jurisdictions provide for finite terms through the use of
limiting terms.
In New South
Wales (for those found unfit and not acquitted following a special hearing),
South Australia, the Australian Capital
Territory and the Commonwealth, the
court is required to set a limiting term when imposing a detention or
supervision order. The
limiting term sets the longest period of detention and
there is a possibility that the person may be released before the expiry of
limiting term. However, evidence in New South Wales suggests release before the
expiration of the limiting term is rare. The
MHRT indicated that it did not know
of any patients being unconditionally released prior to the expiry of their
limiting terms and
that conditional release was also
In New South Wales, the
Australian Capital Territory and the Commonwealth, the limiting term is set by
the court making a best estimate
of the sentence that would have been considered
appropriate in a normal trial. In South Australia, the limiting term is the
to the period of imprisonment that would have been appropriate if the
defendant had been convicted of the offence of which the objective
elements have
been established, without taking into account the defendant’s mental
impairment.

10.3.6 In Tasmania, there are several options for reform:
Make no change to the current position
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas). This would mean that forensic
orders would remain indefinite, with provision for certificates to be issued by
the MHT, and
orders to be varied, discharged or revoked by the Supreme Court.
The test contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
would continue to refer to ‘likely to endanger’
]). This approach is not
recommended by the Institute.
Make no change to the current position
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) in relation to indefinite
detention but amend the discharge procedure
to create a presumption in
favour of a reduced level of supervision in circumstances where the MHT has
issued a certificate and the
person makes an application to the Supreme Court.
This approach is also not recommended by the Institute.
Change the indefinite nature of forensic orders to require the court
to set a limiting term
. This is the approach recommended by the Institute
and so it is necessary to decide:
(a) the approach to setting the term (see [
(b) whether a preventative detention scheme should be introduced that would

]). The Institute recommends that

Replacement of indefinite terms with a limiting term
. The first
decision that needs to be made is whether to retain the indefinite nature of
forensic orders contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas) or whether to introduce a limiting term. As noted, limiting terms
exist in New South Wales (for those found unfit and not acquitted
following a
special hearing), the Australian Capital Territory, South Australia and the
Commonwealth. In contrast, as with Tasmania,
indefinite detention of forensic
patients exists in New South Wales (for those found not guilty by reason of
insanity), Victoria,
Western Australia, Queensland and the Northern
10.3.9 Several law reform bodies have examined the period of supervision or
detention that is appropriate for forensic patients following
a finding of not
guilty by reason of insanity or unfit to stand trial (and not acquitted at the
special hearing). The NSWLRC recommended
that a limiting term apply for both
defendants found not guilty by reason of insanity and following a finding that
the person was
unfit to stand trial (and not acquitted at the special
The view of the
NSWLRC view was that there should be a time limit because it provides an
important protection for forensic patients.
10.3.10 The SASAC’s view was also that limiting terms should be
Similarly, a key
recommendation of the ALRC was that there should be limits placed on the
detention that can be
This approach was
also recommended by the Senate Committee examining indefinite detention of
people with cognitive and psychiatric
impairment in
The LRCWA also
recommended introducing the use of limiting
10.3.11 In contrast, the VLRC and the Western Australian review conducted by
the Department of the Attorney-General preferred to retain
indefinite terms with
regular reviews. The VLRC considered this was the preferred approach in
combination with reforms to ensure
that ‘the decision-making framework in
place once an order is made is rigorous and ensures that the period a person is
closely reflects the minimum period necessary to address the
person’s risk to the
community’.
Arguments in favour of making provision for a limiting
The following arguments can be identified in relation to making
provisions for a limiting term.
(1) An indefinite term creates uncertainty and this is potentially an
incentive for innocent people to plead guilty rather than rely
on unfitness to
stand trial/and or insanity.
The NSWLRC observed that ‘anecdotally it appears that the indeterminate
orders may deter people with mental impairment from
relying on the defence of
mental illness’.
Further, it stated that ‘[i]f this is correct, then it may lead to
outcomes which fail to meet the interests of justice, public
safety, or the
person’s treatment
Conversely, the
benefits of a limiting term are that it facilitates fairness so that
‘forensic patients are not detained or
managed within the forensic system
for longer than they would have been following
conviction.’
encourage people to raise unfitness to stand trial or the defence of insanity.
In addition, it ensures that people who require
treatment are able to access it
given that these people should be managed in the forensic system rather than the
correctional system.
(2) People who are detained indefinitely may spend longer under supervision
than if they had been sentenced following the usual
implications for decisions about whether to rely on unfitness to stand trial/and
or insanity. As observed by the Office
of Public Prosecution Victoria, ‘a
person who is able to understand the process involved in a plea of guilty will
better off being dealt with by a criminal sanction, rather than being
placed on an indefinite supervision
It also raises
questions of fairness.
The relationship between the time spent in detention/under supervision
compared to the sentence a person would have received had they
been sentenced
following a finding of guilty has been examined in Tasmania. Research suggests
that ‘the consequences of an
insanity plea or unfitness to plead [are]
likely to restrict the liberty of the subject to a greater degree than an actual
in all cases except murder and
This is confirmed by recent research undertaken by the TLRI with the assistance

Arguments against a limiting term
The following
arguments have been made in relation to not introducing a limiting term and
retaining indefinite detention:
(1) A limiting term may result in some people reaching the end of the time
limit and being unconditionally released, in circumstances
where he or she is at
risk of causing harm to the
In addition, the
length of the time limit is set at the time of disposition, when the progress of
the defendant’s treatment
and rehabilitation is hard to
However, this

entitled to be released at the expiry
of the sentence, even if they still pose a
risk to others’.
addition, concerns about community safety at the end of the limiting term could
be managed within the civil mental health
Further, a system of

(2) The forensic system has quite different objectives to
sentencing.
It is concerned
with a person’s treatment and safety, and on this basis indefinite terms
are ‘a way of recognising that
[the] orders were based on a therapeutic
framework, rather than one that is corrections-based (where orders have a
definite term).’
VLRC considered that:
Indefinite term orders ... are consistent with the therapeutic focus of the
CMIA. Such orders are also consistent with the principle
of community protection
underlying the CMIA that recognises that the recovery of a supervised person
should proceed on a gradual
basis so that their risk can be managed to a point
where they can ultimately be reintegrated into the community.
The supervision of people under the CMIA is justified on these principles and
not on the basis of proportionality or deterrence which
would form the basis of
a criminal sentence. In the Commission’s view, the duration of orders
should therefore be based on
the time a supervised person needs to recover or
progress through the system of gradual reintegration before they can safely
to the community. The length of time it takes for this to happen varies
from person to person and is difficult to predict at the
time of the making of a
supervision order.
Similar concerns were expressed in submissions to the SASAC that ‘the
amount of time the defendant spends in custody should
be determined by the
defendant’s treatment needs and the protection of the community, rather
than the nature of the offence
committed’.
accords with the view of Boyd-Caine and Chappell, that indefinite detention is
‘able to respond to the individual needs
of each patient on a forensic
order.’ In contrast, ‘definite orders would not necessarily be
capable of responding to
the complexities of diagnoses, responsiveness to
treatment, and access to leave privileges that are critical to the care,
and rehabilitation of forensic
There may also be advantages for an unwell person in accessing services within
the forensic mental health system given that the level
of support and treatment
may be more difficult to access in the civil mental health system.

10.3.14 Other commentators have supported indefinite detention provided there

10.3.15 Previous analysis of the Tasmanian forensic mental health system has
raised concerns about the difficulty that patients experience

Similar observations

Anti-Discrimination Commissioner has illustrated the problems in having an order

Case Example
A man was subject to a restriction order for 16 years. Despite reports from
the Tasmanian Forensic Review Tribunal suggesting that
the restriction order be
lifted and replaced by a supervision order allowing him to reside in the
community subject to the supervision
of the Chief Forensic Psychiatrist, the
courts were reluctant to revoke the restriction order on the basis that they had
no evidence
of how the person would operate in the community.
This was described as presenting a ‘catch 22’ situation given
that it would be ‘almost impossible to gather evidence
to present to the
court to demonstrate how they would operate in a different
10.3.16 There also appears to be a traditional
reluctance on the part of the Supreme Court to allow an individual to transition
an indefinite supervision order to a limited term treatment order in cases
where a person has a mental illness that requires long-term
(potentially
life-long) treatment, such as schizophrenia. This is illustrated in the cases of
CJS v Tasmania
Secretary of the Department of Health and Human
Services v Horacek
, where in both cases the person committed an offence
while unmedicated and, on the evidence, was not a danger to the community while
medicated. However, the concern expressed by the court was that the person may
become non-compliant with a medication regime once
the supervision order was
revoked, and so the potential for danger to the community would re-emerge. While
under both a supervision
order and a treatment order there is power to impose a
compulsory medication regime, the court was concerned about the need to renew
treatment order (in contrast to the indefinite nature of the supervision order)
and the lack of any judicial oversight. In these
circumstances, it was
considered that the long-term management regime provided by a supervision order
was necessary. Again, this
appears to be a ‘catch 22’ situation
given that there is little ability for a forensic patient to demonstrate an
to comply with treatment without a supervision order, until provided
with this opportunity. Further, Smith has argued that these
cases show that
‘too little weight is attached to the fact that the commission of the
offences occurred when a person was undiagnosed
and untreated’ while the
‘single most important aspect [from the court’s point of view] with
respect to schizophrenia
is that it requires lifelong treatment and, in their
view, mandatory
treatment’.
approach is evident in other decisions of the Supreme Court where applications
to revoke supervision orders were rejected because
the person had a chronic
mental health condition and there was a concern about the ability of a treatment
order to ensure compliance
with medication requirements.
10.3.17 However, more recently, people with schizophrenia or a
schizoaffective disorder have had supervision orders revoked. Data
from the MHT
show that since November 2010, there have been six people with schizophrenia or
a schizoaffective disorder who have
had their supervision orders revoked. Other
orders revoked since 2010 include those for four people with a primary diagnosis
of intellectual
disability and two with acquired brain injury. In this period,
there have also been three people with schizophrenia or a schizoaffective
disorder and two with intellectual disability who have had restriction orders
discharged and supervision orders made.
10.3.18 There has also been disquiet about the
inconsistency in outcomes between decisions of the MHT in issuing certificates
the Supreme Court in refusing to discharge orders once applications have
been made. Smith, then President of the Guardianship and
Administration Board
and ex officio member of the Forensic Tribunal, noted the ‘marked
difference between the approaches of
the Forensic Tribunal in issuing the

discharge’.
difference was attributed to the different legislative considerations governing
determinations of each body (see [
]) and to their different
experiences:
the members of the Tribunal, unlike the Court, regularly see a range of
people with more severe or more acute levels of disability
than the person who
is the subject of forensic orders. Such people also require lifelong treatment
and Tribunal members are familiar
with the non-punitive facilities which exist
to ensure that they have it.
However, it may be that there has been a narrowing of the division between
the approach of the MHT and the Supreme Court given that
there are no people on
restriction orders who have a current certificate and eight people with current
certificates on supervision
orders four of whom are currently making
applications for the revocation of those supervision orders.

10.3.24 The Institute received five submissions addressing the issue of
limiting terms for restriction and supervision orders to replace
the current
indefinite nature of these orders. Differing views were expressed in relation to
10.3.25 EOT argued that the indefinite nature of restriction orders risks
leading to human right breaches. EOT argued that ‘while
[Act] requires that restrictions on a defendant’s freedom should be kept
to the minimum consistent with the safety of
the community’, there are no
time limits on the period a person may be detained after a restriction order has
been made. Accordingly,
EOT observed that this has the effect that
‘persons on restriction orders may be detained for periods of time well in
of those applied to persons committing similar offences whose cases had
proceeded through the court’.
10.3.26 QAI also commented on the unfairness of indefinite detention when
compared to the sentence that a person receives following
a finding of guilt for
the same offence, and the fact that individuals with intellectual impairment or
mental impairments are treated
differently to those without such conditions. QAI
argued that contrary to the perception that a finding of unfitness is a
out of jail free card’, it may instead ‘have devastating
consequences for the accused, who may languish in forensic detention
than they would have had they been found fit and steered through the usual
criminal court process’:
It is likely that the longer people are detained the more their capacities
will diminish, particularly given the sterile environment
and limited
opportunities to use those capacities. While the courts have a duty to the
public to manage risk, the cost to the defendant
is high. The indefinite
detention of those with intellectual impairments on the basis of a risk of harm
to others is discriminatory.
Those without mental or intellectual impairments
are not, as a general rule, indefinitely detained on this basis.
10.3.27 In addition to the comparison in outcomes between a sentence and an
order made following a finding of unfitness, QAI described
some of the damaging
consequences for individuals subject to indefinite detention:
The purpose of the order may be in part therapeutic, but here in Queensland
the detainee has no idea when, or if, they will be released.
The substantive
long-term effects are de-habilitation and institutionalisation, because in
Queensland all but a few of the accused
are placed into purpose-built facilities
that in appearance and structure are little different to prisons. With lack of
use, communication
and other skills erode, and the transitioning detainee, many
years down the track, must attempt to relearn them.
QAI described the limitations of the detention process as follows:
According to anecdotal evidence, the staff at Queensland forensic facilities
lack the skills to promote [re]habilitation and reintegration,
and while there
is clinical expertise to develop behaviour support plans, there is a disconnect
between the plans and their implementation.
The plans are often extremely
detailed and complex, but their implementation is left to unskilled support
workers who do not read
them anyway.
In addition, the artificial, hierarchical, structured living arrangements
atrophies detainees’ capacities and living skills.
The longer a person
stays in detention the less likely it is that they will ever emerge from it. It
is almost impossible to prove
a reduction in risk when the person’s risk
environment is so sterile and artificial. Extraneous factors play on risk too.
matter how accurately the Court/Tribunal predicts risk the calculation still
depends upon the availability of accommodation and support
outside forensic
10.3.28 In the Tasmanian context, O’Connell’s submission
commented upon the practical delivery of rehabilitation for individuals
the SMHU. O’Connell supported a time limit restriction for restriction
orders and stated that this should be ‘perhaps
with a proviso that it can
be extended under extenuating circumstances’. In her view, this reform
would improve the delivery
of services to individuals detained under a
restriction order by providing a better framework to give effect to the

rehabilitation and least restriction:
The current legislation essentially allows for rehabilitation but in
practice, there is no onus on services to provide it. It is common
people’s leave is frequently delayed or cancelled due to staffing
pressures. The availability of suitable accommodation
is lacking and both of
these issues undoubtedly lengthen a person’s detention under a RO. Whilst
the legislation states that
principles of least restriction must apply, the
culture of the Secure Mental Health Unit is one of risk adverse practice with
increasingly
poor rehabilitative outcomes. The legislation needs amendment to
allow not only for the provision of gradual rehabilitation into
the community
but [also for] a mandated pathway for it.
O’Connell also advocated the introduction of time limits for

In my experience, the downgrading of SOs is slow and difficult and there is
no onus on the court to lift the order when certain provisions
are met. This
means that all the evidence can be supporting the downgrading of the order but
the Judge takes an opposing view to
maintain the status quo.
10.3.29 In contrast, Walvisch and the DPP supported the retention of
indefinite orders. The DPP did not consider that any change is
In my view the current system in which orders are reviewed annually by the
Mental Health Tribunal is appropriate. The system enables
regular review and
management of an order. The removal of orders should not be considered unless
and until the Tribunal considers
that an order is no longer warranted or the
conditions are inappropriate.
Whilst other jurisdictions have limiting or nominal terms for orders, I would
not recommend adopting such an approach. These orders
focus on community
protection and therapeutic care determined by a person’s treatment needs.
In reality it is the Tribunal
who is best placed to make the initial assessment
as to the appropriateness of the orders. If the Tribunal issues a certificate
an application is made to the court the factors contained in ss 34 and 35 of
the Act will be considered.
10.3.30 Walvisch also supported retaining indefinite orders for restriction
and supervision orders under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas). However, he proposed changes to the current system to facilitate
the discharge of orders. Walvisch stressed that in considering
this issue:
it is essential to keep in mind the purpose which restriction and supervision
orders serve. Given that people subject to such orders
have not been convicted
of an offence, it is clear that they do not serve a penal purpose — their
aim is not to punish. Instead,
they serve a protective function, aiming to
prevent people who have committed acts that are ordinarily classified as
from harming other people. Consequently, as far as
possible their reach should be limited to the supervision of people who continue
to pose a risk to others.
Bearing this in mind, I am supportive of the introduction of measures that
seek to provide a safeguard against the arbitrary and indefinite
detention of
people who no longer pose a risk to the community. In an era of increasing
punitivism and risk aversion, it is essential
that there be specific measures
that ensure that people are not detained for longer than their condition
10.3.31 However, Walvisch opposed the setting limiting of nominal terms based
on a method akin to imposing sentence given that the
person has not been found
guilty, and so is not blameworthy and is not being sentenced for what they have
done. He wrote that:
maintaining a link between the required term of supervision and the maximum
penalty/hypothetical sentence implicitly suggests that
the individual bears some
responsibility for the offence, thereby undermining the purpose of the qualified
acquittal. For example,
requiring a person who is found not guilty of murder
because of insanity to serve a longer period of supervision than a person found
not guilty of rape because of insanity suggests that the former person is more
culpable than the latter. Whilst this may be the popular
perception of the issue
(with the public perhaps thinking that a person receiving a qualified acquittal
for murder should serve a
longer time under supervision), it is not the purpose
of the order. The length of supervision should solely be related to the question
of whether or not the person continues to pose a risk to the community
10.3.32 Instead, Walvisch proposed reforms
(discussed further below) to ensure that any restriction imposed is for the
shortest time
necessary to reflect the risk posed to the community. The
necessary reforms include:
• maintaining a system of regular review by the MHT where the timing of
the reviews is not referrable to the alleged offence
but should be held
• specifying that the purpose of the review is to determine whether the
individual continues to pose a sufficient risk to the
community to justify
continuing the current restrictions on his or her liberty, and thus whether or
not the supervision or restriction
order should be maintained or varied;
• incorporating a presumption in favour of reducing the level of
supervision, unless there is evidence that members of the public
seriously endangered as a result of reducing the supervision status of the
• giving the MHT an appealable power to vary, discharge or revoke
orders made under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) 1999
Walvisch argued that while this approach maintains a system of indefinite
detention (with its drawbacks), the impact of the ‘drawbacks
limited by a strong presumption that the level of supervision should be reduced,
and the capacity of the MHT to vary, discharge
or revoke orders’. In his
view, under such a system, ‘it is anticipated that the term of supervision
for many offenders
would be significantly reduced’.
10.3.33 The Institute recognises that this is complex issue. As noted, the
reason for having the doctrine of fitness of stand trial
and a defence of mental
impairment and cognitive impairment rest on notions of fairness to the
individual — that it is not
fair to require someone to participate in a
trial if they are unable to do so and that it is not fair to attribute criminal
responsibility
to those who are not able to act otherwise. It then seems
counterintuitive to detain such people indefinitely under restriction orders
to subject them to indefinite supervision under supervision orders when this
would not happen if they were found guilty and given
finite sentences.
Accordingly, there are strong arguments in favour of introducing limiting terms
on the grounds of fairness. Further,
limiting terms may encourage individuals to
rely on unfitness and/or the defence of mental impairment where this is
appropriate by
removing the uncertainty of an indefinite order. This will enable
people to receive appropriate rehabilitation and treatment in the
system rather than in prison. The knowledge that orders are for a finite period
may also facilitate better pathway planning
for reintegration into the
10.3.34 On the other hand, the rationales for imposing forensic orders are
conceptually different to imposing criminal sanctions —
the person has not
been found guilty and is not sentenced by the court. This distinction appears to
be undermined if the term of
a forensic order is set on the basis of a
‘hypothetical sentence’. Different considerations do apply where an
has not been found guilty and is not sentenced and so a forensic
order is imposed on a forward looking (risk posed by individual)
rather than
backward looking (proportionate sentence for the offence). However, that said,
there are very limited circumstances where
a court is able to detain individuals
found guilty on basis of prospective risk (other than dangerous offender
legislation which
is infrequently
10.3.35 After considering the competing views and the submissions received,
the Institute’s view is that indefinite detention
should be abolished and
limiting terms should be introduced. While the introduction of the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) was intended to provide a pathway
to release, it would appear that this objective has not been effectively
realised. Moreover,
while further reforms could be made to the indefinite
detention/supervision scheme to facilitate a more predictable and accessible
pathway to release, the Institute’s concern is that this model is too
susceptible to an undue focus on risk and prevention
rather than rehabilitation.
In addition, the Institute’s assessment is that current system does not
reflect the Act’s
underlying principles, in that it does not provide an
appropriate balance between individual freedom and autonomy and community
In fact, the Institute’s view is that the current model may
potentially impede community safety by acting as a disincentive
for offenders to
seek special orders and that the protection of forensic patients and the
community would be better served by the
introduction of a finite order.
10.3.36 The uncertain nature of release for individuals subject to an
indefinite order and the likelihood that many individuals will
spend longer
under supervision than if sentenced by a court acts as a disincentive to rely on
fitness to stand trial and/or the defence
of insanity in circumstances where
this may well be appropriate. As discussed at [
], there are significant issues with
the treatment and rehabilitation services available to individuals with mental
health impairments
and cognitive impairments who are incarcerated. Clearly,
these unmet needs have significant consequences for the individual but also
the community. While the community is protected while the person is in prison,
this protection is only short-term and does not
address community safety in the
longer term. Indefinite terms also affect the practical delivery of
rehabilitation services for forensic
patients and the Institute’s view is
that a finite term will provide a clearer framework to plan for the release from
order. There is also clear support for this approach in other
found unfit and not acquitted following a special hearing), the Australian
Capital Territory, South Australia and the
Commonwealth. It was also recommended
by the ALRC, a Senate Committee, the SASAC and the LRCWA.
10.3.37 Although the Institute is recommending the introduction of limiting
terms for restriction and supervision orders, the Institute
still recognises the
legitimate concerns about the unpredictability of progress in a
defendant’s treatment and rehabilitation
in these cases. While the
Institute does not recommend the retention of indefinite detention as the
appropriate response to these
concerns, the Institute’s view is that the
model that should be adopted is the introduction of limiting terms
the introduction of a preventative detention scheme that would allow for an
extension of the person’s forensic patient status.
A forensic patient
should also be able to apply for an order to be removed or varied prior the end
of the limiting term. Accordingly,
Recommendations 40, 42 and 43 are to be read
Recommendation
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) should be
amended by abolishing the indefinite nature of forensic orders and introducing
limiting terms.
Setting the limiting terms for restriction
and supervision orders in Tasmania
10.3.38 Once a limiting term is adopted for
restriction and supervision orders in Tasmania, it is necessary to decide the
which the limiting term is set.
10.3.39 In addressing the process of setting the limiting term, the NSWLRC
identified four basic models:
(1) Using the
hypothetical sentence
that would have been imposed had
the person been convicted in the ordinary way of the
This is the current
approach in New South Wales, the Commonwealth and the Australian Capital
(2) Using a
modified sentencing approach
such as exists in South
Australia, where the hypothetical sentence is imposed on the basis of the
objective facts only. Another variation
would be to specify that it relates to
the hypothetical non-parole period rather than the total sentence or to provide
that certain
mitigating factors are to be presumed (such as a guilty plea or
(3) Using a
fixed statutory formula
to determine the limiting
(4) Using a
time limit formulated by adopting a risk management approach
that determines a time limit taking into account ‘the likelihood of
rehabilitation, the likely length and success of treatment
(and impact on
offending behaviour) and future

and against the different models considered by the NSWLRC for setting the

10.3.41 The application of the hypothetical sentence approach is the
preferred approach in other jurisdictions. In other words, the
term is set by
reference to the sentence of imprisonment the court would have imposed if the
person had been found guilty in an ordinary
criminal trial. This means that, so
far as is possible, general sentencing principles such as retribution,
denunciation, proportionality
apply ‘as well as special principles that
apply when sentencing offenders with cognitive and mental health
impairments’.
as noted, it is recognised that this approach is ‘not without
difficulties, both conceptually and in
10.3.42 This approach was reaffirmed as the most appropriate approach by the
NSWLRC. This was also the recommendation of the
However, to address
some of the limitations of the model, the NSWLRC also recommended the following
measures to make sure that the
hypothetical sentence approach does not
overestimate the commensurate sentence:
(a) The court should estimate the sentence as if the person has been found
guilty at a normal trial, and also taking into account
the person’s
cognitive or mental health impairment; and
(b) In order to make sure that the limiting term is fair in comparison with
those imposed where a person is found guilty at a normal
trial, the court should
have a broad discretion to discount the sentence taking into account that it may
not be possible to demonstrate
particular mitigating factors such as remorse or
a guilty plea.
