limiting term should be set for supervision and restriction
orders if one is
adopted in Tasmania.
If there is a time limit for restriction and supervision orders under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas), on what basis should
it be determined?
10.3.44 The Institute received two submissions that addressed the method of
setting the limiting term.
10.3.45 As noted above, Walvisch did not support the introduction of limiting
terms given his views about the inappropriateness of
using sentencing
principles/maximum penalties as a reference point. Walvisch argued that:
The maximum penalty for an offence bears no connection to the time at which
it is appropriate to assess the risk a person may continue
to pose to the
community. That is an issue that relates to matters such as the nature of the
person’s impairment and the treatment
available for that impairment,
rather than the penalty for a charged offence.
For a similar reason, the measures should also not refer to the sentence that
would have been appropriate if the person had been convicted
of the offence
concerned. The extent of a person’s hypothetical blameworthiness for an
offence for which they have not been
convicted (even if modified to take into
account specific mitigating factors) bears no connection to the length of time
they should
serve under a supervision order before a review or release.
While EOT acknowledged the concerns about setting limiting terms based on a
sentencing approach, the submission expressed support
for the model proposed by
Legal Aid NSW. In their model, Legal Aid NSW proposed that nominal or limiting
terms be set equivalent
to the non-parole periods of the sentences that would
have been imposed had the person been found guilty. This approach was advocated
by EOT as providing an identifiable release date which would benefit the
wellbeing of the individual and conform to broader human
rights concerns:
Importantly, it would also provide those in detention with identifiable
future release dates which they could work toward. It would
also satisfy the
requirement to introduce improved human rights safeguards for those on
restriction orders. Under the present arrangements
persons who are detained may
express feelings of frustration and hopelessness about the uncertainty of their
future, in large part
because of the uncertainty of their period of detention.
Having no expected future release date gives little motivation to aim for
self-improvement or to address the underlying factors contributing toward their
original offending behaviour.
10.3.46 Clearly, there are conceptual and practical difficulties in setting a
limiting period regardless of the method chosen (see
Table 8.10). However, the
Institute does not consider that the difficulties in setting limits should mean
that indefinite terms should
be retained. Therefore, regardless of the problems
that have been identified, the Institute’s view is that a method should
adopted to allow courts to set the term. On this basis, the Institute’s
view is that the best approach to setting the limiting
terms is to adopt the
hypothetical sentencing method. This means that the court identifies the
hypothetical sentence that would have
been imposed had the person been convicted
in the ordinary way of the offence. The Institute recognises that there is a
artificiality in such a process. Furthermore, inevitably there are
concerns about linking the length of an order imposed following
a qualified
acquittal or where a finding cannot be made that the defendant is not guilty,
with the setting of terms using sentencing
principles. However, this approach
appears to be favoured by a majority of jurisdictions that have adopted limiting
terms. It addresses
issues of arbitrariness and fairness arising from the
current indefinite period of detention and allows a term to be set that is
broadly comparable with the sentence that would have been imposed.
10.3.47 In recommending that limiting terms be
based on hypothetical sentences, the Institute stresses that the objectives of
forensic system are different to the objectives of sentencing. Even though
the limiting term is set by reference to a notional sentence,
the limiting term
is not intended to be a sentence and does not operate as a sentence in the
ordinary way. The limiting term is instead
a signal to the individual and the
forensic mental health system that there is a defined release date in the
future, and the intention
is that the treatment and rehabilitation of the person
should be focused on release by that date. However, this does not preclude
earlier release or longer restriction/supervision where this is appropriate.
Recommendations
41. In imposing a forensic order under the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas), the court must nominate a limiting term, which
would be the court’s best estimate of the length of the sentence that
would have been imposed had the person been found guilty at a normal trial (a
hypothetical sentence).
42. A forensic patient should be able to apply to the Supreme Court for a

Consequences of reaching the limiting term
10.3.48 The consequences of reaching the limiting
term for the release of the individual must also be addressed. The term could be
an absolute limit (ie, the time at which the individual is released) or a limit
that is imposed with an option to extend. As noted,
a system of limiting terms
does not preclude an individual making an application to have their order
revoked or varied prior the
expiry of the limiting term (if the legislative test
is satisfied).
However, if a
limiting term is adopted, it is necessary to decide the consequences of reaching
the term set. It is necessary to determine
whether the limiting term would
operate as an
absolute limit
(as in the Australian Capital Territory and
the Commonwealth) or a
limit imposed with provision to extend
of restriction or supervision prior to the end of the limiting term (as exists
in New South Wales where limiting terms
apply for unfitness matters following a
special hearing and South Australia).

10.3.54 On the other hand, there are strong arguments against a preventative
detention scheme, including that forensic patients ‘should
not be subject
to further restriction on their liberty following the expiry of their limiting
term, apart from those that may be
imposed under the civil
reason, many stakeholders in the NSW review process expressed considerable
concern about the creation of a preventative
Further, preventative
detention schemes for forensic patients are said to violate Art 14 of the
which ‘prohibits the deprivation of liberty on the basis of
actual or perceived impairment even if additional factors or criteria
used to justify the deprivation of
generally, preventative detention schemes that apply to convicted offenders are
controversial because they offend against the
principles of proportionality and
finality in sentencing, they amount to double punishment and because prediction
of future risk
problematic.
As the TLRI has
written previously:
an assessment of the merits and the use of indefinite and preventative
detention regimes necessarily involves the balancing of potentially
conflicting
rights of victims, offenders, and society as a whole. This conflict of rights
unsurprisingly results in tensions at the
policy level, as Parliament and the
judiciary must grapple with the balance of ostensibly irreconcilable interests.
Secondly, indefinite
and preventative detention regimes are justified as a means
of preventing future harm, but the task of predicting future dangerousness
fraught with uncertainty and predictive models have been extensively criticised
for a tendency to over-predict
Such schemes are also contrary to international human rights instruments and
for this reason the courts and international tribunals
have stressed that their
powers must be exercised only in exceptional
circumstances.

10.3.57 EOT suggested an alternative approach to
the consequences of a limiting term for restriction orders. This derived from a
suggested by Legal Aid NSW. EOT’s view was that two periods should
be set for limiting terms: (1) a non-parole period; and
(2) a sentence. Based on
these two periods, EOT suggested the following approach:
• During the non-parole period, a person should only be released from a
restriction order if a certificate is issued by the
MHT and confirmed by the
Supreme Court;
• After the non-parole period has expired, the presumption should be
that the person is released in the following sequence:
o Conditional release at the end of the non-parole period
o Unconditional release at the end of the sentence period.
It was argued that this would ‘shift the legal presumption against
detention’ after the expiry of the notional non-parole
Importantly, it would not derogate the current powers of the Mental Health
Tribunal to issue a certificate that would enable the release
of a person
conditionally or unconditionally prior to the expiry of their nominal term. Nor
would it prevent the Supreme Court from
putting in place other orders or
conditions to come into effect on release after a nominal non-parole period,
such as a treatment
This model creates a rebuttable presumption of unconditional release at the
expiry of the sentence period, which means that the court
can order either
conditional release or no release at that time.
10.3.58 As indicated, the Institute’s view is that if limiting terms
are introduced for forensic orders, provision should be
made to allow those
orders to be extended in exceptional cases. Despite legitimate disquiet about
the implications for human rights
of preventative detention, the
Institute’s view is that accommodation needs to be made for the
unpredictability of treatment
and rehabilitation. A power for the prosecution to
apply to the court to extend the term of a restriction or supervision order is
necessary to address the issues of community protection in circumstances where
it would be unsafe to release a forensic patient at
the end of a finite term.
Accordingly, the Institute proposes a ‘half-way’ model between
indefinite detention (where
the onus is on the individual to justify release)
and a finite term where an individual is automatically released at the end of
term in all cases.
Recommendation
43. A limiting term should not operate as an absolute limit on the duration
of an order. There should be power for the prosecution
to make an application to
extend the order.

10.3.60 After considering these options, the NSWLRC recommended (and the

that apply to

10.3.61 Key features of the NSW model are that:
(a) The Supreme Court makes the order on the basis of a twofold assessment of
the unacceptable risk of serious harm to others posed
by the forensic patient if
released from detention or supervision and the inadequacy of other less
restrictive means of managing
Considerations that the court has regard to include: the safety of the
community, expert reports, any orders or decisions of the MHRT,
person’s level of compliance with any obligations imposed while being a
forensic patient, especially while they were on
leave or conditional release;
the view of the court at the time the limiting term was imposed and a report
from the forensic patient’s
treatment team which would include information
arrangements
that do not involve continued supervision or detention are not
appropriate.
(b) The term of the extension order is five years subject to further
(c) The MHRT conducts regular reviews.
(d) The Supreme Court can revoke the extension order on the ground that
circumstances have changed significantly so as to render the
extension order
unnecessary.

10.3.62 In the Issues Paper, the Institute sought feedback as to the most

10.3.64 The Institute’s view is that the NSW
model should be adopted in Tasmania to allow for the extension of orders beyond
the limiting term set by the court, with appropriate changes to reflect the
Tasmanian approach to decision-making and the role of
the MHT. This model is
based on the approach to the scheme of preventative detention that applies in
New South Wales for the continued
supervision or detention of high-risk sex or
violent offenders. It recognises the need to balance the risk of the discharge
or revocation
of the order with the least restriction principle and accommodates
the role of the MHT and the Supreme Court in the process. Accordingly,
Institute’s recommendation is that the scheme for the extension of orders
should have the following key features:
1. The DPP should be able to make an application to the Supreme Court prior
to the expiration of the limiting term for an extension
of a restriction order
or a supervision order. The DPP should also be able to apply for a restriction
order to be replaced with a
supervision order instead of an extension of the
restriction order.
2. The Supreme Court should consider the request for the extension order on
the basis of a twofold assessment of the unacceptable
risk of serious harm to
others posed by the forensic patient if released from detention or supervision
and the inadequacy of other
less restrictive means of managing the risk. This
regime reflects the principles contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
, which provides that the ‘court is to
apply, where appropriate, the principle that restrictions on the
defendant’s freedom
and personal autonomy should be kept to the minimum
consistent with the safety of the community.’
3. Considerations that the court should have regard to include:
• the safety of the community
• expert reports
• any certificates issued by the MHT
• the person’s level of compliance with any obligations imposed
while being a forensic patient, especially while they
were on leave
• the view of the court at the time the limiting term was imposed
• a report from the forensic patient’s treatment team which would
include information about the need for ongoing management
of the person and the
reasons why arrangements that do not involve continued supervision or detention
are not appropriate
• a report on the attitudes of victims, if any, and next of kin
(These factors are broadly consistent with the provisions set out in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
4. The term of the extension order should be five years subject to further
5. The MHT should be mandated to conduct annual reviews of the extended
forensic order.
6. The Supreme Court should have the power to make an interim extension order
if it appears that the limiting term or existing extension
order will expire
before the proceedings are determined and that the matters alleged in the
supporting documentation would, if proved,
justify the making of an extension
7. The Supreme Court should at any time be able to vary or revoke an
extension order on the application of the DPP or the person subject
forensic order if satisfied that circumstances have changed sufficiently to
render the order unnecessary.

Test for making,
varying, discharging or revoking orders under the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
10.3.65 In Tasmania, the
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) sets out the test for the Supreme Court
to apply when making, varying, discharging or revoking orders. The same
apply to all decision-making by the Supreme Court and are discussed
in detail at [
]. As noted, these provisions aim
to provide an appropriate balance between the need to protect the safety of the
community and the
principle of least restriction with the defendant’s
freedom and personal autonomy. In making this assessment, there is a focus
the legislation (and by the court) on the likely danger that the defendant poses
to other people if released. In other words,
a key feature of the test is an
assessment of whether the defendant is, or would if released be, likely to
another person or other persons generally.
10.3.66 This section of the Report focuses on whether there is a need to
reform the test of endangerment contained in the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) for making an assessment of whether to detain a
person under a supervision order, impose a supervision order or a treatment
order or release a person conditionally or unconditionally. In addition, it
considers the basis on which a forensic patient should
be released from
detention or supervision before the end of the limiting term. Consideration is
also given to whether it is desirable
to introduce statutory presumptions (in
favour of release or detention) that would operate to guide the Supreme
Court’s decision-making
when considering the release of an individual
before the end of the limiting terms. Accordingly, it is necessary to
(a) on what basis an order should be made, and
(b) on what basis a person can be discharged or have an order varied prior to
the expiry of the limiting term.
10.3.67 In the Issues Paper, the Institute outlined several reform options
that could be considered in the context of the Tasmanian
forensic mental health
system. These included:
(a) revisiting the role of dangerousness in making, varying and revoking
(b) including statutory presumptions in relation to the detention or release
of defendants;
(c) making changes to the model of decision-making;
(d) making changes to the leave system.
The endangerment test
10.3.68 The NSWLRC identified two common features in cases following a
finding at a special hearing or a finding of not guilty by
(a) the absence of established criminal responsibility, and therefore the
absence of any principled basis for punishment; and
(b) the possibility that the person’s cognitive or mental health
impairment may give rise to a risk of harm, and a consequent
restrictions on the person’s liberty to ensure the safety of the community
This means that punishment is irrelevant in these cases. Accordingly, the
limits of the person’s detention are guided by the
requirement to achieve
a balance between community safety and the individual’s freedom and
personal autonomy,
by the concept of risk. However, as McSherry has written, ‘the problem
really lies in drawing up appropriate criteria
which will lead to the detention
of those who really may do harm in the future, whilst allowing for the absolute
discharge of those
who do not pose a risk to the
As indicated,
the test in Tasmania is ‘likely to endanger’.

Need for reform
. Other jurisdictions have reviewed the
circumstances in which a person may be made a forensic patient and the process
by which the
order is able to be varied or discharged and have examined the
principles and factors that should be considered by the court. This
has generally focused on the concepts of risk of harm and dangerousness.
10.3.72 In Victoria, the VLRC sought feedback on whether the concept of
endangerment is an appropriate basis for the court’s
assessment of whether
to detain or release a forensic patient. A common view was that
‘endangerment’ is an imprecise
concept that involves too much
subjective interpretation.
The NSWLRC considered that there is ‘very little guidance on what
[endangerment] meant’.
Similarly, other commentators have observed that the focus on dangerousness
provides little guidance as to how the court should exercise
discretion. They argue that this has resulted in the courts according
‘higher weight to factors relating to community
protection than factors
related to treatment and ongoing evidence of mental
concluded that a test based on an assessment of dangerousness is inadequate as
it is inconsistent with the test for determining
the detention of a patient in
the civil mental health system (which requires an assessment of whether it is
necessary for the protection
of the person or others from serious harm). It is
also inconsistent with contemporary language of risk
assessment.
The notion of
dangerousness is said to be inappropriate because it is ‘a vague and
unhelpful way of expressing the risk of
a particular individual causing
Instead, the
NSWLRC suggested that the language of risk better reflects the approach taken by
clinicians and health professionals
in the mental health
10.3.73 In response to concerns about the inadequacy of the current test, the
NSWLRC recommended ‘a risk-based threshold for
release [that] should
require that there be no significant risk of serious
This test would constitute an appropriate hurdle that protects both community
safety and the interests of the
individual.
In Victoria, a
different approach was recommended to address concerns about the language of
‘dangerousness’ and to move
to a ‘risk’-based test. The
VLRC recommended that ‘an “unacceptable risk” [is] an
appropriate measure
of the likelihood of risk the supervised person
This approach
was proposed for four reasons:
(1) it demonstrates that ‘there is some level of risk that will be
acceptable and will counteract any assumption that the person
must prove that
they pose no risk before their level of supervision can be
(2) it ‘recognises that assessing risk requires a level of subjective
judgment by the decision-maker on the level of risk that
society is prepared to
accept when balanced against the supervised person’s right to liberty and
(3) although ‘it incorporates some level of social judgement. A test
based on unacceptable risk is more in line with modern
risk assessment than a
test based on
dangerousness’;
(4) it ‘will encourage decision-makers to engage in more nuanced
assessments of a supervised person’s risk, rather than
a “black and
white” assessment of whether a person is “dangerous” or
dangerous”’.
10.3.74 The VLRC also suggested that the test should specify the type of harm
it seeks to prevent in order to provide clarity to decision-makers
and experts
applying the test. Accordingly, it recommended a test based on physical or
psychological harm to a
In New South Wales,
‘serious harm’ is nominated as the degree of harm to be judged
because it aligns closely with the
approach adopted in the civil mental health

10.3.76 Two submissions to the Institute addressed these questions. The DPP
stated that ‘the decision making is in accordance
with the principles
encapsulated in the legislation’ and it was his view that the current

10.3.77 After considering the competing views, the
Institute has concerns about the current test for the making, varying and
of the orders which requires the court to determine whether the
applicant is ‘likely to endanger’ the community. As noted,
is imprecise because its focuses on the likelihood of danger materialising
(understood to be a real chance or possibility)
rather than the level of harm.
Moreover, this approach does not reflect contemporary approaches to risk
assessment. Accordingly,
the Institute’s view is that the test should be
changed by removing the imprecise concept of ‘dangerousness’ and
replacing it with a test that sets out the nature of the harm contemplated.
Further, the Institute considers that the test should
be reformulated so that it
requires a risk assessment. This accords with modern risk assessment approaches
that move away from the
conflation of mental illness with dangerousness. With
regard to these matters, the VLRC’s recommended model provides a useful
precedent for Tasmania. In accordance with that model, the Institute recommends
that the level of harm should be an unacceptable
risk of causing physical or
psychological harm to a person or persons generally. The use of
‘unacceptable’ risk recognises
that some level of risk is acceptable
and allows the court to balance the level of risk that society is prepared to
accept that is
consistent with the person’s freedom and personal autonomy.
(other options unacceptable risk to the safety of the community/no
significant
risk of serious harm).
Recommendation
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
should be changed to refer to whether the defendant does, or would if released,
pose an unacceptable risk of causing physical or
psychological harm to a person
or persons generally.

Need for statutory presumptions in relation to detention/supervision
10.3.80 In Tasmania, there is currently no
presumption in favour of release or detention/supervision based on the
assessment of risk
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas). Rather, the court makes its decision in relation to variation,
discharge or revocation by balancing a number of factors prescribed
Position in other jurisdictions
. Jurisdictions also differ
with respect to their statutory presumptions for and against release or
detention. For example, in New
South Wales, the MHRT must not release a forensic
patient unless it is satisfied that the public will not be seriously endangered.
This creates a presumption in favour of detention unless the decision-maker is
satisfied that the public will not be seriously endangered.
This presumption
applies to those found not guilty by reason of insanity and to those found not
fit to stand trial and not acquitted
at a special hearing prior to expiry of the
limiting term. This is also the case under the
Crimes Act 1914
and in Victoria
during the nominal term.
contrast, in some jurisdictions the decision-maker must order the release of a
forensic patient unless satisfied that the person
poses a risk (as defined in
that jurisdiction). This creates a presumption in favour of release. This is the
case in the Northern
Territory and in Victoria (after the expiry of the nominal
In some jurisdictions
there is no presumption in favour of release or detention — this is the
case in Tasmania and Western
The need for reform
. If a system of limiting terms is adopted
in Tasmania, consideration should be given to introducing statutory presumptions
release or reduced supervision prior to the expiry of the limiting term.
This is the approach in New South Wales, where there is
a presumption in favour
of detention when making decisions about release prior to the end of the
limiting term. The presumption is
that a ‘person will remain in detention
unless it can be positively established that their release ... will not present
Mental Health (Forensic Provisions) Act 1990
provides that
the court ‘must not make an order for the release of a forensic patient
unless it is satisfied ... that the safety
of the patient or any member of the
public will not be seriously endangered by the patient’s release’.
The NSWLRC recommended
that this presumption should be retained because it is
necessary for community
There is also a
presumption against release in South Australia, where the
Criminal Law
Consolidation Act 1935
(SA) provides that ‘the court cannot release a
defendant ... or significantly reduce the amount of supervision ... unless the
court is satisfied, on the balance of probabilities, that the safety of the
person or any member of the public will not be seriously
endangered by the
person’s release’. This is also the case in Queensland and under the
Crimes Act 1994
10.3.83 Accordingly, in the Issues Paper, the Institute asked:
If a system of limiting terms is adopted in Tasmania, should a presumption
against release or reduced supervision be created prior
to the expiry of the
limiting term and then a presumption in favour of release from
detention/discharge from supervision after the
expiry of the limiting term?
10.3.84 Only one submission to the Institute addressed this question. EOT
supported the introduction of a system of limiting terms
in Tasmania, and as
indicated at [
], considered that
there should be shifting presumptions within the model proposed. A court should
be required to set a nominal non-parole
period and a nominal head sentence when
imposing a supervision or restriction order. In EOT’s view, there should
be a presumption
in favour of conditional release at the end of the non-parole

whether a presumption against release or reduced supervision should be

the legislation to specify that the court must not release the

that this not necessary. There are clearly enumerated legislative factors and
principles that give effect to the need to
ensure that any order is consistent
with the safety of the community. There is also the need to give explicit
consideration to the
risk of harm to the community.
Recommendation
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) should not
be amended to introduce a presumption that prior to the expiry of the limiting
term a court cannot discharge a restriction
order or revoke a supervision order
unless the court is satisfied in relation to the risk of harm test.
Making changes to the current decision-making model
10.3.86 In Tasmania,
the MHT reviews
forensic orders within 12 months of their making and then in each 12-month
period afterwards. The MHT can issue a
certificate if it determines that a
forensic order is no longer warranted or that the conditions of the order are
not appropriate.
However, it does not have the power itself to discharge or vary
the forensic order. This can only be done by the Supreme
Accordingly, there is
usually a two-step process (MHT review and Supreme Court hearing) to revoke or
discharge forensic orders, and
this may operate as a barrier for forensic
patients making an application to the Supreme Court to revoke or discharge an
Data from the MHT show
that it is not uncommon for a person to have received several certificates at
reviews conducted by the MHT
before a court application is made to discharge the
restriction or supervision order. Further, there are some individuals on
supervision
orders who have received numerous certificates from the MHT and have
not made applications to the Supreme Court for the revocation
of their orders.
Nevertheless, the two-step process may be viewed as providing an additional
safeguard for the community given that
a person’s mental health may
fluctuate. In this context, it is noted that the MHT data show that there are
some patients who
receive certificates at the MHT review (and who remain on the
forensic order) and subsequently have a review hearing where no certificate
issued. In addition, the Institute has identified differing approaches to the
issue of certificates by the MHT and the discharge
of orders by the Supreme
10.3.87 As identified in the Issues Paper, a possible way to deal with
difficulties in varying or discharging forensic orders is to
empower the MHT to
vary, discharge or revoke orders made under the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) 1999
(Tas). Some of these difficulties may, of course, be
ameliorated by the introduction of finite terms for forensic orders and by
changes to the test to be applied in varying or removing them. However,
these measures do not exclude the possibility of arming the
MHT with an
appealable power to vary, discharge or revoke orders prior to the end of the
limiting term. Extending the power of the
MHT in this way would remove the
unnecessary duplication in the current two-step process where the MHT reviews
matters and issues
certificates, but the forensic patient must then apply to the
Supreme Court for variation or discharge of forensic orders. The MHT
already has
power to make, vary, renew and discharge treatment orders and to determine leave
from the SMHU for patients subject to
restriction
Any disquiet about
extending the MHT’s powers in relation to forensic orders might be allayed
by making its decisions appealable.
This model would accord with the approach in
many other jurisdictions, as recognised by the

of people subject to forensic orders have been adopted

10.3.92 After considering this issue, the Institute recommends that the dual
process of decision-making be retained so that the MHT
conducts annual reviews
and the Supreme Court makes decisions about the variation, discharge or
revocation of forensic orders prior
to the expiration of the limiting term. The
Institute believes that this model is most likely to ensure public confidence in
process. However, as noted, the Institute acknowledges that there are
problems with the current process including impediments to
applications being
made to the Supreme Court, a duplication of process and no legislative
requirement for the Supreme Court to give
weight to the MHT’s
recommendations. Accordingly, the Institute recommends the enactment of a
requirement for the court to
take account of the certificates and
recommendations made by the MHT and of a presumption in favour of varying,
revoking or discharging
a forensic order if a certificate is issued.

Amendment to leave provisions
Boyd-Caine and Chappell
have observed that leave privileges:
are critical to the patient’s ability to progress through the forensic
system towards release. In the first instance, leave
privileges provide an
opportunity for patients to be tested in terms of responsibility, trust, insight
into their illness, and the
general progress of their rehabilitation. Leave
privileges are also gradual steps towards greater liberty and access to family,
and the broader
Leave in Tasmania is also critical to rehabilitation of forensic patients and
operates over the longer term with the aim of supporting
and facilitating the
discharge of restriction orders. However, this is not explicitly recognised in
Mental Health Act 2013
In the Issues Paper, an option identified for reform is to make changes to
the leave system so that there are more options in relation
to the type of leave
available, including the ability of the MHT to make extended leave orders to
provide forensic patients with
an opportunity to demonstrate their progress
towards rehabilitation. This reflects a graduated approach to release from
and is closely tied to rehabilitation and
reintegration.
It would also
address the concerns raised by the court in
of the supervision order as it would allow a person on a restriction order to
live outside the secure mental
health unit for extended periods of time (with
the ability to move the person back to the SMHU if required).
10.3.99 In Victoria, there is a unique system of leave that ‘specifies
the type of leave that is available based on the location
and duration of the
summarised the types of leave as
(a) Special leave – this allows a forensic patient on a custodial
supervision order to leave their place of detention and receive
services for a
period not exceeding seven days for the purposes of receiving medical treatment,
or 24 hours for non-medical treatment
purposes. This can be granted by an
authorised psychiatrist and can be suspended by the Chief Psychiatrist.
(b) On-ground leave – this allows a forensic patient to leave their
place of detention and receive services but requires them
‘within the surrounds’ of the place of detention. It can be
authorised by the Forensic Leave Panel and suspended
by the Chief
Psychiatrist.
(c) Limited off-ground leave – this allows a forensic patient, for a
maximum of six months, to leave their place of detention
between the hours of 6
am and 9 pm and outside those hours on a maximum of three days in any seven-day
period. It can be authorised
by the Forensic Leave Panel and suspended by the
Chief Psychiatrist.
(d) Extended leave – this allows forensic patients to leave the place
where they are being detained for a period of time not
exceeding 12 months. The
Supreme Court can make an extended leave order and it can be revoked by the
Chief Psychiatrist. It is also
a requirement that a custodial supervision order
not be varied unless a 12-month period of extended leave has been

Step-down/step-up facilities
Reviews conducted in

10.3.102 In South Australia, the SASAC reported that there is a recognised

and being released — either conditionally or unconditionally — into
the community.
forensic facility in South Australia is James Nash House and it has a

while the court was able
to order the release of a person from detention to release under licence in the
community to reside at Ashton
House, Ashton House did not operate as a step-up
There was no provision
for the Clinical Director to return a person to James Nash House without
applying to the court to suspend
or revoke a
Accordingly, the
SASAC concluded that there needed to be more flexibility to allow licensees to

clinical need with the MHT being empowered to assist with the efficient
operation of the
step-up and step-down process without need to apply to the
10.3.103 The VLRC also considered that incorporating more flexibility in

supporting the person and would be consistent with recovery oriented
It recommended the

an intermediate step between the high-security
facility of Thomas Embling Hospital and community
accommodation’.
could also be used where people have breached conditions of a non-custodial
supervision order.

10.3.106 The Institute received two submission in
response to these questions.
10.3.107 O’Connell made observations in relation to need for a
step-down model:
In relation to the need for a medium secure unit, I do agree that a step-down
model is required. There is a stand-alone unit within
the WLC grounds that is
not utilized but could be used as a medium secure unit with access to more
lenient leave provisions. A forensic
hostel (as used in the UK) would also be
There is no
doubt that the poverty of appropriate supported accommodation delays
people’s rehabilitation and release by years. Community-based
supervision
is a much more cost effective option compared to a SMHU.
O’Connell believed that to address problems in the current system, the
legislation needed to be amended to ‘not only allow
for a provision of
gradual rehabilitation into the community but a mandated pathway for it’.
As noted, O’Connell highlighted
a lack services available to provide for
rehabilitation under the current system, with leave frequently cancelled or
delayed due
to staffing pressures.
10.3.108 EOT also believed that a legislative pathway for discharge from a
restriction order would lead to better planning to release
(and so facilitate
the reintegration of the person into the community):
The adoption of legislative changes enabling conditional or unconditional
release after a fixed period of detention would increase
the importance of
rigorous release planning, including the identification of community-based
support and rehabilitation pathways.
Whereas secure mental health facilities such as the Wilfred Lopes Centre may
be successful in addressing acute illness and provide
for the needs of high risk
patients, there is also a need to provide transitional arrangements that
encourage behaviour improvements
and enable those who are on restriction orders
to transition to community living options.
Ongoing support and risk management is best achieved by a graduated
transition from a forensic disability setting to a supported
‘step-down’
option and thereon to eventual release into the
community. This would enable the implementation of ongoing risk management
arrangements
to ensure both the client and the community are safeguarded and
that individuals are connected with the appropriate supports and
services to
meet their individual reintegration needs.
Whilst the Mental Health Tribunal currently has the capacity to determine
applications for leave from secure mental health units for
patients subject to
restriction orders, there are limited options outside of the Wilfred Lopes
Centre available for those who would
benefit from a more step-wise approach.
What this means in practice is that there are few transitional arrangements
between detention and release. Calls have previously been
made for a second
secure mental health centre similar to a group home for this purpose.
Providing a range of forensic services would require close and cooperative
multi-agency collaboration, with independent health and
social care services and
stakeholders committed to improving services for people with disability. When
transitioning clients from
facilities such as the Wilfred Lopes Centre to
community living options, adequate funding is required for the provision of
assessments and maintenance services to provide the requisite
interventions to ensure ongoing support and future risk management.
At a minimum, it should be a requirement that through-care plans be developed
for all patients on restriction orders. Such plans would
identify and support
treatment and rehabilitation services, including arrangements to enable the
person to transition into progressively
less restrictive environments and
eventually be reintegrated into the community.
Linkages to funding under the National Disability Insurance Scheme (NDIS) may
provide avenues to develop more flexible options for
forensic patients ready to
be conditionally released to live in the community.
10.3.109 These submissions highlight the practical barriers in the forensic
mental health system for individuals to progress from
a restriction order to a
supervision order. There are resourcing problems that affect the availability of
leave as well as the lack
of a ‘step-down’ facility in the
community. These issues at a crucial stage in the forensic mental health system
appear to undermine the intent of the
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas). The submissions suggest that the lack of a
legislated pathway ‘out’ suggest that transitional arrangements back
to community living are not a priority. It is the Institute’s view that
this gap in the system needs to be addressed to ensure
that it operates more
effectively. If this is to be achieved, additional resourcing will be required
in the short-term as will the
cooperation of stakeholders. The Institute
recommends that this deficit in the system be addressed as a priority by
government. It
is likely that such an approach will ultimately result in greater
community safety, better protection of human rights of people with
mental health
and cognitive impairments and an overall cost saving to the community.
10.3.110 No submission addressed the introduction of an extended leave
provision (as exists in Victoria). However, as noted, extended
leave is a key
feature of the transition pathway in Victoria, and the Institute’s view is
that this model should be introduced
into the transition process. This would
allow people to have an extended period living in the community to demonstrate
their progress
towards reintegration and rehabilitation.
10.3.111 Submissions did not address technological solutions that may be used
to deal with concerns about risk and community safety.
Legislation (yet to
commence) will make such technology available in New South Wales so that the
MHRT will be able to order its use
as a condition of leave or conditional
release. However, it is noted that there are concerns about the necessity for
electronic monitoring
as well as its negative impact on rehabilitation and human
This is an issue that
requires further consideration and could be addressed as part of a review of
transition arrangements in Tasmania.
Recommendations
50. The Tasmanian Government should initiate a review to identify the
barriers in the forensic mental health system to patients’
reintegration
into the community with a view to ensuring that appropriate pathways are
available for the gradual reintegration of
forensic patients into the community,
consistent with the principles of least restriction and community safety.
51. As part of the review of the transition pathways from a restriction order
to a supervision order, consideration should be given
to the amendment of the
leave provisions so that an order can be made allowing forensic patients to
leave the SMHU for an extended
52. As part of the review, the use of technological
solutions to overcome concerns in relation to risk and community safety should
be considered.
Conditional release orders
10.3.112 There is no statutory limit contained in the
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) for conditional release orders. This
means that a conditional release order (or some conditions of an order) may be
and there is no provision in the legislation to have the conditions
varied or removed.
inflexibility may potentially cause unfairness.
Position in other jurisdictions
10.3.113 Only New South Wales (for defendants where a finding of not guilty
was unable to be made following a special hearing), Western
Australia (for
defendants found not guilty by reason of insanity) and the Commonwealth make
provision for a conditional release order
to be made by the
A bond may be made for
up to five years in New South Wales, three years in the Commonwealth legislation
and 24 months in Western
Australia, the court can make a conditional release order for five years for
summary and minor indictable
Approach to reform
10.3.114 In relation to conditional release orders under the
Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(e), there appears
to be a deficiency in the legislation as it does not provide a limiting or
maximum term for the bond or provide
any mechanism that would allow a person to
apply to have the terms of the conditional release order varied or discharged.
a court wished, it could provide for a person to be conditionally
released into the community under the supervision of Forensic Mental
Services or
the Chief Forensic Psychiatrist, for example, or to take medication, comply with
medical treatment, notify of a change
of address, employment or training or
comply with directions and these could be imposed indefinitely and with no
option to have them
removed or changed. In contrast, the term of a treatment
order under the
Mental Health Act 2013
(Tas) is six months and the limit
for a conditional release order imposed by the court as a sentence, is five
Other indefinite
orders under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
provision for review and revocation.
10.3.115 Accordingly, the TLRI sought feedback on whether a limiting term
should be set for conditional release orders.
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
amended to provide for a limiting term for a conditional release order to
replace the current indefinite nature of these
10.3.116 No submissions received by the Institute addressed this issue.
However, the Institute’s view is there is a need for
reform. As noted, a
deficiency in the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(e) is that there is no mechanism that would allow a person to apply to
have the terms of the order varied or discharged (and no statutory
limit for the
term of the order limiting). Accordingly, the Institute’s view is that a
limit should be set. Based on the approach
in other jurisdictions, where the
period of the order is generally the same as the limit for good behaviour
bonds/conditional release
orders imposed as a sentence, the time limit for a
conditional release order under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas) should be equivalent to the period for a conditional undertaking
Sentencing Act 1997
, that is, five years.
Recommendation
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(e) should be amended to provide for a maximum time limit for a conditional
release order of five years.
The appropriateness of orders under the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) for people with intellectual
disabilities
10.3.117 The Tasmanian forensic mental health system has been criticised for
failing to provide a suitable framework for people with
intellectual
disability.
It has been
argued that people with intellectual disability ‘can sometimes be subject
to a restriction order largely as a result
of the failure to provide an
appropriate level of community support to prevent offending
behaviours’.
addition, a failure to provide appropriate services in the community may result
in people on supervision orders behaving ‘in
a manner that places both
themselves and others at substantial risk’ and being apprehended and
detained at the SMHU.
Further, there are problems in relation to patients with intellectual disability
being able to obtain discharge of orders because
‘the behavioural
manifestations of their intellectual disability ... are unlikely over time to be
seriously modified and thus
the level of risk to the community is unlikely to
substantially decline. It is only with appropriate levels of supervision that
risk of reoffending can be
mitigated’.
basis, the Forensic Mental Health Tribunal expressed concern ‘that
Tasmania’s secure mental health facility is
effectively being used to
“warehouse” people for whom the State is unable to provide
Case example
B was placed on a supervision order after being found unfit to stand trial
for a change of arson. He had a well-established history
of moderate
intellectual disability. He does not suffer from a major mental illness.
B worked in a full-time capacity and was a reliable worker. He maintained
stable accommodation in a shared flat over 15 months. He
was successful in
adhering to his supervision order for a period of approximately two years.
During this period, a 24 hour one-to-one
care package was in place.
According to reports received by the Tribunal, B only began to breach his
order when his support funding was pared back, resulting
in reduced supervision.
As a result, he absconded from his supervision program for five or so weeks
culminating in his apprehension
and detention at Wilfred Lopes Centre.
He remained in detention for a period of 16 months until an adequate support
package was made available.
10.3.118 Recently, amendments have been made to the
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) to provide enforcement provisions in
relation to conditional release orders made under
. In the second
reading speech, it was stated that:
Forensic orders are often not appropriate options for defendants who have an
intellectual disability. In such cases, release on conditions
may be appropriate
orders for the court to consider but the absence of a mechanism to enforce the
conditions imposed is problematic
and means judicial officers rarely use this
As noted, the TLRI’s examination of fitness to stand trial and insanity
cases between 2005 and June 2018, identified 12 cases
in the Supreme Court
involving individuals with intellectual disabilities with conditional release
orders being imposed in two cases,
restriction orders imposed in one case and
supervision orders being imposed in nine cases.
10.3.119 Similar concerns have been raised in other jurisdictions in relation
to the appropriateness of forensic orders for people
with cognitive impairments,
as have concerns that facilities and services designed primarily for people with
mental illnesses are
not appropriate. This latter issue was identified in South
Australia where the SASAC expressed the view that there needs to be a
secure facility for people with cognitive impairments, as well as
‘suitable purpose-built facilities or accommodation
for licensees with
cognitive impairments’.
The SASAC also considered measures that could be put in place in the absence of
a purpose-built facility and stated that it is ‘essential
that supported
accommodation tailored to the needs of individuals with cognitive impairments be
developed’.
Queensland, the Butler Report highlighted the inappropriateness of forensic
orders and detention in mental health units for people
with an intellectual
disability and pointed to the lack of appropriate facilities for such
10.3.120 Likewise, in Victoria, the VLRC highlighted disquiet about the
suitability of the system for people with intellectual or
other cognitive
impairment:
While arrangements for mental health treatment for people on supervision
orders are relatively well established, the same does not
exist for people with
an intellectual disability under the CMIA. Unlike people with a mental illness,
people with an intellectual
disability under the CMIA:
(a) rarely have their supervision order revoked
(b) lack a clear treatment pathway
(c) are not subject to clinical oversight by the Senior Practitioner
(d) lack secure accommodation facilities and accommodation options in the
In response, the VLRC made recommendations that would clarify supervision
responsibility for people with cognitive impairments and
mandate the preparation
of treatment plans.
10.3.121 The NSWLRC observed that ‘[a]n issue that has arisen
repeatedly ... is that the criminal justice and forensic systems
do not deal
effectively with people with cognitive
impairment’.
context of dispositional orders, these inadequacies are said to relate to the
very limited nature of services for people with
cognitive impairment in the
community, such as secure
accommodation.
stated that many of the issues were primarily operational rather than legal, and
accordingly recommended the development
of a working group to ‘develop an
action plan to deal with detention, care and community support of forensic
patients with
impairment’.
made recommendations to acknowledge cognitive impairment in the law including
renaming the relevant legislation the
Mental Health and Cognitive Impairment
(Forensic Provisions)
10.3.122 In light of these concerns, as part of its review, the Institute
sought feedback on the operation of the the current
Criminal Justice (Mental
Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) model for people with an intellectual disability
or cognitive impairment:
Are the orders available following a finding of not guilty by reason of
insanity or that a finding cannot be made that the defendant
was not guilty of
the offence charged under the current
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment)
(Tas) model appropriate for people with an intellectual disability
or cognitive impairment?
Are changes needed to the
Criminal Justice (Mental Impairment) Act
(Tas) in terms of the orders available and the process to vary or
discharge an order to better meet the needs of people with an intellectual
disability or cognitive impairment? What are the likely cost implications of
making these changes?
Are changes needed to the services that support
Criminal Justice
(Mental Impairment) Act 1999
(Tas) model to ensure that it meets the needs
of people with an intellectual disability or cognitive impairment? What are the
cost implications of making these changes?
10.3.123 The Institute received two submissions that addressed these
questions, both of which focused on the differences between mental
illness and
cognitive impairment and the need for services to address the needs of people
with a cognitive impairment.
10.3.124 The submission from QAI concentrated on the needs of people with
cognitive impairments and distinguished these needs from
those with mental
In Queensland, the majority of defendants whom the court has determined to be
unfit have some form of intellectual impairment, and
the majority of those found
‘unsound’ have some form of mental illness. It is important to
distinguish the two. The first
group may need habilitation and education,
particularly in relation to offending behaviour, but intellectual impairment is
illness. The second group are more likely to have a mental illness
requiring treatment.
Further, it was argued that the staff at the major Queensland facilities do
not have the skills to cope with the rehabilitation needs
of people with
cognitive impairments.
10.3.125 In the Tasmanian context, EOT also made observations about the
appropriateness of the services provided at Wilfred Lopes
for people with
cognitive impairment:
The Wilfred Lopes Centre provides specialist mental health treatment for
prisoners with mental health issues that require specialist
treatment, people
who are on remand who require inpatient specialist care, and people who are
found unfit to plead or not guilty
by reasons of insanity and are under a
restriction order.
At the same time, however, the Centre is also in exceptional circumstances
used as a place of detention for prisoners with cognitive
or intellectual
disability and in many cases patients on restriction orders have comorbid
diagnoses, including intellectual disability,
cognitive disorders, physical
disability, substance or alcohol abuse disorders, dementia and/or acquired brain
The fact that a person who does not have a mental illness or whose mental
illness is one factor among a range of diagnoses can be
placed on a restriction
order has several implications. Not only does this mean that treatment for
mental illness may be secondary
to other therapeutic needs, it also means that
other forensic orders (such as a supervision order which places the person under
responsibility of the Chief Forensic Psychiatrist or a treatment order which
makes provision for treatment as specified under the
Mental Health Act
(Tas)) may not be suitable or applicable.
The default position then becomes one where a person is at risk of being
detained indefinitely because of a permanent impairment.
There are no less
restrictive options.
This means that the pathways out of detention are more complex and may
require more flexibility during detention and in planning for
The underlying principle for those who are held unaccountable for their
behaviour due to a finding of unfitness to plead should be
appropriate treatment and care. In the case of those with intellectual
disability, cognitive impairment or other complex
needs this may require more
extensive treatment and more complex rehabilitation services. In turn this will
require better integrated
models of clinical and non-clinical service provision
and more effective services aimed at transition to living in the community,
including community-based accommodation suitable for people with high and
complex needs.
These factors raise the question about whether there is a need to provide a
greater range of alternatives when imposing restriction
orders to enable more
flexible options for compulsory treatment and custody for people with cognitive
impairment or other intellectual
disability.
10.3.126 It appears that there are problems in the current forensic system
for people who do not have a mental illness, but who have
an intellectual
disability or cognitive impairment. These problems are clearly outlined in the
submission of EOT, which stresses
the unsuitability of many aspects of the
current system for people with intellectually disability and cognitive
impairments. Rehabilitation
pathways should be developed for people with
intellectual disabilities or cognitive impairments. The Institute recommends
matter be addressed in the review of the ‘step-down’
pathways for forensic orders.
Recommendation
54. Rehabilitation pathways should be developed for people with intellectual
disabilities or cognitive impairments. The Institute
recommends that this matter
be addressed in the review of the ‘step-down’ pathways for forensic
Appendix 1:
Proposed reforms to unfitness to stand trial test
England and Wales
– Criminal Procedure (Lack of Capacity)
3. Capacity to participate effectively in a trial
(1) This section has effect for the purposes of section 1.
(2) A defendant is to be regarded as lacking the capacity to participate
effectively in a trial if the defendant’s relevant
abilities are not,
taken together, sufficient to enable the defendant to participate effectively in
the proceedings on the offence
or offences charged.
(3) In determining that question, the court must take into account the
assistance available to the defendant as regards the proceedings.
(4) The following are relevant abilities—
(a) an ability to understand the nature of the charge;
(b) an ability to understand the evidence adduced as evidence of the
commission of the offence;
(c) an ability to understand the trial process and the consequences of being
(d) an ability to give instructions to a legal representative;
(e) an ability to make a decision about whether to plead guilty or not
(f) an ability to make a decision about whether to give evidence;
(g) an ability to make other decisions that might need to be made by the
defendant in connection with the trial;
(h) an ability to follow the proceedings in court on the offence;
(i) an ability to give evidence;
(j) any other ability that appears to the court to be relevant in the
particular case.
(5) For the purposes of subsection (4)(e) to (g), an ability to make a
decision is to be regarded as consisting of—
(a) an ability to understand information relevant to the decision,
(b) an ability to retain that information,
(c) an ability to use and to weigh the information when making the decision,
(d) an ability to communicate the
New South Wales
Mental Health (Forensic
Provisions) Act 1990
(NSW) should be amended to include a statutory fitness
test, as follows:
A person is unfit to stand trial if the person cannot be afforded a fair
trial because it is established on the balance of probabilities
that the person
is unable to do any one or more of the following:
(a) understand the offence with which the person is charged
(b) understand generally the nature of the proceeding as an inquiry into
whether it has been proved that the person committed the
offence charged
(c) follow the course of proceedings and understand what is going on in a
general sense
(d) understand the substantial effect of any evidence that may be given
against the person
(e) understand the information relevant to the decisions that the person will
have to make before and during the trial, and use that
information as part of a
rational decision making process
(f) communicate effectively with, and understand advice given by, legal
representatives, and
(g) provide the person’s version of the facts to the court, if
Section 6(1)
Crimes (Mental
Impairment and Unfitness to be Tried) Act 1997
(Vic) should be amended to
provide that a person is unfit to stand trial for an offence if, because the
person’s mental processes
are disordered or impaired, the person is or, at
some time during the hearing, will be:
(a) unable to understand the nature of the charge
(b) unable to understand the actual significance of entering a plea to the
(c) unable to enter a plea to the charge
(d) unable to understand the nature of the hearing (that it is an inquiry as
to whether the person committed the offence)
(e) unable to follow the course of the hearing
(f) unable to understand the substantial effect of any evidence that may be
given in support of the prosecution
(g) unable to decide whether to give evidence in support of his or her
(h) unable to give evidence in support of his or her case, if he or she
wishes to do so, or
(i) unable to communicate meaningful instructions to his or her legal
practitioner.
Commonwealth
Crimes Act 1914
(Cth) should be
amended to provide that a person is unfit to stand trial if the person cannot be
supported to:
(a) understand the information relevant to the decisions that they will have
to make in the course of the proceedings;
(b) retain that information to the extent necessary to make decisions in the
course of the proceedings;
(c) use or weigh that information as part of the process of making decisions;
(d) communicate the decisions in some
Appendix 2:
Schedule 1 – Mental health service delivery principles
1. The mental health service delivery principles are as follows:
(a) to respect, observe and promote the inherent rights, liberty, dignity,
autonomy and self-respect of persons with mental illness;
(b) to interfere with or restrict the rights of persons with mental illness
in the least restrictive way and to the least extent consistent
protection of those persons, the protection of the public and the proper
delivery of the relevant service;
(c) to provide a service that is comprehensive, accessible, inclusive,
equitable and free from stigma;
(d) to be sensitive and responsive to individual needs (whether as to
culture, language, age, religion, gender or other factors);
(e) to emphasise and value promotion, prevention and early detection and
intervention;
(f) to seek to bring about the best therapeutic outcomes and promote patient
(g) to provide services that are consistent with patient treatment
(h) to recognise the difficulty, importance and value of the role played by
families, and support persons, of persons with mental
(i) to recognise, observe and promote the rights, welfare and safety of the
children and other dependants of persons with mental illness;
(j) to promote the ability of persons with mental illness to make their own
(k) to involve persons receiving services, and where appropriate their
families and support persons, in decision-making;
(l) to recognise families, and support persons, of persons with mental
illness as partners, with mental health service providers,
in the provision of
their treatment and care to the extent that this is appropriate and consistent
with their own wishes;
(m) to respect the wishes of persons receiving services, and the wishes of
their families and support persons, to the maximum extent
consistent with the
health and safety of those persons and the safety of others;
(n) to promote and enable persons with mental illness to live, work and
participate in their own community;
(o) to operate so as to raise community awareness and understanding of mental
illness and to foster community-wide respect for the
inherent rights, liberty,
dignity, autonomy and self-respect of persons with mental illness;
(p) to be accountable;
(q) to recognise and be responsive to national and international clinical,
technical and human rights trends, developments and advances.

Commonwealth

absolutely or subject to
